THE BED PAGE.
Arthur A. D. Bayldon writes re Tennyson :
Tennyson is to this century what Pope was. to
his —the skilled craftsman who, lacking the in-
spiration of his immediate predecessors, elabo-
rated his creations on a more artistio but less
convincing plane. But whilst Pope’s intellect
was keen and decisive and objectively alert and
consequently rarely illumined, delighting in the
swift play and parry of the epigram, Tennyson’s
was slow and broodingly subjective and conse-
quently often sleepily illuminated. But each was
the best craftsman of his time. Swinburne is a
sound-singer, a colour-painter, too prodigal of
riotous imagery to be a great craftsman who re-
cognises the power of limitation and the wealth
that lies in the miserliness of words. Neverthe-
less, even in Tennyson’s finest work one catches
the tap of the trowel. Sometimes ib is a word
that jars—the cold and artificial ‘ polished argent,’
for example, as applied to a woman’s bosom in
‘ A Dream of Fair Women.’ More often ib is a
line that lacks muscle—frequent in his blank
verse. Sometimes his limitation is too positive,
as though the presentation had been planned out.
In ‘Break, Break, Break,’ he reached his highest
plane of artistic pathos. It is brief, yet per-
manent ; its suggestiveness adds to its charm. An
indefinable something, a far-off recollection, a
wistfulness —too deep for tears—glides over our
spirits as we read it, such as we feel when the sun
sinks with banners furled and lances sheathed in
cloud.
Tennyson’s Intellect was artistic rather than
poetic, which implies that his sympathies were
limited. _ When I say ‘limited,’ I mean that,
despite his breadth of range, they issued from his
artistic faculty. He felt, and manfully endea-
vored to combat, this deficiency by choosing
themes _ eminently human. He sometimes su>
eeeded in touching a human, but always through
an artistic emotion. Then self-abandonment to
an impulse is wanting. That outburst of passion
or grief, constantly found in his inferiors, is ab-
sent. Moore, a mere pigmy in other comparisons,
volleyed more passion into those few simple
stanzas that begin ‘ Come, rest in my bosom, my
own stricken deer,’ than you can find in all Ten-
nyson. In ‘ Rizpab,’ a powerful thing, it is the
gruesome element, not the human, that is abiding.
I agree it is time to explode the idea that ‘ In
Memoriam ’ is intellectually a great poem, A
smattering of Spencer and Plato garnished with a
student’s every-day knowledge of the positive
sciences furnishes its intellectual basis. In ‘The
Ancient Seer ’ (that is the poem, I think ; I’ve not
a Tennyson at hand) you have ‘ First Principles ’
versified.
As a metaphysician, Matthew Arnold over-
shadows Tennyson. It has long been the prac-
tice to praise Tennyson as an artist at the •
expense of Browning. This is an injustice to.
Browning. He could manipulate words nearly as
skilfully as Tennyson when he so minded.
Take Tennyson at his best for sweetness :
Myriads of rivulets hurrying through the lawn;
The moan of doves in immemorial elms
And murmuring of innumerable bees.
What a quick ripple and long wave of sound!
Now compare it with this from Browning—
One loves a baby face, with violet* there,
Violets instead of laurel in the hair,
As those were all the little locks could bear.
Again, take Tennyson for rough force—
A full tide
Rose with ground-swell, which on the foremost rocks
Touching, upjetted in spirts of wild sea smoke
And scaled in sheets of wasteful foam, and fell
In vast sea cataracts . . . ever and anon
Dead claps of thunder from within the oliffs
Heard through the living roar.
Listen to Browning—
Here’s John the Smith’s rough-hammered head. Great eye,
Gross jaw, and griped lips do what granite can
To give you the crown-grasper. What a man I
In fetching home the sights and sounds of
nature, has Tennyson surpassed this from Brown-
ing—
That’s the wise thrush—he sings each song twioe over,
Lest you should think he never could recapture
The first fine careless rapture ?
And Browning is full of such passages I
But the truth is Browning was too robust, too
virile, too full of an original genius walking in
god-like freedom, to waste his hours in burnishing
the facets of a jewel, however priceless. He had
no time to strew his path with flowers of fancy :
subjects of paramount importance clamored for
expression. Sometimes he struck on a theme the
other handled. Compare the fighting force and
lyric rapture of ‘Prospioe’ with ‘Crossing the
Bar,’ and choose between them. Even to men
content to surrender all claims to rivalry, Tenny-
son is inferior in fundamental brain-stuff. For
real brain-sinew Rossetti’s second sonnet on ‘ The
Sun’s Shame’ is beyond him. The dramatic
thrill at the death of Stephen in ‘The White
Ship ’ he never gave us ; and that superb mono-
logue of angel lamentation, ‘The Blessed Damozel’
—despice its obvious blemishes—has a spiritual
expansion and firmness of handling above his
reach. Comp_are it with the ‘St. Agnes’ Eve’—
there is an affinity of mood them.
Philosophically, too, ‘ The Cloud Confines ’
quivers nearer the mark than ‘ln Memoriam’;
and note its condensation, hardly surpassed by
Shakespeare’s ‘ Lust ’-sonnet —the most brainy
bit of work in the language.
If there be anything in Landor’s dictum that a
great man puts into movement other great men,
then Tennyson is not a great man. Browning,
Arnold, and Swinburne don’t betray his influence ;
Rossetti and William Morris stand aloof from
him. As for the smaller fry : take them as they
spring to memory—Francis Thompson is a dis-
ciple of Crashaw; William Watson of Words-
worth, by the way of Arnold; Le Gallienne and
Davidson of Keats; Bridges, of the austere
Milton. Who else? Oh, Lewis Morris! Yes,
he writes weak Tennysonian verse interlarded
with old Biblical texts: a worthy disciple, for-
sooth 1 Had he not written ‘The Organ Grinder’
I should not have remembered him. The laureate-
Austin, if he be anything, is a nature-poet of
Wordsworth’s school.
Tennyson never surpassed his volumes of 1848.
His triple gifts are there : his skill as a craftsman;
his breadth of range; his presentation of the
scientific knowledge of his day. His most charac-
teristic work as a craftsman is ‘ The Lady of
Shalott,’ with its Leightonian beauty; ‘The
Lotos Eaters’—steeped in the glamor of ‘The
Eve of St. Agnes;’ ‘Ulysses’—his best piece of
blank verse as verse; ‘ A Dream of Fair Women ’
—marred by defective machinery; ‘ The Palace of
Art’ and ‘The Day Dream.’ The influence of
Southey and Theocritus (what a strange bringing
together, by the way) is obvious in his idylls.
As he grew older, his work became more Saxonian
and flowing, but less opulent; there was a marked
decrease of poetic touches; and his imagery
became commonplace and< scanty. On the other
hand, his mastery over the artificial resources of
hia art, over vowelation and the mouthing of syl-
lables, was more prominent.
Of Tennyson as a man I’ve not much to say.
Like most men of genius, he was egotistical,
selfish, and full of infantine whims; but, unlike
them, he had the knack of making money. No
second-hand clothes-dealer had a keener eye for a
bargain. He was the terror of publishers ; and
for this I forgive him much, as they are devils to
deal with. Like Carlyle and Macaulay, who
should have been flogged for their brutalities, he
had few gentlemanly instincts. He was wont to
shake his head at everything Browning published.
In fact, it is not recorded he was ready to recog-
nise the merits of others, unless, as in the case of
Watson, they pandered to his vanity. Compared
with Hood and Lamb, he is repellent. Those
kindly sympathetic souls are more precious to the
world, after all, than Tennyson with all his|ame.
I cannot retire from the subject without draw-
ing some attention to Tennyson's plagiarisms. He
plundered everywhere, bhakespeare’s ghost must
have quaked at his pilfers. With the most inso-
lent coolness he filched a line from poor Peele—
-4 You soarce could see the grass for flowers.’ And
times without number he most clumsily reset his
looted gems. Even obscure poets (some entitled
to admiration, by the way; Vaughan, for ex-
ample) were ruthlessly overhauled for material.
After all has been said against him, the main fact
remains that he cast into our song-treasury many
precious jewels for which we must be grateful";
remembering at the same time that no poet in his
lifetime was more amply recompensed.
: +
William Watson’s ‘The Unknown God’ had
the stimulus of rivalry with Kipling’s ‘Reces-
sional ’ —and it remains the best thing in W’s new
volume, ‘The Hope of the World and Other
Poems.’
Altogether W.W’s work might run to about half as
many words as ‘ Robbery Under Arms,’ say, yet it is com-
posed in no less than ten volumes, which cost altogether
something over £2. That seems to be the only way to
make poetry pay in England: to issue small volumes
at a high prioe in limited editions of 500 to 1000,
For the British public, which buys as few books as it can
help, seems to buy poetry least of all. And when you
do read of big verse sales, like Kipling’s and Ingelow’s,
it almost follows that a lower standard has been striven
for, or a weaker keynote struck.
A M.L. correspondent commented to The
Bulletin on the intellectual dignity of Watson’s
poem as compared with Kipling’s ; and no doubt
Watson’s advance is substantial. A line like
Have mercy on thy people, Lord 1
in limiting the truth, decisively limits the artistic
effect of verse for people with any sense of rele-
vancy. Win. Gay’s ‘Federation’ sonnet is simi-
larly spoiled by the prayer—
O may we rise united, penitent,
And be one people, mighty, serving Theel
What is there to be penitent about? We are
what we are—mere effects of causes ; and ‘ pen-
ance ’is nowadays a word without meaning. The
sensible watchword is Hope, not Regret; the
sensible use of dead selves is as Tennyson’s
stepping-stones to higher things. And, in any
case, what is a legendary Hebrew deity—or even
‘the Unknown God’ —doing in the Federal
galley ? We are obviously federating for
our own benefit for the service of Man;
and a poem which suppresses this essential
truth to make rhetorical use of catchwords
loses artistic force by removing its appeal
to the regions of chimera. And by the
way, Watson, though his poem contains some
dignified and impressive verses, is himself
most part beating the air; though doubtless his
words still have validity for many people. And
in the penultimate verse he trips in precisely
the pitfall into which—probably with deliberate
intent on audience and effect—Kipling has
jumped. For Watson knows no more than any-
body else what is the work of heaven ; and when
he prostitutes his ‘ Unknown God ’ to back his
personal polemios (regarding Greece and other
matters) his preliminary denunciation is levelled
against himself.
• * * *
As a whole, the ‘ Hope of the World ’ volume is
feeble ; and the paraphrase of Matthew Arnold’s
‘ Empedocles on Etna ’ in the title-poem is at
once too strong and too weak to be flattering.
Occasionally there is a phrase or verse to charm ;
but more than ever the author wants fire and
force.
* * » *
Alex. Montgomery’s novel, ‘ The Sword of a
Sin,’ deals effectively with the historic tragedy of
Prince Rudolph of Austria. The theme seems
well-calculated to hit a popular audience where it
reads; and Montgomery has handled it in the
terse, clean, polished manner of which he is so
excellent a master. The marked intellectual ele-
ment in the story is perhaps its chief attraction to
a thoughtful reader; and it will be interesting to
observe whether the unthoughtful reader finds
that the addition of brains flavours pleasantly or
otherwise his favorite romantic pabulum.
——
A brilliant little yarn by Morley Roberts glows
in Lond. ‘ Critic ’ of Feb. 12. Two men and their
wives travelled in Egypt. The women craved for
danger—excitement—romance. The men felt
themselves tame, civilised, contemned. One of
them arranged—for a lesson—an excursion, and a
mock capture by dervishes—
The shadows sprang at them and tore them from their
saddles.
‘The Dervishes 1 ’ said Yera, ‘Help! Fay, Fay!
Where are you ? ’
But the leader carried Fay into a tomb.
‘Suleiman, help 1 Oh, Antony, Antony 1 ’
And Tempest came whirling and drove into the men.
‘ What’s this ? ’ he cried.
And Lamont came, laughing 1 , ■
‘ It’s nothing but a joke I ’
‘A joke, you fool I’ cried Tempest. ‘Where’s your
wife ? ’
‘ I see her,’ cried Vera. ‘ She comes out of the tomb.
They took her there.'
She came, wide-eyed, dishevelled.
‘lt’s nothing, Fay. I did it for you with Suleiman!
It’s a play in the desert! ’
He laughed, and Fay laughed too.
‘You fool, you damned fool,’ cried Tempest. ‘God
help us! '
‘Stop laughing, Fay,’ cried Lairoht.
‘Fay, Fay,’ said Vera, ‘stop laughing.’
‘ For God’s sake, Fay.’
But she still laughed dreadfully.
And the man who had carried her into the tomb
laughed.

Writes 1 B.’
The list of Australian painters, and of men who have
painted in Australia, begins to grow long as it is meritori-
ous. One may recall particularly Westall, Oswald Brierly,
Skinner Prout, Conrad Martens, Nicholas Chevalier,
Abram Louis Buvelot, Von Guerard, Walton, Weigall,
Bunny, Folingsby, Piruenit, Longstaff, Arthur Streeton,
Johnstone, and Menpes, besides many admirable artists
whose reputation is, perhaps, more particularly local.
Westall is practically a olassic ; Oswald Brierly is a water-
color sea-scapist of world-wide reputation ; Skinner Prout
is almost unpurchasable ; and Conrad Martens’ work is
so prized that it rarely comes into the market. To the
worth of Chevalier and Weigall their English successes
amply attest. Buvelot’s ‘ Waterpool at Coleraine (Vic.)’ is
a landscape of genius. Many of Von Guerard’s pictures of
Australian scenery are now preserved in European gal-
leries. Walton was as spirited and sympathetic a painter
as almost any school could produce. Young men like
Bunnv, Condor, Fox and Menpes can show in any exhibi-
tion in the old world. Folingsby made an English name
long before he dreamt of visiting Australia. Robert
Atkinson’s work is treasured in England for its represen-
tations of South Sea life and scenery. John Gully is a
bold water-colorist of whom any country might be proud.
Longstaff is a great painter whose past work only faintly
shows what he has it in him to achieve. Piguenit has
some thirty or forty Australian and Tasmanian landscape's
which might, if collected, form as truthful and valuable
a panoramio gallery of the scenery of our quarter of the
globe as any conceivable. Johnstone’s powerful ‘ Off the
Track ’ and 1 A Mllabong ’ are creditable to the artist
and faithful presentments of our country. Streeton,
Mahony, Julian Ashton, Lister, and others, should have
little difficulty in holding their own against many Euro-
peans ; and an artist like Nerli should have no difficulty
at all.

Says ‘ Gladstone Advocate ’ of Feb. 15—
A Rockhampton friend sends along a ‘ dodger,’boldly
printed on paper of a gorgeous blue and headed ‘Swim-
ming Extraordinary,’ which tells how ‘Mr. Arthur A. D.
Bayldon, the well-known Sydney “ Bulletin ” Poet and
Writer,’ will give a public exhibition of the ‘Champion
Fancy Stroke of the World ’ at the Corporation Swim-
ming Baths on such-and-such a date,
And somebody forwards a notice of the actual
performance, from which—
Regardless of the fact that the swimming suit through
some leakage admitted the water, Mr. Bayldon deoided
to next exhibit his champion back-stroke. It is marvel-
lous in the extreme, and won for him a great uproar of
applause. He rolled into the bath with legs and arms
strapped, and then sinking within a foot of the bottom
darted the whole length of the bath at an amazing speed.
Mr. Bayldon is widely known in literary circles as a poet
and writer of great imaginative force, eto.
Talk about the sorrows of the local young man
who goes on the land ! What are they to the
griefs of him who goes on Literature ? To be a poet
—and have to ‘ dart the whole length of the bath
with legs and arms strapped’ for bread (or butter)
—what tremendous irony !
THE UNKNOWN GOD.
When, overarched by gorgeous night,
I wave my trivial self away ;
When all I was to all men’s sight
Shares the erasure of the day ;
Then do I cast my cumbering load,
Then do I gain a sense of God.
Not him that with fantastic boasts
A sombre people dreamed they knew j
The mere barbaric God of Hosts
That edged their sword and braced their thew :
A God they pitted ’gainst a swarm
Of neighbor Gods less vast of arm ;
A God like some imperious king,
Wroth, were his realm not duly awed {
A God for ever hearkening
Unto his self-commanded laud ;
A God for ever jealous grown
Of carven wood and graven stone ;
A God whose ghost, in arch and aisle,
Yet haunts his temple—and his tomb ;
But follows in a little while
Odin and Zeus to equal doom ;
A God of kindred seed and line ;
Man’s giant shadow, hailed divine.
O streaming worlds, O crowded sky,
O Life, and mine own soul’s abyss,
Myself am scarce so small that I
Should bow to Deity like this !
This my Begetter? This was what
Man in his violent youth begot.
The God I know of, I shall ne’er
Know, though he dwells exceeding nigh.
Raise thou the stone and find me there,
Cleave thou the wood and there am I.
Yea in my flesh his spirit doth flow,
Too near, too far,, for me to know.
Whate’er my deeds, I am not sure
That I can pleasure him or vex :
I that must use a speech so poor
It narrows the Supreme with sex.
Notes he the good or ill in man ?
To hope he cares is all I can.
I hope with fear. For did I trust
This vision granted me at birth,
The sire of heaven would seem less just
Than many a faulty son of earth.
And so he seems indeed 1 But then,
I trust it not, this bounded ken.
And dreaming much, I never dare
To dream that in my prisoned soul
The flutter of a trembling prayer
Can move the Mind that is the Whole.
Though kneeling nations watch and yearn,
Does the primordial purpose turn ?
Best by remembering God, say some,
We keep our high imperial lot.
Fortune, I fear, hath oftenest come
When we forgot—when we forgot 1
A lovelier faith their happier crown,
But history laughs and weeps it down S
Know they not well, how seven times seven
Wronging our mighty arms with rust,
We dared not do the work of heaven
Lest heaven should hurl us in the dust 9
The work of heaven 1 ’Tis waiting still
The sanction of the heavenly wilL
Unmeet to be profaned by praise
Is he whose coils the world enfold ;
The God on whom I ever gaze,
The God I never once behold :
Above the cloud, beneath the clod:
The Unknown God, the Unknown God.
William Watson.

Various Verses .
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Dear B.— Be the central idea of Wells’s
“ The Invisible Man,” as discovered by “ M‘G.,”
I guess it belongs to that prince of story-tellers,
Ambrose Bierce. Bierce is very fond of the in-
visible monster, and used it again and again long
before Wells appeared in the literary market —
witness his shuddersome story, “That Damned
Thing.” The story “ M‘G.” mentions, which I
have read somewhere, is, I imagine, a creation of
the author of “In the Midst of Life.” —8.
THE PATH THRO’ THE BRACKEN.
(SERENADE.)
There is a path thro’ the bracken, my Sweetheart:
The saintly Queen of Night is climbing high,
A warm December zephyr stirs the musk leaves—
Little Sweetheart, I am by.
Come, sweet Love, the dew is falling,
Hearken to the bronzewing calling
In the coppice nigh !
The path is silver-litten, and in silence ;
But lullabies of distant sounds fall there,
And faint harmonics of the singing river:
Come, dearest ! I am here
Waiting, where the scented grasses
Smile thro’ tears as Evening passes
Dewy, cool, and clear.
The path leads under our sentinels silent,
The rung dead gums with white arms held elate.
Down in the hollow, ’neath the brooding myrtles,
Little Sweetheart, I wait :
Hasten, ere the winds of Morning
Sigh their faintest far-off warning
At the Dawn’s red gate !
0 lover, listen ! ’Tis the sliprail rattling—
A little while, and light upon thy heart
Her silken hair like gossamer will tremble,
And her sweet lips will part,
Breathing soft her faith abiding,
Breathing low her fond confiding.
Lover, calm thy heart !
Tas.
BARKIS.
MOTHER DOORSTEP.
“ Wanted Kind Person to take charge of Baby Boy (or
Girl),” &e.— Any newspaper, any day.
“ Early this morning the body of an infant was found
on a doorstep in street,” &c. —Any newspaper, every
other day.
Unto the Person Kind there came
A young girl bearing her fruit of shame :
She fell—and It had to pay the price—
Innocent Lamb of Sacrifice !
Lovingly then the Person smiled,
Gazing upon the face of the child ;
Smiled like an ogress—“ Don’t despond !
I am of children all too fond.”
Then said the mother, speaking low,
Kissing the babe she had borne in woe:
“Treat him tenderly—nurse him well.”
Hotly the tears on the baby fell.
Taking the mother’s coin with a leer
Ogress remarked : “ Don’t cry, my dear,
Motherly persons to me are known,
One is named Wood and another Stone.
“ Either of them will your baby keep
Hushing him into a soft, long sleep,
Crooning a lonesome,lullaby song ;
They have been used to children long.”
Cold and yet kind was the nurse’s breast,
Cold fell the rain on the babe at rest ;
Pale was his face as an immortelle,
Old Mother Doorstep had nursed him well.
V.J.D.
DIVINE DREAMER.
Not he who shapes an idol from a clod,
And trusting, kneels to it, is greatest. No,
He who creates a world and then can go
And live within it, he is most like God !
VON KOTZE.
THE LAUGH OF THE WOMEN.
From the dawn of the world’s first morning, the
song of the men has been:
“Ours is the rule of the ages o’er the red field and
the green ;
Ours is the wider life of the clanging city and
mart.”
Low is the laugh of the woman hearing these
things in her heart.
Soul of the stronger passion, man of the wider
life,
If the broad sea rushed between you, you and
your new-made wife,
As you paced on the desert shore way by the grey
sea’s dusky swell,
Would the thrill of fatherhood reach you—the
throb of the pulse-strings tell ?
Your lips to our ears are singing of love-red mouth
and a kiss :
Is our soul a child’s or a babe’s, though we know
not the meaning of this ?
There are thrills of unnoted rapture in the heart
beneath your hand,
So passionate pure, so exalted that God can under-
stand.
What is the thing we worship, women the free
and the bond ?
Husband, emblem and symbol—we crave for the
word beyond.
We have made Man in our image, and bowed our
knees to a stone,
But you who own us and hold us, you we have
never known
From the East and the harem curtain, to the open
doors of the West,
Low is the laugh of the woman holding the child
to the breast;
Sweet the prayer of woman for the stranger who
lies at her side :
“Grant him our peace, speak him comfort, let
him be satisfied ! ”
M.L.

SATURDAY, APRIL 2, 1898.
An Optimist down South.
To say that because some crude, ill-digested pattern is
not accepted by a people as soon as it is offered, that
Federation will not be got at all, is opposed to reason and
experience. Federation being what we have described it,
a step in social evolution, we shall get it as certainly as
we have got manhood suffrage ; and for precisely the
same reason, because the growth of the social organism
will have made it necessary. To argue that if we do not
federate to-day we shall never federate, is to say that we
shall cease to advance along the line of our national
development; or to agree with Mr. Cook that we must re-
main politically stationary, or perhaps actually go back.
Australian Federation is just as certain as the next, inflow
of the tide, and this because it is the result of an organic
law in the social sphere. Rashness may deform the pat-
tern of our Federal Constitution, just as unwisdom may
attempt to thwart it; but neither the one nor the other
can avert our obvious destiny. What we have to guard
ourselves against is permitting our emotions to take the
place of our judgments. There has not been shown one
scrap of reason why we should take the Federal Bill of
1898, with all its manifold imperfections and dangers,
with the debts and railways unfederated, if, in a few
years, we can get a measure from which these imperfec-
tions will be eliminated. Just as the Bill of 1898 is more
acceptable than the Bill of 1891, so the Federal Bill of
1902 may be easily conceived of as an improvement on
he present measure. —Melbourne Age.
The Australian anti-Federal party, and the
semi-anti-Federal party, and the tired party
whose theory is “ Leterslide,” have spread
much literature abroad within the last few
weeks. The above slab is, in The Bulle-
tin’s opinion, the most interesting sample in
the lot. There is such a cheerful optimism
in it ; it reflects so beautifully the Aus-
tralian’s tired feeling, and his tendency to
hang the darned thing up for awhile, and
his easy confidence that he has all futurity
at his disposal and is immune from the acci-
dents which happen to other races. More-
over, the Age’s happy assurance that it really
doesn’t matter if Federation is postponed
till 1902, or some other date, contrasts so
beautifully with the same paper’s late im-
passioned yell that the big loan must be
floated immediately. The big loan is the
one subject in Australia regarding which
that tired feeling never predominates.
And it is such awful bunkum—this Federal
literature with the great Australian tired
feeling in it. Federation is undoubtedly ‘‘ a
step in social evolution,” but it doesn’t
follow, because it is so, that “ we shall get it
as certainly as we have got manhood
suffrage.” For that matter they haven’t yet
got pure manhood suffrage in Victoria —only
a modified form of property suffrage with a
certain amount of manhood suffrage mixed
with it. Also there are any number
of obvious steps in social evolution
which never eventuate—a community may
evolute in so many ways. So far, all
the evolution in Australia has been in the
direction of bursting up and disintegration,
and in North and Central Queensland the
spirit of disintegration is still in full blast.
It is a spirit that goes on by itself, in wet
weather and dry ; while the spirit of union
has been laboriously created, and is only
kept alive by careful nursing in which
respect it resembles almost every other spirit
that is worth preserving. “ To argue,” says
the Syme organ, ‘ ‘ that, if we don’t federate
to-day, we shall never federate, is to say that
we shall cease to advance along the line of
our national development.” It is a very
beautiful sentiment, with many long words
in it—but the one consistent line of national
development which Australia has developed
up to date has been in the direction of
further splitting apart into hostile States,
and the floating of new loans. To assume
that Federation will come of itself because it
is in the line of national development is
a pleasant, sanguine idea ; but, in reality,
the only real hope of Federation lies
in dragging the country off the old
bad lines of national development, about
which the less that is said the better.
For that matter, the thing has been too
chaotic to be called development, and it
hasn’t been national in any sense, but purely
provincial.
* * * *
The Federal scheme of 1898 is admittedly
better than the scheme of 1891. Therefore,
if the latter is rejected, the scheme of 1902
may be better, or it may be a great deal
worse, or there may never be any 1902
scheme at all. The doctrine of arithmetical
progression doesn’t apply in such a case.
The fact that a man is richer in ’9B than he
was in ’9l doesn’t prove that by 1902 he may
not be looking for crusts at midnight in the
dust-box. The fact that a man is stronger
in 1898 than he was in 1868 only proves that
by 1968 he will be dead. There is always a
point where improvement stops and retro-
gression sets in, and the Federal scheme of
1898 may be the nearest approach to an
ideal plan of union that these disjointed
provinces will ever evolve. Of course,
it is quite possible that four years
hence the provincial asperities will be so
much further smoothed down that the
provinces will be prepared to agree upon a
much more complete and workable scheme
of union than any yet constructed. But the
world is full of possibilities of the most
wildly unexpected character. The provinces
may not be on speaking terms by 1902. Be-
fore that date a Cornstalk army may be trying
to burn Melbourne, or a Victorian force may
be looting Sydney ; or Britain may have re-
sumed possession of the Northern Territory
and let it out to Pritchard Morgan’s Chow-
and-nigger syndicate, and the possibility of
making a real Australian Federation may be
postponed for a few centuries till after the
great Austral-Chow civil war is fought out.
The map is spotted all over with countries
which have gone to wreck because they
missed a chance and it never came again, and
whether this is or is not the chance that will
never come again no one knows. The pre-
carious art of weather-prophecy, which is the
only kind of prophecy left with us, throws
no light on the subject. But it may be the
very last opportunity that is coming our way
in the next dozen generations.
» • * *
The man who says that Australian Federa-
tion, even if the present opportunity is
neglected, is just as certain as the due inflow
of next tide, only escapes being the worst kind
of an ass, by virtue of the fact that even the
tide doesn’t always come back when it is due.
Only a few years ago a very large number
of respectable Dutch subjects went to sleep
as usual in Java with a full conviction that
they knew all about the next inflow of the
tide, and how far it would come in, and
when it would get there. But Krakatoa
upset the arrangements so completely that
multitudes of them were washed away, and
have never been accounted for from that day
to this. Also, by way of another case in
point, there is a locality in the far North
of Australia where the tide arranges things
very much as it pleases, and the man
who thinks he knows all about the next
inflow is often very badly left. It is
no doubt cheering to reflect that the pre-
sent chance of Federation can’t be j the
last one unless the awful possibility fore-
shadowed by Mr. Cook comes to pass, and
we begin to retrograde. And it is also a
very solemn thing to remember that half the
nations of the world at some time or other
lost their opportunity and began to retro-
grade, and have been retrograding ever
since. No one can tell for certain at the
time which is the lost opportunity that will
prove the turning-point ; there is no huge
label hung upon it to distinguish it from any
other opportunity ; and the lost opportunity
of Australia in 1898 may or may not be the
one—it is all a gamble.
* * * *
There is much comfort in the news that
whatever chances of making an Australian
nation the present generation of Australians
may fool away, still it is our “ obvious
destiny ” to be a nation, and the destiny
will work itself out, anyhow. There would
be a lot more comfort if it were not that so
many equally obvious destinies have gone
broke, or been lost or mislaid, and have
utterly failed to come to pass. A destiny is
just as likely to go in the wrong direction as
a horse if it is steered badly ; and, in any
case, no one knows exactly what is a destiny.
In theory it is a kind of hazy culmination, or
apex, or pinnacle that lies in front of us, or
else behind us ; and all that can be said
about it with certainty is that the destiny
of any community will only be fully recog-
nised after it is dead. Until then it is never
sufficiently obvious to be quite reliable,
and, as a rule, a nation gets just as much
destiny as it carves out for itself. And, as
regards the present case, this much and no
more is known with certainty : Australia has
a chance now to federate on reasonably
equitable conditions. If it loses this chance
it may get a better one, or a worse one, or
none at all, later on. The bone is obvious
enough ; as to the shadow, it isn’t even quite
certain that there is a shadow. The Bulle-
tin votes for the bone, anyhow.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
“Tattersall and his Critics.”
Apropos of a variety of circumstances and in
reply to various correspondents and inter-
viewers, The Bulletin wishes to very dis-
tinctly state what has already been clearly
embodied in a recent article on “ Tattersall
and his Critics”—viz., that this paper had
and has no intention or desire to injuri-
ously reflect on the good name of “ Tat-
tersall.” The Bulletin has every confidence
in “ Tattersall’s ” good intentions—it has often
put money into his sweeps, and it is ready to
do so again. The sole object of the above-men-
tioned article was to show how, in its opinion,
“ Tattersall’s ” sweeps could be placed above
criticism, and how the said good inten-
tions, which appear at present to miss
fire, might be carried into effect. In
short, The Bulletin’s position with re-
spect to “Tattersall” is just this: It believes
“ Tattersall ” to be absolutely honest, but it
regards certain details of his system as cal-
culated to cast quite unnecessary suspicion
on his bona-fides. “ Tattersall’s ” sweeps
are gambles, and The Bulletin asks
“ Tattersall ” to strictly conform to the
etiquette of gambling, by letting his clients
play, as it were, with an ostentatiously new
pack of cards every time. It is not enough for
an enormous concern like “ Tatts.’” sweep to
be honest—it must look honest and scrupulously
business-like right down to the minutest parti-
cular. There can be no “ sentiment ” in gamb-
ling for money.
4.
The Kalgoorlie Riot.
The Forrest Government’s new regulation
prohibiting alluvial miners from digging below
10ft. produced unexpected results at Kal-
goorlie (W.A.) a few days ago. Premier For-
rest had gone there to lay a foundation-stone,
and the alluvial crowd wiped up the town with
him- and made him retire with great loss of
dignity. It was, undoubtedly, a rude and ob-
jectionable outrage, yet it was neither so brutal
nor so cowardly nor so indefensible as the daily
papers have represented it. The Forrest
Government first of all gave to the miners the
right to dig for alluvial at any depth, and mul-
titudes of them depended on the work for a
livelihood. Then it took away the right—-not
by any parliamentary process, but merely on
the say-so of the boss-firm of Forrest and Co.
—and its new regulation deprived numbers of
the miners of their sole means of support. This
was a worse outrage than the one which the
miners committed when they wiped up the
dusty town of Kalgoorlie with Forrest—
an outrage is most cowardly when the attack-
ing party lacks provocation and has such odds
on its side that the one attacked has no earthly
show, and when the Forrest Government, in
the calm seclusion of its office, where no miner
could get at it, deprived hundreds of men of
their livelihood by the safe process of writing
something on a sheet of paper, it took fewer
risks and gave its victims far less show than
the Kalgoorlie mob gave to Forrest. In
attacking him they at least faced armed police;
when the Forrest Government attacked them
it did it by a perfectly safe process, and faced
nothing more*deadly than some ink and a
wafer. There is little redress in Parliament
for the miners, because the franchise is so
arranged that the large population of the gold-
fields has less representation than one or two
men - and a few sheep in the pocket-burghs
of the Ministry. There is no redress at
law, for the ancient fossils who are returned
by the two-men-and-a-sheep vote of the old
pocket-burghs make the laws. When there is
no justice and no fair hearing to be had in any
lawful way, the unregenerate tendency to wipe
up the town with somebody breaks out. If the
act of the miners was a cruel and cowardly out-
rage, the excuse for it is that it was provoked
by an outrage which was probably quite
as cruel and which was still more cowardly.
A great deal has been said about the “ blot
upon the law - abiding character of Aus-
tralia,” but the laws that a community is sup-
posed to abide by are laws made by a Parlia-
ment representative of the people—not the
irresponsible ukase of a Minister who only
holds his position because the Parliament does
not, in any sense, represent the people. And
the utter lawlessness of the Forrest Govern-
ment’s action in abolishing a legal right on its
own authority, was a vastly bigger blot, any-
how.
Nationhood v. Shoddy.
Last week Sydney Telegraph declared bitter
war against the Convention’s scheme of Federa-
tion, and openly avowed that it did so in the
interests of Foreign-trade and its particular
friends—the firms whose German names of
Schmidt and Schneider and Schroder hang
out in long rows down by York-street, Sydney.
Its article of March 23 was one long shriek
to the people of N.S.W. to throw overboard
the prospects of nationhood in the future, so
that they may have their reward in the present
by getting lots of foreign-made boots landed at
Circular Quay, while they borrow the money to
go sand-shifting with. A few samples from
that famous howl are as follows :
The Bill has been so framed that they would have to
begin by forcing’ this colony to accept a fiscal policy which
is utterly obnoxious to it. The financial provisions are
such that an immense load of customs duties would at
once have to be shouldered by the people. We would be
compelled to raise a million a year or more of customs and
excise taxation, for which the colony would not have any
use. The money would be taken out of the pockets of our
people by the Federal Government, and, in the form of a
“ surplus,” handed over to the State Government. There
being no legitimate use to which this surplus taxation
could be applied, however, and it being impossible to give
it back to its rightful owners, illegitimate purposes might
very soon be expected to suggest themselves. It would,
therefore, in the end, amount to every man, woman, and
child in the colony compulsorily paying from a pound to
thirty shillings a year to a political-extravagance fund.
And the Federal Government, being the tax-gatherer, the
elector could only make his protest to a Legislature in
which it took twenty New South Wales votes to count for
one Tasmanian or West Australian vote. But, even
were the Federal Government willing to grant redress,
it could not do so. The Bill proposes to strap this
unnecessary load of customs taxation on to the back
of New South Wales so securely that it can only
be detached by an amendment of the Constitution.
Leaving aside the fact that an inter-State Commission un-
der this unrepresentative Government would interfere in
the management of our railways, for the commercial suc-
cess of which we would still retain all responsibility, it is
enough for the electors to ask themselves are they pre-
pared to go into a partnership of which a condition prece-
dent is the absolute and irrevocable surrender of Free-
trade ? About the answer to that surely there can be no
hesitation.
For utterly shameless misrepresentation this
should be a record. In the first place, the
Constitution doesn’t commit the Common-
wealth to Protection or Freetrade —it leaves
the question absolutely open. All it specifies
is that, if the Federal Parliament decides to
raise revenue by customs and excise, not more
than one-fourth thereof shall be applied to the
support of the Federal Government, while the
other three-fourths shall be returned to the
States. But the Federal Government may, if it
pleases, raise all the money it requires, or
any proportion it pleases, by direct taxation
and utterly abolish customs and excise. It is
merely agreed that, if the total customs and
excise revenue amounts to 6d. or £6,00U,000
or eighteenpence per annum, three-fourths
of it must be returned to the States.
Also, the Tasmanian and Westralian voter
equals about nine (not 20) N.S.W. voters in the
Upper House only ; in the Lower House the
numbers go practically according to population,
so that N.S.W. will have far the largest voting
power. And it is the Lower House which will
draft the tariff, and until the Lower House
passes it the Senate, where the small provinces
have more than their due share of population,
doesn’t come in anywhere. But probably the
wildest Ananias of the whole collection is
the statement that, if a Protective tariff is
adopted and N.S.VV. gets back £1,000,000
per annum more customs revenue than it now
receives, “ there is no legitimate use” to which
it can be put. N.S.W. has now an annual
shortage of some £900,000 or more. About
£BOO,OOO of this is disguised by paying all
manner of daily expenses out of loans, and the
other £lOO,OOO or more is the deficit which
Premier Reid says isn’t there. The legitimate
use which N.S.W. has for another million a
year is to make its finances decently honest
instead of the lie and swindle and fake they
now are. And if that isn’t a legitimate use-
well, what is it ?
Another Loan.
The news that Premier Skddon, of Maoriland,
is in favor of floating a £2,000,000 loan for rail-
way-building purposes evidently means that
any small remains of the self-reliant policy
which Ballance inaugurated are to be thrown
up. The news comes very closely after the
luckless Wellington election; it probably
means, therefore, that Digger Dick, recogiiising
that his luck is turning, proposes to try his
hand at a boom policy on the old Vogel lines
by way of strengthening his position. Seddon
and Turner are the only existing Premiers who
have kept up even a pretence at honest finance
—the other five have been professional mendi-
cants at Bull’s back-door all the days of their
lives —and apparently Seddon andTuRNER have
resolved to abandon the game simultaneously.
They didn’t keep it going very long in any
case. The average, easy-going Australian,
with his habit of hanging on to the apron of
mother-BuLL, and his poor little brag
about how this is the richest country
on earth, and his poor little wail about
the impossibility of doing anything un-
less a much poorer country will lend
him the money to do it with, and his
lament about his inability to stand taxation,
and his cant phrase about “ developing the
great resources of the country,” is a pretty
sick spectacle. There are a good many reasons
why Maoriland, at all events, should have
sworn off borrowing for good. In the early
days it was the most awful plunger of the
Seven Provinces. It has a debt of millions
—equal to over £6O 13s. per inhabitant. For
24 millions of this—equal to about £33 per
head—there are no reproductive assets to show;
it was borrowed in the old, awful days because
it was easier to meet current expenses out of
loans than to pay taxes. The British public
debt is equal to about £l5 per head; there-
fore Maoriland, in the course of 45 years,
despite the fact that it had, and still has, a
la'rge land revenue to help out its public in-
come,' has piled up about 60 per cent, more
debt than Britain, with no land-revenue to fall
back upon, did in two centuries. Because of that
vast load of unproductive debt Maoriland is the
most heavily-taxed province in Australasia, and
the accumulation of more debt only means that
it will have to be taxed still more heavily. It
has become remarkably prosperous since it, to a
great extent, abandoned the loan policy ; its
population is increasing rapidly ; its industries
are booming, under the influence of a strongly
Protectionist policy, more than those of any
other province in the seven ; it has a handsome
surplus every year ; aud it has partially cleared


away the wreck which the ending of the last
loan-boom brought upon its financial institu-
tions. All of these are reasons why,
having got out of the mire, it shouldn t make
haste to go back there. Maoriland, with its
big annual surplus, and with sufficient wealth
to raise even more revenue than it now does, is
better fitted than any other province to dis-
pense with loans and construct its public
works out of revenue. Anyhow, the question
which all the Australias have to face is this :
If they can’t afford now to set aside their land
revenues for public works purposes and meet
their ordinary expenditure out of taxation as
many poorer countries do, how will they be
later on when their land - revenues are
either greatly diminished or exhausted, when
the annual bill for interest is far larger than it
is now, when their credit is used up, as it must
be some day, and when the inevitable expen-
sive war-trouble, which comes to every country
soon or late, arrives at their doors { When
that time comes, this community will probably
strike the great idea of digging up its present
seven Premiers and hanging their remains on
seven tall trees as a token of humble but sincere
execration.
Quacks versus Doctors.
The Bulletin often receives letters in this
strain :
Dear Bulletin.— Re quackery, is there so much differ-
ence after all between the authorised pretender to special
knowledge and the unlicensed? A quack is an empiric.
But empiricism is founded upon experience, and half of
the pharmacopea is built up of its conclusions. The in-
telligent empiric observes, experiments, and is content
with results. He may be wholly ignorant of pathologic
science ; but he has found that, by the application of a
certain agent, under certain conditions of ailment, a
beneficial result is attained. There he rests. And for all
practical purpose of usefulness, as a leech, he is as good
as the man who gives a learned reason for every dose
he administers. The latter, whose claims are founded
upon his special education, is so tor handicapped that his
■ observations of disease and the action of remedial
agents are necessarily made through the spec-
tacles of his “ school.” And just as a great lawyer
falls into the error that the interpretation of the law (as
■established by precedents) must be the source of justice,
so the average practitioner of the schools concludes that
his text-books hold the truth. But as the practice of
medicine is but a theorem —a supposititious principle of
truth —and as there are several schools, all more or less
■divergent in their teachings, how can we assume that the
man who writes M.D. to his name is any more infallible
than he whom we deem quack? May not a conscientious
:and intelligent quack, free from the shackles of tradition,
learn as much from observation of cause and effect
as the mere schoolman, even giving the latter the
advantage of hospital practioe ? We know that it is so,
and that hundreds of physicians have quietly appropriated
■discoveries made by laymen who have worked upon the
■eclectic principle.— Old Jack.
Here is much confusion of ideas. The person
■who deals with an ailment according to per-
sonal experience of the effects of any particular
treatment, is—since he has to that extent de-
duced from observed phenomena a general rule
—by just so much the less an empiric. The
principles of formulated therapeutics, how-
ever, are founded upon vastly more compre-
hensive generalisations deduced from the in-
•conceivably wider induction of instances pre-
sented by the whole experience of mankind
including, most probably, not only the dis-
coveries of “old wives” and “herb-doctors,”
but even the very knowledge which the quasi-
■quack claims to have arrived at by personal
■observation ; whereas the empiric—even when
be isn’t a blind experimenter, pure and simple
—is but proceeding upon a few isolated
constituents of the vast corpus of accumulated
knowledge which is foolishly sneered at as that
■of the “schools.” The Bulletin has before
mow admitted that, while a non-legally-qualified
person may be a born healer, a duly-belettered
practitioner may be a dangerous blunderer, but
the chances—as in every other calling—are so
immensely in favor of the man who has learnt
his business that the person who would sooner
trust to an “eclectic” experimenter on his
“ own ” than to a student of the “ text-books ”
which record the physiological and pathological
observations of all times and all countries,
should, upon a very moderate amount of reflec-
tion, be prepared to write himself down an ass
—with a very large “A.”
Jerry-built Tradesmen.
Melb. Trades’ Hall begins to wonder whether
the Working-men’s College is all that it has
been cracked up to be as a technical school ;
whether, in fact, it is a technical school at all.
At a late meeting of the Trades’ Hall Council
a good deal of rhetoric was expended upon the
merits of the institution, and the great benefits
conferred by it upon the working-man. The
coopers’ representative said all this, or some-
thing like it, and others said some more. But
then the under-side of the case was turned up
by a house-painter member, who remarked
that the coopers’ opinion would be considerably
modified if cooperage were taught at the school
as painting is. That touches the spot. Bor
the best part of a score of years the virtues of
“technical education” have been continually
trumpeted, until the term has become a sort of
sacred thing. Instead of making better crafts-
men of apprentices, Melbourne’s alleged tech-
nical college has turned out hosts of young
men knowing just enough of a trade to take
the place of skilled artisans in an emergency.
So long as the jerry-builder was abroad in the
land the jerry-built tradesman was good enough
for his job. But the occupation of the jerry-
builder is for the present gone ; he is a house-
agent now, or on the sewage works, or he has
gone West. The young jerry-built tradesman’s
stomach, however, has just as bad a habit of
telegraphing for provisions as the full-blown
tradesman’s has ; and as booms and droughts
and other things have left the community with
not too much meat in its cupboard, the jerry-
built tradesman, knowing his own weakness,
offers to work for bread without meat. Almost
every daily paper issue has some such
advertisements as this: “Carpenter young,
plumbing, papering, useful, 155., no tools.” No
wonder, then, that when the college applies to
the trades for more support, the men who have
had the pinch feel like replying that “ those
who have been pushed out by these students
can’t be expected to pay the piper for the manu-
facture of more of the same sort of competition.
It is a boom system pure and simple—a relic of
the time when pretty well everybody was
making a short cut —this man to fortune through
a “ bank,” that one, less ambitious, to a trade
through a working men’s college. The “ banks ”
have been either wiped out or reconstructed ;
but the college remains in statu quo chiefly be-
cause the sufferers have been working men who
haven’t known what was the matter with them.
But light is being let in on the subject now;
and the trades are beginning to demand a
technical college which will not devote itself to
the manufacture in red-hot haste of shoddy
craftsmen who are only worth 15s. a week
themselves, and whose inefficiency helps to drag
other men’s wages down to the 15s. level also.
That there has been trouble between authori-
ties and prisoners at Darlinghurst Gaol is cer-
tain, whether or not it rose to not. And the
Rev. B. Carey, in spite of Minister Gould s
denial, appears convinced that he was told to
“ mind his own business ” when he pointed out
how young prisoners and first offenders weie
brutalised in the gaol by enforced association
with hardened criminals, and how prisoners
were ill-treated by brutal warders. The public
little knows what contamination of infamy,
what infamy of outrage, is possible behind the
four walls of a prison. In his new ‘ Ballad
of Reading Gaol ” Oscar Wilde throws ghastly
light on prison methods. He describes the
strangling of a fellow-convict
They hanged him as a beast is hanged :
They did not even toll
A requiem that might have brought
Rest to his startled soul.
But hurriedly they took him out,
And hid him in a hole.
The warders stripped him of his clothes ;
And gave him to the flies :
They mocked the swollen purple throat,
And the stark and staring eyes :
And with laughter loud they heaped the shroud
In which the convict lies.
And of the whole wretched penal business as
administered by men without hearts at the in-
stigation of men without brains
The vilest deeds, like poison weeds,
Bloom well in prison air ;
It is only what is good in Man
That wastes and withers there ;
Pale Anguish keeps the heavy gate,
And the Warder is Despair.
For they starve the little frightened child
Till it weeps both night and day : ,
And they scourge the weak, and flog the fool,
And gibe the old and grey ;
And some grow mad, and all grow bad,
And none a word may say.
And those scourging stanzas, if all were known,
are as true in Australia as anywhere. bays
Michael Davitt, an acknowledged authority
on prison treatment, in his new book of Aus-
tralian impressions :
Dark cells are still a strong feature in Darlinghurst
discipline. They appeared to me, on inspection, to be in
pretty frequent requisition. Seven days in one of these
cells would be calculated to drive an ordinary health} per-
son half insane. It can, therefore, easily be imagined
what the effect of repeated punishments of this homble
kind will have upon bodily and men tally-weakened
criminals. This barbarity has been abandoned alto
gether in Maoriland and Tasmania, and somewhat
modified i n all the other provinces, except New So uth bates.
It is a form of punishment which never did, and never
could, produce any good of any. kind upon any human
Being in its application. It might terronse for the
moment but the after-effects on the minds of criminals
mentally debilitating as these were bound to be, would
work more in the way of fixing vicious habits by lessening
the strength of resistance than in frightening evil-doers
out of their indulgence. A prison discipline, no matter
how severe, which cannot be upheld without the daik cell
and the “ cat," is more a proof of the incapacity of its ad-
ministrators than of any dogged insubordination m cer-
tain classes of criminals.
And of the treatment which the Rev. Carey
denounces as brutalising young prisoners,
Michael Davitt says “it is almost as savage
as the nature it is intended to cure. in a
word, the callous cruelty of N.S.W. gaol
officials is making criminals, not curing tnein.
Let N.S.W. Parliament mark it!
Item from Eng. Judge Lawrence s sum-up in
the aristocratic Nevill swindle-case (sentence,
five years’ penal servitude) :
Tf this had been the case of some wretched city clerk,
with'a famny of seven children who had -ffered some
great loss, and in his straits had helped himself from his
master’s till, I am afraid there would be nobody to speak
for him. It is said that in your position any punishment
p issed upon you would be felt more keenly than by one
hi a humbler position. I wish to point out to you, and to
others, that the higher the position the greater the re-
sponsibility. It is said, and this comes most close to my
heart that what you have done will bring sorrow on
those connected with you. In the case of the clerk whom
I mentioned, the blow falls with double severity on his
wife and children.
Tliis is very different soup to that ladled out
from the Australian bench, which habitually
lets the social-position offender down lightly on
the ground that the mere appearance in court
is a keen punishment to his refined nature, and
so on. But Australia can learn a good deal
from England in matters connected with the
dispensation of justice, with public honor, and
with private honesty. English insolvency-
judge Vaughan Williams, for example,
wouldn’t have whitewashed the Melb. boomers
with one fink in the bucket, while their
carriages waited at the door of the court 01
just round the corner. And just attempt to
fancy a man like Meagher attempting to enter
the House of Commons after the verdict of the
jury in the Dean conspiracy case, and the
scathing commentary of Chief Justice Darley
when he was evicted from the legal profession
—to say nothing of his (Meagher’s) own
admissions on the floor of the Leg. Assembly !
Also, had Sufeield been so scandalously mis-
tried in England he would have been released
long ago. John Bull publicly aims at
standards a lot higher than prevail out here
and often reaches them.
The Premiers’ Conference the other day re-
solved, in respect of colored-races immigration,
that every province should pass an Act on
the lines of the Natal Act—and vigilantly
enforce it. Premier Nelson, of Kanakaland,
stood out, of course. But, if the other pro-
vinces keep to the agreement, a very effective
bar against the cheap and nasty alien will be
set up. Natal has been suffering from the
plague of Indians even more than N.S. Wales ;
and last year a series of four Acts were passed
which were received with great indignation by
the Bengali and his British exploiter.
The first (says an authority) is a quarantine law of ex-
ceptional severity, obviously directed not against con-
tagious diseases, but against immigration. The second
Act, “ to place restrictions on immigrants, practically
prohibits Indian immigration by compelling the applica-
tion for admission to be written and signed in one ot the
languages of Europe. Without such authorisation an
immigrant can receive a license to carry on any trade or
calling only by incurring exceedingly heavy penalties,
which are further imposed on any master or owner of a
ship which may bring prohibited immigrants to the
colony. The third Act, “ to amend the law relating to
licenses to wholesale or retail dealers,’ places the tight
of granting or refusing licenses to trade in the hands or
an officer appointed by the Town Councils or Boards,
representing the very classes most opposed to Indian com-
petition. Nor is permission allowed to appeal against his
decision in any court of law. It further provides that no
license shall be issued to any person who does not keep his
books of account in the English language. This provision
shuts the door on all the large Indian traders and mer-
chants who are unacquainted with English, while it is
equally fatal to the smaller pedlars and hawkers who are
unaccustomed to use any account-books at all. Ihe fourth
Act “deals with the question of passes, ana subjects
any Indian found without a pass in his possession to
arrest and imprisonment without any ri<rht of appeal or
compensation for wrongful arrest and detention.
“Un-English and ungenerous” John Bull
calls these measures ; but, as the small white
community of Natal is practically fighting for
its life, John Bull’s indignation (on behalf of
his injured pocket) has been cheerfully dis-
regarded. And there is urgent need for all-
Australia to follow Natal s example.

Dear Bulletin. —Someone with lordly ideas
sneers at the alleged £lOOO limit, to N.S.W.
civil-servants’ salaries. A few comparisons ;
The English service is among the best-remune-
rated in the world, yet the majority of depart-
mental heads only receive from £9OO to JMoOO
a year. [7 here, a man who behaves himself is
sure of his pay and pension. Ed. B.] In veiy
special cases, like those of pennanent undei-
secretaries, with a large hand in the control of
international affairs, as much as £2OOO, or even
£2500, is given. Even W. H. Preece, chief of
the British electricians, only draws £lOOO
salary. At least a dozen men in N.S.W. Govt,
employ (Judges excluded), with merely local
reputations, draw upwards of £looo—yet the
editor of a “ great ” Australian daily has his
services assessed at about that sum ; subordi-
nate scribes do not average beyond £6 a week ;
most lawyers are glad to clear £300 a year, and
the general run of medicoes, despite their
costly apprenticeship, cannot have much more
than that amount left after they have paid for
equipages and the conventional extravagances
of their profession.—T.D. [Probably the above-
mentioned British economy is the reason why a
common London “ beak ” gets £1500 a year for
fining the local drunk. — Ed. B.j
S.M. Herald, Melb. Argus, and other gloomy
Tory organs which continually parrot the cry
that M.L. Labor Govt, is “ driving population
from the country ” are invited to took at
N.S.W. Statistician’s just-published figures of
population-growth in 1897. The N.S.W.
excess of births over deaths was 1 - 75 per cent. ;
the excess of Victoria 1 '38 per cent.; and of radi-
cal Maoriland 1'68 per cent. And the excess of
arrivals over departures in M.L. was 0 - 41 per
cent., and in N.S.W. only 024 per cent., while
Victoria shows an excess of departures over
arrivals of 1'27 per cent. —so that wickedly-
misgoverned M.L. is attracting more popula-
tion than N.S.W., and very much more than
Vic. The figures of nett all-round increase of
population (births and immigration) in 1897
run —W.A., 17*37 per cent. ; Tas., 3’37 ; Q.,
2’65 ; M.L. (excluding Maoris), 2‘09 ; N.S. W.,
1-99 ; S.A., 0-78 ; Vic., 001. Both W.A. and
Tas. increases are due chiefly to an exceptional
influx of miners, so that M.L. stands second on
the list of the normal-increase bunch —with
N.S.W., S.A. and Vic. at varying distances
below her. And Editor Gullett and Editor
Willoughby can make a note of it.

Van Dobson (Convention edition) is a curious
instance of Conservative consistency. In dis-
cussing the appellate jurisdiction of the pro-
posed High Court, he argued for trusting the
Federal Parliament, and claims that:
The power which is given to the Federal Parliament
will be exercised with ordinal common-sense, and with a
due regard to justice.
On the Control of Rivers, he would also trust
the Federal Parliament; but, on the bookkeep-
ing proposals, he says :
I am not prepared to trust the Federal Parliament on a
question of that sort.
Mem. : Tasmania is not particularly interested
in Control of Rivers questions ; but, in finance,
she is a Dobson of quite another color. Again
(Control of Rivers debate) :
I do not think it my duty to frame a Constitution that
people will accept. ... I shall be satisfied if I know that

I am not going to bother myself about whether they will
or will not accept it.
Yet, on financial clauses, he somersaults with :
If there is a guarantee in the Bill that the State finances
will never be less than in the year before that in which
they enter into Federation, tlle Constitution will be moi d
readily accepted by the people.
And this, ye gods ! this a nation-builder ! ! ! !
We hear a deal about the kind of persons sum-
moned to serve on coroners’ juries, and their
qualifications—or disqualifications—for the
work. The country has to pay thousands a
year for the services and opinions of these juries,
and what are they worth? Absolutely nothing.
Why not abolish them entirely ? They are in-
deed an ancient institution ; an interesting relic
of the past; but quite useless to-day. If a
coroner’s jury brings in a verdict of murder or
manslaughter against a suspect, the coroner
commits him for trial—then the Attorney-
General may, or may not, file a bill against
him ; if the jury finds there is no case against
him, the same Attorney-General may still file
his indictment. If the jury finds manslaughter,
the Attorney-General may nevertheless proceed
for murder, so the jury really doesn’t amount
to a row of pins, anyhow. To allow the
coroner to sit on all inquests without a jury
would be giving him no more power than is
possessed by magistrates who, when inquiring
into the commission of any indictable offence,
sit without juries, and no one ever thinks this
is an injustice to the person charged. Coroners
juries are out of date, they are useless, they
are expensive—let us abolish them.
The Black North-West.
The white man came to the black north-west
For the east and south were want-opprest;
With a willing heart and a strong right hand
In search of work in a white man’s land.
He humped his drum, for his purse was low,
And he tramped cross-tracks that the drovers
know,
Till he struck a town in thy wild north-west
Which the dark Malay and the Jap. infest.
But his worn heart quailed when he learned, alack,
That he must be cheap, and he should be black.
For the master-men in the black north-west
Love the white men well but the cheap men best.
For a white man owns to a white man s vice,
He must have meat, but a Jap. eats rice ;
So the master men in the wild north-west
Love a white man well but a black man best.
They gave him work, out of charity,
At a black man’s wage in a white country ;
For you need not starve in a white man’s land,
You may have a bone if you lick the hand.
And he longed full oft with a hungering mouth,
To fight once more for the crumbs down south ;
But they held him low till his big heart shrunk
In the soulless wreck of a hopeless drunk.
Then the men white-faced with the inside black
Turned him south again on the dead men’s track,
Where the bird-picked bones of a man at rest
Warn the white men back from the black north-
west.
Let the cry go forth and be learnt by rote ;
Let the Patriot speak when the white men vote,
For the master men in the wild north-west
Keep a white man’s hell in an Asian nest.
Onslow, W.A.
WATT WHIPPLES.
“ Delcomyn ” on the Australian black
Editor Bulletin.—S.F. Monitor falls foul of the local
Chronicle for saying-, when reviewing Mark Twain’s last
booh that the Australian aborigines are Maoris. After
upsetting this statement, the Monitor itself goes for the
nigger. This is the indictment: —“ The native Australian
is a shiftless hound with a thirst. He is taciturn and
morose. He is a skulking coward who never faced an
enemy, but always struck in the hack. He is a thief and
a liar, incapable of being educated, and impervious to the
pleadings of civilisation. He is of low stature, and so
lazy that he will rather starve than work.” Now this is
making a bigger hash of things than the Chronicle man
made. In the first place, the writer is describing, or
partly describing, an Australian native ruined by “ the
pleadings of civilisation.” Of the native in his primal
state he evidently knows nothing. The myall nigger is
anything but shiftless and can subsist on the most
limited supply of fluid. The “ pleadings of civilisation
give him that expensive thirst. Instead of being
taciturn and morose, he is cheerful on the slightest
encouragement, and he is certainly not sulky. As to
striking in the back, etc., that is the sort of warfare he
has been brought up to ; it is his military tradition, and,
of course, he follows it, but he is no more a “ skulking
coward ” than the white man would be under similar cir-
cumstances—i.e., if defending his councry against in-
vasion of a powerful and superior raoe, armed with
unknown weapons of deadly precision. As to being
a thief and a liar: in his natural state he is not
a great thief, although he will pilfer a little until
matters are explained to him , and if he lies at times he
does it to please you. Ask him if there’s water in a
certain hole, he will not hesitate to assure you that there
is an ample supply; but this is done to give you
pleasure, as he knows you want to hear that there is
water there. A blackfellow will always say what he
thinks you want him to say ; and he lies with a reason-
ably-good motive. Instead of being impervious to the
pleadings of civilisation, he is singularly susceptible to
them, as represented by the civilisation of the back-
blocks, which is all that he comes in contact with, and
which’blights and degrades him at a touch. He is not
low in stature, but has a fairly high standard, far superior
to the present rising generation of Australian city-bred
whites, who generally reach the shoulder of the girl they
are walking with. And as to his laziness—why, if the
blackfellow would “ rather starve than work,” there
wouldn’t have been one here when the Dutch discovered
Australia. In his primitive state, uncontaminated by
white association, he is a hard worker, making finished
weapons, tools, hunting gear, baskets and fishing nets
with simple means of manufacture that would mock the
most ingenious European. No, the white man should not
call the black man lazy until he hunts the fat, loafing
bummers off our street corners. We have made the black
man a drunkard, a liar, and a loafer, and now abuse him
for learning our lesson so well.

“Jack the Giant-Killer” came back to
Sydney last week, and turned itself on for a short
spell at the Tivoli. By the time these remarks
appear in print, however, it will have only two
more nights to run, and, therefore, it doesn’t call
for more than 2 k columns of profound analysis.
The present “ Jack ” is not altogether the
gorgeous spectacle it was at the Royal a month or
two ago. Since then it has been dragged to Mel-
bourne and back—which is not meant as a literal
statement that it was tied on behind the train with
a string and towed through the grit—and some-
thing of the dust of travel has accumulated on its
head, and the cold cinder has got into its ear. In
its second edition it looks more like Bill than
Jack. This is only a vague, general impression
which it is difficult to analyse ; but, anyhow, the
new jokes don’t seem quite so good as the old
ones, and the new girls don’t look quite so lovely
as their predecessors, and the eo. hasn’t the same
space in which tospread itself as it had at the Royal,
andthenightTHE Bulletin was there pretty Lottie
Moore didn t do her old reliable somersault, and
the audience which expected to see her two plump
pink extremities flourish over her curly head was
disappointed. Still, the original “Jack” was
such a tine production that even in its somewhat
faded state it is a very respectable pantomime.
The Hulines, the Haytor family, the Tiller
quartette, and Story Gofton’s meaty giant are
all the same as they used to be, which is some-
thing, anyhow. And when the thin, silly boy
whacks the silly fat boy with a great whack on
his artificial stomach, it recalls pleasant memories
of old pantomimes in the flowery days that are
gone. That vacant, vast youth with the artificial
stomach that the unlettered thin youth whacks
him on is a great pantomime institution, and
when he is finally worn out and departs out of the
Xmas show for good, he will leave a large void
behind him.
** * *
The MacMahon firm stages “ A Yorkshire
Lass ” at Sydney Lyceum on Saturday. This
piece is new to Sydney and is understood to be a
picturesque war-drama of the Crimean period with
Blood in it, and the flag waving triumphantly
over the accumulated horrid cadaver on the gory
field of strife. All of which is very comforting
and also gives scope for a vast amount of beautiful
uniform.
* * *
“ Claudian ” is to keep going at Her
Majesty’s till Easter, when it will be succeeded by
the “ Silver King.” The old bad Roman with
the sneer and the legs seems quite callable, too, of
holding up his end of the log till then, or even a
great deal longer. There is a certain fascination
about these plays of the old gaudy times, even
apart from their intrinsic merits, and they are
full of interesting problems. The people don’t
seem to toil as they do now ; they are a garish,
bejewelled crowd in purple and fine linen, who
apparently make an honest living by selling each
other as slaves, and hollering at the Prefect, and
1 eaning up against the tremendous marble palace
of the bad Roman with the diamonds and the leer.
The ladies, at all events, don’t work ; if they did
the quaint robes which have no shape or waist,
and which hang anyhow, would assuredly fall off.
The Roman lady, when she rises in the morning,
evidently stands up in the middle of the
floor with nothing on her but the pure
atmosphere of Italy, and then her solitary
garment —a piece of cloth 40yds. long—is
hove at her with a fork by her attendant slave-girl
to slow music. It never rains in the Roman
drama, and even the brown hermit who leads his
blameless existence in a blue cave seems to stand
the exposure very well, and make a comfortable
existence by working miracles and having visions.
And if everything else failed the unemployed in
those days could always go out and denounce a
Christian for breakfast. Those good times are
past, unfortunately ; the Domain lodger of to-day
is denounced by the Christians instead, who write
to the papers about him.
** * *
Last week The Bulletin complained that
** Claudian” dragged a good deal, but on second
thoughts it is perhaps right that it should. The
Roman drama has to go slow, else the lady of the
period would get mixed up in the tail of her un-
accountable garment and come down wallop, and
the very brief tail of the Roman gentleman’s kilt
would fly up disgracefully behind if he put on a
apurt ; and everything has to wander with an
impressive stride to be in keeping. Claudian even
dies slowly after making a long oration
among the ruins of his gorgeous palace, which fell
down gradually in the previous act, and as he dies
with his feet to the audience he has to fall down
gently, so that his brief skirt may not be disar-
ranged by the wind. After a second view, the
restful atmosphere of the drama began to soak
more effectually into the writer’s soul, and his
views on “Claudian” are more favourable. But
he deeply regrets the conversion of that tremendous
Eagan. The unregenerate Prefect whose evil leer
lights the vegetation is a far more effective char-
acter than the benevolent gentleman of the three
succeeding acts. The vast Roman legs don’t go
so well with benevolence and morality somehow.
