* * #
Sydney Opera House closed up so unob-
trusively one night not long ago that the exact
«late of its closure escaped this paper’s notice.
There was no public announcement of the end of
the Barnes-Stanford season ; one night the greasy
portal remained shut, and the forlorn dog was left
in possession of the door-step, and that was all.
The Barnes-Stanford firm induced the old house
to keep going longer than it generally does; but
the usual downfall was inevitable in any case.
The writer had a horrible vision once that Wil-
liamson was induced to take the Opera House on
a long lease, and that he played Wilson Barrett
there to an audience of one, at eighteenpeuce,
but when he awoke he found that the dream was
merely the result of a cat-fight in the garden.
** * *
The Cartwright Comedy co. will open in
Sydney with “ A Marriage of Convenience,” and
“Dream Faces.” From which it would appear
that Cartwright isn’t a star who shines brightest
as a self-advertiser. In “The Marriage,” he
doesn’t show at all in the first two acts, and the
“fattest” character in the comedy falls to
Thursby, the beauty-man of the crowd. Cart-
wright’s seventeen-year-old daughter, Miss Edith
Morley, is said to have had some stage tuition in
Germany before her father brought her along to
try the effect of the fair damsel on the Australian
“ dog.” The same dawg will submit to any number
of such experiments without barking. Miss Edith,
in the language of profane bagmen, is a “plum,”
likewise a “ peach ” glistening with morning dew.
The grocer who described her as a consignment of
sugar is not a poet.
** * *
The public which stays away from Melb.
Bijou missed another bright and brainy enter-
tainment on Saturday. Good things are happen-
ing at the Bijou, but Charles Cartwright’s season
is frost-bitten, and, though the performance
“knocks” a limited first-night audience, the
applause is not to be accepted as a promise of
good business to come. “ The Tree of Know-
ledge ” was an artistic success on Saturday,
nevertheless it is probably a distinct financial
failure at time of writing, in which case “ The
Idler ” will take its place for the final week of the
company’s wasted endeavors. Time for Rickards
and Cartwright to complain that they haven’t re-
ceived sufficient encouragement from the Mel-
bourne press. In the flariug light of the Albani
concert-notices their grievance is very apparent.
* • * •
“The Tree of Knowledge,” by R. C.
Carton, is strong in everything but tricky effects.
It puts well-drawn characters upon the stage, and
they tell an interesting story in a natural way.
There are no clap-trap situations, no violent asser-
tions of one character’s importance at the ex-
pense of all the others. A rather gloomy bachelor,
who has lived with a fascinating light o’ lpve and
been duly deserted by her, chooses to reckon him-
self unfit for marriage with his mother’s adopted
daughter. The fascinating wicked creature turns
up again as the wife of the gloomy bachelor’s best
friend (a manly simpleton), and, having discovered
that her young husband isn’t as rich as
she took him for, she elopes with a cyni-
cal capitalist after a stormy interview with
her ex-lover, who would fain strangle the lady
rather than let her now treat his best friend
as she had treated him ten years ago. Then, the
adventuress, and the deserted husband, and the
drawling capitalist having played their parts, the
gloomy bachelor is left in the last act to marry his
mother’s nice adopted daughter and make the two
women happy. The whole-souled harlot, when
last seen, was going off in the darkness with her
new protector, and her husband was asleep on the
sofa when the drama left him. She kissed the
sleeper on his forehead ere she went away, re-
marking that the best and the worst women are
equally subject to such little weaknesses. A keen
moralist this wicked female. She had previously
remarked that “ no virtue is so austere as that of
the man who has found somebody else.”
• * * •
Miss Beatrice Lamb plays the adventuress
well and truly—a striking performance by a gor-
geously-apparelled artist. This is Miss Lamb’s
best achievement in Melbourne ; also Arthur
Styan, as the cynical “betrayer,” Miss Stewart,
as a white-haired parent, and Miss Darragh, as
the nice girl, are better than they have been in
any previous piece. Charles Thursby is a good-
looking young husband, anyhow. Hartley
Manners appears in the sham whiskers
of a ruined father to great disadvantage, but
Edwin Lester is singularly well-suited to the
small part of a mayor. Charles Cartwright is not
at his strongest as the gloomy bachelor, but Cart-
wright in any capacity is interesting and mag-
netic, and his attempt to choke Miss Lamb seems
likely to result fatally, sooner or later. “ The
Tree of Knowledge ” is mounted regardless of
empty benches. An admirable show.
** # *
“ When London Sleeps,” at the Royal, is
a tremendously-farcical melodrama with a lucra-
tive future before it. The seeds of trouble are
sown in the green-room of a travelling circus,
where a vociferous Fitzgerald Brother keeps the
peace among any members of the co. who may be
thirsting for one another’s blood. Here the White
Queen of the Slack Wire tells her love to an ele-
gant hero in drab tights and spangles, and becomes
suddenly possessed of a vast inheritance. From
that time forward the White Queen is chased by
a remorseless captain, who is next heir to
the inheritance, also by a circus lady who
covets the spangled hero, and the married
life of the White Queen becomes most un-
comfortable. They lure her into a haunt of
vice, concerning which The Bulletin will blush
in silence. Her character is saved, however, by a
clown on this occasion. A subsequent plot to
roast the White Queen and her child in the dead
of night, when London sleeps, is frustrated by a
stroke of dramatic genius. The house in which
she was shut up to burn happens to be “on the
telephone,” and the Queen —or somebody like her
—walks along the telephone-wire with the child
on her back. There is no apparent decep-
tion about this awful proceeding, which is
illuminated by stage-flames from the burning
housetop. In the last act the Queen gets
casually left in a temple at an aliened Indian Ex-
hibition (in London), and the captain, disguised
as a high-priest, is roasting her upon the altar of
Somnah when her husband springs from the
interior of the sacred idol and hands the captain
over to the police. The bustle, scenery, and
splendid foolishness of “ When London Sleeps ”
form a pleasant evening’s amusement. W. J.
Montgomery, Charles Holloway, Edwin Kelly, J.
P. O’Neill, William and Amy Gourley, Miss
Duggan, Miss Deorwyn, and 20 other people took
part in the festivities. The telephone wire-worker
is understood to be an expert professional, and
The Bulletin hopes she is sure-footed, for the
cheeild’s sake.

At the Alexandra they are playing a some-
what less lurid drama than usual under the title
of “The Days of the Land Boom.’ The title
looks promising, but the plot, on investigation,
proves to be lacking in local allusions, and
although Dan. Barry himself is very reminiscent
of a famous undertaker, deceased, the resemblance
is seemingly accidental. “ The Days of the
Boom ” will not run for more than one consecu-
tive week.
** * *
Florence Schmidt has gone on a concert-
tour to her native Queensland, where, unless the
natives make the same mistake as the Victorians
in Melba’s case, she will make a pile. This young
lady, who possesses one of the finest soprano
voices ever put into a human throat, is struggling
to raise enough money to finish her studies in
Europe. If her Queensland countrymen waste
this opportunity of doing honor to themselves by
doing honor to her, they will miss a chance that
may never occur again in a blue moon. All sorts
of things may happen in a country like Queens-
land, but a great soprano voice combined with a
true musical soul only happens rarely in
the whole world. When it does come, let us
not wait for the “’Ome” imprimatur, but
sail in on our own responsibility and rave
at first hand, instead of doing a lot of
second-hand raving about great singers who come
here in their decadence. Florence Schmidt has a
great, pure soprano, of endless range and limit-
less power. She tills the big Sydney Town Hall
with perfect ease. Though she may lack the cul-
chaw and finish of the Baris polishing-schools,
she, at any rate, has not had the lusciousness and
freshness of her voice polished out of her. Sydney
is going to give her a benefit; and, if the Queens-
landers don’t do as much for her as the Southerners,
they deserve to produce nothing but politicians.
** * *
More American comment on Melba—this
time complimentary, by clever Geraldine Bonner
in S.F. Argonaut—
What a voice ! To what class did it belong? What did
it suggest? It was Italian—at least, the voice that
belongs to Italian music. It had in it all that soulless,
pagan joy that exults and throbs in the music of Italy.
It was one of those voices, like Patti’s, that seem in some
way above the responsibilities and pains and passions of
this world, and yet is not a voice that belongs to the
world of harps and angels. They are the voices of the
Golden Age, without conscience, without care, without
concern, 'full of the love of life, echoing the songs
of birds, the ripple of water, the murmurous music
of the wind in trees. Of the clamors, and tears,
and groans of the children of this world they know
nothing and hold no notes of understanding or of sym-
pathy. Wagner felt with these, and he made his music
cry with the cries of their anguish and thrill with the
pulse of their rapture. But the Italian voices—the
singers of the pure bel canto— shake out their notes like
the bird balancing on the branch, oblivious, indifferent,
but exultingly perfect. Melba’s voice is neither so flexible
nor so ethereal as Patti’s. The flute-like quality is not so
remarkable. The tone is richer, suggests in some way
■ more the singing of a woman and less the singing of a
sprite or an Undine. Yet it has not the lucidly sym-
pathetic tone which made Albani’s the most caressingly
feminine voice of its day and generation. It is pagan
and sensuous, unconcerned and without heart, but
full of a measureless joy and delight. One of the
most remarkable things about it is its suggestion
of fluent, effortless force and abundance. The ease with
which it is produced, the prodigal lavishness with which
it is used mid the strength and freshness of it give an
impress! m '< inexhaustible, buoyant power and richness.
For herself, Melba is a stout, handsome woman, looking
like a Jewess. She is not graceful, and she makes little
or no effort to act. Indeed, there is an air about her as
of one who says : “I sing for you—that is enough !”
Dressed magnificently in pink, embroidered in silver tin-
sel, she went through her long solo in the first act —the
Opera, by the way, was “Traviata”—walking up and down
the stage, and now and then approaching the footlights,
and challengingly -almost insolently in the power of her
triumph—flinging at the audience a tour de force of song.
* * *-
C.R. : The Continental Concert experi-
ment tried at Melb. Exhibition was a sad financial
failure - chiefly because the Govt, which, in
matters of this sort, is the bond-slave of every
Big or Little Bethel (always with the genial ex-
ception of the R.O. Ebenezer) permits only
temperance bilge-water to be sold in places
of public entertainment after 7 p.m. This
means that men’s liquor is not sold in
these places at all, as they don’t open until
after that time. The reason of the restric-
tion is obvious. If such drinks as lager beer or
wir.e were allowed to be sold at the C. Concerts,
for instance, the majority of the male audience
would, of course, get hog-drunk as rapidly as
possible, and then stagger out and lurch and
plunge through the business streets to the annoy-
ance of God-fearing persons who would then be in
the thick of their Saturday night’s shopping.
At Poverty Point.
Kyrle Bellew and Mrs. Potter, at latest dates, were
doing fairly at London Adelphi with “ Charlotte Corday”
in the evenings and “Lady of Lyons ” matinees. Even
London seems to have outlived the artificial sentiment of
Bulwer Lytton’s piny, and the critics treat the revival as
an interesting curio in dramatic art. Bellew, however,
gets warm praise for his hearty, picturesque rendering of
Claude Melnotte, and Mrs. P., in the name-part, is kindly
spoken of. It goes without saying that they don't get
praised up to their own ideas of “ fair play.” Mummers
in London (as elsewhere) protest against alleged news-
paper plots to crush their respective shows for the sake
of Irving, or Beerbohtn Tree, or George Alexander, or
somebody else who happens to be the rage. They tell
most absurd yarns, based on the purely mummeric
supposition that the theatrical notice writers, editors,
and proprietors of at least twenty important journals
form a happy family of conspirators, engaged in an ever-
lasting attempt to ruin deserving showmen. It is the
accursed cant of the perfeshun. All mummers and
managers have it more or less on their tongues or in their
hearts. Look even at that level-headi d commercial actor,
Wilson Barrett. No man has been more generously
treated by the press, and yet when he aims at paralysing
the public as a tragedian, and gets only moderate praise,
his satellites start the “ conspiracy ” cry. In the vocabu-
lary of histrionic conceit there is no such word as Fail.
Charles Thursby, the beauteous young man of the Cart-
wright Comedy Co., is vaguely rumored to be the son of
an earl, or something of that kind. Probably the female
wish is mother to the thought. Melbourne girls—as
many as saw him in “A Marriage of Convenience”—
adore Thursby, and all girls like to dearly love the son of
a lord.
Walter Bentley, foiled for aldermanship, consoles him-
self with the alluring thought of tacking M.L A. to his
name after next Q. elections. As monitor of Saturday
Nioiit he keeps his name well in the front, and “does a
Ranji ”in the daily press whenever he can. “ Speak well
o’ mv bairn, speak ill o’ my bairn, but aye keep speaking
of him.”
Carl Hertz (writes one of his assistants to a Melbourne
friend) is doing well in India, but there are drawbacks.
Says the letter : “ This is a dreadful country. It is now
135 deg. in the shade, and they say that’s not hot! But
the heat’s not the only misfortune; the stink of the
blackfeilows is enough to knock you down—worse than
the Yarra used to be! The boss is doing splendid biz.
He has got the audiences by the wool; he can’t go wrong,
for he has the luck of a Chinaman. We are turning them
away nightly. Played three weeks in Bombay, and could
have played another three, but the plague was raging so
badly we were glad to get away. They are dying in hun-
dreds daily at Bombay. It is terrible to walk out, for you
see nothing but corpses being carried away. It is all
right in Calcutta ; the plague has not reached there yet,
but the authorities are very particular—they examine you
about 50 miles along the line from Bombay to Calcutta.
Next address, Hongkong, G.P.0.”
One of city organist Wiegand’s musical enemies, testi-
fying before Sydney Council, remarked that “if one
pandered to the public taste, the most rapturous applause
would follow the performance of ‘ Pop goes the Weasel.’ ”
Which incited the combative Wiegand to give his follow-
ing vveek’s audience the tune in question. “This air,”
says the D.T.s musical crank, “considered by some
experts to possess no educational value, was a pregnant
one to M. Wiegand. His improvisation, spontaneous and
natural, was distinguished for its contrapuntal features.
At times he treated his theme playfully, toying with the
weasel in a freer style. Allowing his fancy full play, he
produced some dainty effects with the carillons, and,
finally returning to a serious vein, he closed in a most
profound and scholarly style. ‘Bravos!’ and much
applause greeted the improvisation.”

Reid’s Confession of Faith.
It has always been a matter of uncertainty in
Sydney whether the Herald or the Telegraph
owned Premier Reid, but last Monday the ques-
tion was practically settled. The two papers
parted company on the Federal question, the
Herald going for the high ideal of Australian
nationhood, while the Telegraph decided to
grovel in the mud of provincialism and sacrifice
all the great possibilities of the future to the
imported calico interests of the present. And
when they parted company they both whistled to
Reid to follow them, whereupon he decided his
ownership by running after the Herald. He is
now following the Herald slowly, with his tail
down, and at a considerable distance —but still he
is following it. On Monday last, in the presence
of a vast public meeting, Premier Reid defined
himself as a Federationist, though not a very
enthusiastic one. He devoted more time, cer-
tainly, to pointing out the disadvantages of
the Convention Bill than its advantages—still,
in the end, he promised to give his own .vote
for the acceptance of the Constitution, and the
mere promise counts for a great deal. His analysis
of the Convention scheme was, in most respects,
very fair, despite a streak of occasional hostility.
The most notable statement in it was the one
which knocked the ground from under the
Telegraph’s crude invention, that the Conven-
tion had committed the future Federal Govern-
ment to Protection, and that every true apostle
of shoddy should vote against it in consequence.
The only clause in the. Constitution which can
possibly be tortured into an appearance of com-
mitting the Commonwealth to any fiscal policy is
Premier Braddon’s famous amendment, according
to which only one-fourth of the Customs and
Excise revenue is to be retained by the
Federal Government for its own expenses,
while the other three-fourths are to be divided
among the States. The parochial shoddyites
represent this as meaning that the Federal Go em-
inent is to pay all its expenses out of one-fourth of
the customs and excise revenue and return the
balance, thereby necessitating a very heavy tariff.
Premier Reid’s explanation put the matter in its
true light, and showed that the clause was rather
an incentive to the Federal Treasurer to raise
what revenue he required by direct taxation than
otherwise. If the Federal Treasurer wants
<£250,000 and raises it by direct taxation he need
only raise the quarter million he requires. If he
wants <£250,000 and proposes to get it by customs
duties he must collect a whole million, for of every
<£l he collects in customs duties he is bound to
hand 15s. to the State Treasuries. Therefore, it is
very much easier for him at a financial crisis to
raise revenue by income or land tax than by
Customs, and the assertion that the Common-
wealth is committed to Protection stands out, not
only as a fiction, but as an infamously stupid one.
The most lamentable part of Premier Reid’s
speech was his declaration that he was bitterly
opposed to federating the railways ; but even'with
this reservation in favor of provincialism he has
declared himself sufficiently to aggravate his old
supporter, the Telegraph, to red-hot fury. At
time of writing Premier Reid and the Telegraph
don’t speak, but merely heave road-metal at each
other as they pass by.
The Ship Atacama.
The Bulletin has received a letter from
Messrs. Sly and Russell, solicitors ’to Mr. Mahlon
C. Cowlishaw, which states that Mr. Cowlishaw
was the sole registered owner of the lost ship
Atacama ;, and, that it cost Mr. Cowlishaw £lBOO
to do the repairs. Messrs. Sly and Russell further
state that the captain of the Atacama, who super-
intended the repairs, had carte blanche to do
everything that was necessary so that the vessel
should be in a seaworthy condition in every re*-
peot.

* * » *
The Maoriland Government expects to close its
financial year at the end of this month with the splendid
surplus of £400,000. With £400,000 to spend on public
works, the floating of a big loan becomes all the more in-
excusable.
* * • *
The Victorian State Land Bank announces its
first ballot for the repayment of bonds. According to a
particularly insane provision of the Act, money received
as repayments from borrowers must not be lent out again
however much it is needed, but must be paid back to the
bondholders. Therefore the State Land Bank is selling
new bonds with one hand and repaying old ones with the
other. The lenders don’t want their money back—what
they do want is a long, safe investment. The arrange-
ment by which their money may be forcibly repaid to
them before they have even drawn one year’s interest
makes it difficult to get the public to invest in these bonds
at all. The State Land Bank doesn’t want to part with the
money ; it has plenty of uses for it in its business. But
the fool of a law says that things must be done this way,
so they are done this way.
• * * *
The Insurance and Banking Record figures
out that 14 Australasian banks have lost £11,842,721
among them since 1893. And this doesn’t include the
losses of the Federal, Mercantile, and Bank of South
Australia, and of the thirty or forty land banks and build-
ing societies which have died or reconstructed since the
boom, nor the immense losses of certain large loan and
mortgage concerns. If these had been included and the
figures had gone back to ’9l, probably the losses of public
companies alone would have been not far from £30,000,000.
Among the banks, the biggest single item in the list is
that of the Q.N.B. (£2,332,684). Then comes the Com-
mercial of Australia (£2,061,547), the B.N.Z. (£1,453,331
written off in the last five years, though if its previous
writings-down were included it would be the boss disaster
of the lot), and the E.S. and A. (£1,233,863). The
Record’s list deals only with losses actually announced,
but anyone who knows anything of the inner workings of
Australian banks could locate another £2,000,000 or more
which certain banks and alleged banks have yet to make
provision for. f
* * * *
At the end of 1892 the banks doing business in
Australasia had £8,833,296 of reserves among them. At
the end of ’97 they could only muster £5,029,042.
The most lamentable bank on earth —the Stan-
dard Bank of Melbourne —reached the end of another
stage in its career on 31st December, 1897. The half-
year’s income was £1246, and the expenditure (interest
on borrowed money and working expenses) was £10,479,
so the outlay was 8J times the receipts. Some of the
doings of this amazing bank are a? follows: —
Receipts. Expenditure. Deficit.
December, 1894 .. £26,744 £33,581 £6,837
June, 1895 .. .. 23,238 28,146 4,908
December, 1895 .. 17,241 18,754 1,513
June, 1896 .. ... 10,962 18,026 7,064
December, 1896 ... 3,268 i 1,261 7,993
June, 1897 .. .. 1,571 10,678 9,107
December, 1897 .. 1,246 10,749 9,503
It is difficult to imagine anything more hopeless than a
bank which gets its deposit money at the exceedingly
low rate of 2£ per cent., and yet can’t earn enough
to pay one-eighth of its outgoings. Why the utterly
disgraceful Standard wasn’t blotted out long ago when
(by comparison) respectable institutions like the City
of Melbourne, Mercantile, and Federal went under,
is a mystery. If the latest receipts are any
criterion, the Standard’s assets should be saleable for
about £65,000 as against about £756,000 of liabilities to
the public. The two biggest assets are “ Doubtful
accounts, £520,105,” and accumulated deficiency, £435,838.
The first batch of reconstructed deposits will become due
in a few months, and, as the Standard is obviously quite
unable to pay, the depositors have been requested to decide
what they intend to do with the most melancholy bank on
earth. If they are well-advised, they will do something
sudden and to the point, and prevent the institution
publishing any more dismal half-yearly literature. As a
thoroughly bad advertisement for Victoria, the Standard
Bank is unique.
• * * *
The Queensland Auditor - General’s report on
the Q.N. Bank makes things look rather better, in one
respect, than they appeared in the balance-sheet. The
“ profit, £8000,” certainly didn’t look genuine, by reason
of its beastly exactness, but the A.-G. states that the
visible profit was £16,635 Bs. lid. of which £8635
Bs. lid. was transferred on general principles to the
contingency account now being accumulated to cover
further depreciation. He also adds that the bank
property has now been written down to the
actual valuation. Incidentally, however, he turns a
merciless light on the smallness of the Q.N.B.’s business
nowadays ; out of “ Private deposits and other liabilities
£918,911,” it appears that £551,907 are current account s
and about £60,000 fixed deposits and interests; the rest
is “ other liabilities.” Also, the board explained proudly
that the securities sold during the year realised
£10,929 over the book values, arid this created
a general idea that the great mass of assets
had been written down to less than their real
value. The A.-G. reports that the profit was made by
selling Mount Morgan shares on a rise, and that the other
properties realised less than the estimate. This leaves
the old doubt about the possibility of further losses still
in full force. In these and other matters the report of
the Queensland Auditor-General is so different from the
average auditor’s meagre, sketchy certificate that Tiie
Bulletin’s conviction as to the advisability of a regular
State audit of all banks is more emphatic than ever.
* * » *
The Modern Permanent Building Society (Mel-
bourne) has negotiated with its creditors to keep on
accepting the reduced interest rate of 2£ per cent.,
and it gets five years extra for repayment of the
principal. If the Society’s earnings are large enough
to allow of it the depositors are to get more than 2£
per cent, and not over 5 per cent., but the chance is
probably too small to call for much consideration. Last
year the Modern made a loss of £7511 ; and out of
£688,000 assets £345,000 consists of house property in
possession and nearly £lOO,OOO is “ vacant land.” The
chief joy of having £345,000 worth of house property in
Melbourne now is that all these houses have to be con-
nected at heavy expense with the new Fitzgibbon sewers.
The Modern’s values are mostly the old paper values of
the good old times, and this big institution doesn’t even
pretend to know where it are, as things go now. In fact,
it simply says that its assets used to be worth so much,
which is about as much to the point as the fact that there
used to be a megatharium.
* » * •
That comparatively new concern, the Farmers’
Co-operative Fire and Marine Insurance Association of
Canterbury (M.L.) continues to prosper remarkably. The
net premiums for the year were £44s4—about £4OO more
than last year—and the profit on insurance business alone
was £2114. The co. also received £2929 as interest on in-
vestments, so the total profits are £5043. Shareholders
get 6 per cent, dividend, and a bonus of 10 per cent, on
their business ; also, £lOOO is added to reserve, making it
£4OOO, and about £5OO is carried forward. The Farmers’
Co-operative co. is the newest insurance co. in Austra-
lasia, and, considering the general tendency to sell the
business to Bull, any attempt at forming a new local co.
is commendable. Also, considering that it has £48,910
capital and £5850 of reserves to set against its very small
amount of insurance risks, it may fairly claim to be the
safest fire and marine co. in the Seven Provinces.
* * * •
The Standard Fire and Marine Insurance Co.,
of Maoriland, was struck heavily by the Melbourne fire,
and the year ended 31st December, ’97, showed a loss of
£4872. The co. paid a half-year’s dividend at the rate of
7J per cent, before the disaster fell upon it, and, after
that, not having the courage to stop the div. altogether,
it paid at the rate of 21 per cent, per annum for the
second half—making 5 per cent, for the year. Adding
the loss of £4872 and the dividend of £3750 together, the
Standard went £8622 to the bad, and has had to take
£lO,OOO from reserves. This leaves it with £75,000 capi-
tal and £35,229 reserves, and, if it wants to pull itself to-
gether again, it will need to keep the dividend down to 5
per cent, for a few years to come. For a concern of the
size and age of the Standard, the reserves are not nearly
so large as they ought to be. The biggest part of the
Standard’s assets appear simply as “ Investments,
£62,692,” which is about as little information as could
well be put into one line of print.
* • * *
At the end of 1897 Sydney and its contents
were insured for £60,426,000. Of this amount, N.S.W.
companies held 24J millions ; other Australasian com-
panies, 9J millions ; British and other foreign companies,
26j millions. In the last three years the business of the
Australasian companies remained stationary, while that
of foreign companies increased by f-million. But since
the end of ’97 three local co’s doing business in Sydney
have sold out to Bull, so the foreign offices now hold prac-
tically half the business.
» * * *
The costly Melbourne sewerage scheme is a vast
blessing in one respect, anyhow. It is causing the sum-
mary destruction of hundreds of foul, dilapidated two and
three-roomed wooden shanties in the low-lying regions of
Richmond and Oollingwood—structures which are so far
gone as not to be worth the cost of connection with the
new sewers. Melbourne was so over-built in the boom
that a lot of these mean little shanties have never been
wanted since, and have been steadily decaying for years
past. Their removal gets rid of many grievous eye-sores
and fever-beds, and, by reducing the number of super-
fluous houses, it will probably increase the value of those
which remain to an extent that will more than cover the
loss. Another advantage is that these old-time hovels
were often built on frontages just about a dog’s jump
across, and, when once they come down, the new building
regulations will prevent any similar horrors taking their
places.
* * * *
Advice to Australians' about to try their for-
tune on the Yukon : Never play cards ; never carry a
pistol when in camp. No cards, no rows ; and the “ gun
less man is never shot—at least, not more than about once
in a blue moon.—Uncle Sam.
* * * »
Shipowners on the Pacific Slope seem to be
rigging up all the old hookers available to carry human
cargoes to the Klondyke. Already two vessels bound
from ’Frisco have been lost with all on board. One
barque found floating keel-upwards (she had sailed with
40 souls), was built exactly 50 years ago. The Bulletin
predicts that a few ancient vessels which Australian
owners now have in view, will also fulfil their apparent
destiny, and plunge down in the submarine wake of many
a Newcastle coal-ship or Californian emigrant vessel.

from Australasia.

* * * •
The 1721 b. slug of dyscrasite found in B.H.
Consols mine, the other day, is one of the largest ever
won from the property—always remarkable for its silver
marvels. The slug, which had to be dug out of oxidised
iron, will yield about 80 per cent, of fine silver. The
famous “Turtle” slug discovered in 1891 weighed over
16001 b., and was worth, at the then price of silver, about
£4OOO.
* * * *
British B.H. directors still have strong hopes of
the success of the English sulphide-treating process, so
far secret, which, for two years past, they have had under
their wing. A parcel of 50 tons ore has just been sent
’Ome for experimental treatment on a practical scale.
The British, by the way, was the scene of the last serious
mine-accident at Broken Hill, three lives being lost; and
there has been a pretty strong tendency to go for the co.
for damages over the matter. But the labor bodies, which
were moving, split on the subject, and are now calling
one another names.

Announced that an error has been found in the
return of matte from the Lake George mines, N.S.W. —
47J tons in excess of the actual quantity sent away. That
represents about 20 tons of copper besides gold and silver.
Somebody will probably suffer for this. Time has shown
in this case how much easier in mining it is to foreshadow
great things than to accomplish them.
* * * *
Though a somewhat disappointing mine, the
Myalls United (N.S.W.) is doing good work with 7 and
Bdwt. rock. The cost of mining, milling, and manage-
ment is given as 11s. 3d. per ton, or about 15s. including
cyaniding. A good profit is thus shown, but with such a
capital it will take a big production to pay small divs.
Victory Forest Reefs (N.S.W.): The directors
claim that the mining development on this mine is the
quickest done in Australasia. Divs. are talked of, but
there are some heavy debts to clear off.
* * * *
Again something seems to have gone a bit wrong
with the squatters’ mine, the Overflow, N.S.W. One man
proposed to cradle the ore for gold ; then a lead-smelting
furnace was talked of, and one purchased ; finally dry-
crushing and cyanide were resorted to. News that every-
thing was going like clockwork came along, and shares
moved upwards. Then followed just a whisper that per-
colation was slow. The slime trouble again ?
* * « *
Cobar Great Western, N.8.W., decided to add
another 10 stamps to the running ten. Seems to writer
the better plan would be to add a cyanide plant instead.
That would have doubled the production at very much
less cost. It could have been erected for less money than
it will take to conserve water to ensure the constant run-
ning of a 20-head mill.
* * * •
The pneumatophors, which Sydney Smith,
N.S.W. Minister for Mines, imported on the strength of
reports that they were new contrivances that would per-
mit work to be carried on in underground places full of
smoke or gas, have hardly come up to expectations. Ex-
tensive trials have been made at Broken Hill, where great
loss of property and life has hitherto occurred upon out-
breaks of fire in the extremely gaseous Oregon timber ;
but, although men, when supplied with the instrument,
can breathe for half-an-hour in any atmosphere, the
mouthpiece and other parts are so awkward and unwieldy
that the contrivance is declared useless for anything be-
yond exploratory purposes and in the most favorable
circumstances. Further, it does not afford any protection
to the eyes against smoke or gas, though that could, no
doubt, be overcome.

• * * *
The Klondike Prospecting Dish, advertised in
this issue, is supported by testimonials from the N.S.W.
Inspector of Mines and other authorities, as being a great
improvement on the primitive, no-account dish of earlier
days. The manager of the Mount Cornish mine, in par-
ticular, booms it, on the ground that it washes the dirt
much faster than the old dish and saves all the gold—
which the common or garden vessel certainly doesn’t.
The manager of the Beacon gold-mine gives similar testi-
mony—he reports that he put 3gr. of gold in a dish of
gravel, and recovered it all by the washing process in
ljmin., while the same experiment in an ordinary dish
took 3min. and lost igr.

* * * *
The Pikedale Copper Mine (Q.) near the N.S.W.
border, the prospectus of which has been extensively
advertised throughout Australia, has started to work in
earnest, the flotation having been underwritten in Bris-
bane. The managing director is Mr. Rodda, for some
years manager of Lake George. The ore runs from 6ft. to
12ft. wide, from 2 to 10 per cent, copper, and averages
some 20oz. silver. Some thousands of tons have been
smelted. It is on a freehold, and the only drawback is the
owner’s royalty.
* * * *
The Leigh’s Creek coal deposit in northern S.A.
may be developed, after all. An Adelaide-English syndi-
cate is nosing it and a number of far-northern copper
shows. But, as to the latter, the report of successful
trials of iron telephone wires (copper has always been used
hitherto) comes as a steadier.
• * * *
George Lansell, the Bendigo mining king, has
at last done a rash thing, of which he ought to be ashamed
in his old age, and which, on reflection, he will surely
regret. Some weeks ago a number of men, including an
underground boss, were suddenly discharged, and
recently he intimated that others would follow. Suspicion
of gold-stealing the cause. The men waited on Lansell,
and pointed out the difficulty of getting work elsewhere
with the rumor abroad. The old man was adamant, and
th£ men declined to wait for their dismissal and left.
Their manager, Northcott, a relative of Lansell’s, who has
been manager of the 180 mine for about 30 years, sup-
ported the men’s attitude by his own resignation. Bravo !
Northcott. From all accounts, it would be hard to do
much gold-stealing in the mine, which has for years been
poorer than a church-mouse. The Victoria Quartz, the
adjoining mine, is much richer, and there the men have
asked in writing to be searched daily.
» * ♦ *
The Deddick movement in Melb. grows more
interesting and humorous. Last week there was a con-
ference of Deddickskites, representing the parent “mine”
and its large litter of kittens, and, “ on the motion of
Mr. Begelhole, seconded by Mr. J. B. Tonner, it was
decided to take united action to secure railway, telephonic,
and postal services for the field by forming a permanent
committee.” It has to be noted that the scene of this
sparkling Begelhole-Tonner comedy was Chief-Secretary
Peacock’s office in Collins-st., wherefore the “united
action ” of Begelhole, Tonner and co. promises to express
itself in further appeals to the Minister of Mines for the
railway, telephonic, and postal services aforesaid. Minis-
ter Foster’s constituents are scattered around the dark-
some Deddick district, and Chief-Sec. Peacock is legal-
manager for one or more Deddick syndicates. The much-
boomed Gippsland mountain may be what Begelhole and
Tonner say it is, or it may be the “ frost ” it has appeared
to sundry other people ; anyhow, the obvious political
duty of Peacock is to let the Deddick crowd do its
hustling in somebody else’s office.
* « * *
Some £50,000 has been spent in prospecting the
old Poverty mine, Vic., since the gold gave out some 20
year’s ago, about half of it by the present owners—an
English co. that has reason to expect better things in
opening up the next lift. Curious name for a mine that
crushed many thousand tons of three, four, and five-oz.
stuff, and yielded gold to the value of 1J million. Origin-
ally consisted of three claims, but the whole of the gold
came from less than 150 ft. of ground, and most of it from
25ft.
• • » •
The M.L. Govt, proposes to charge for the use
of the cyanide process H per cent, royalty on ore of less
value than £3, 2 per cent, between £3 and £4, and 21 per
cent, for all above £4 per ton in value. The patentees would
never have had any trouble in Australasia if they had
been content with such charges. As it is they get heavy
royalties from a few works, and none at all from a great
number. When is this question going to be fought out in
N.S.W. ?
* * • *
Quartz : “Mr. Knox greatly errs in stating the
divs. of the North Cross G.M. co., Stawell, at £300,000.
The North Cross paid over £750,000. The £300,000 re-
ferred to by Mr. Knox was paid by the Oriental G.M. co.,
the adjoining lease, south, on same line of reef. The
Oriental co. had 206 ft. along the reef, and an area of two
acres; from this it raised 85,893 tons of quartz for
141,0360 z; value, £556,612. The North Cross returns
are : Tons crushed, 266,488, for gold, 323,7120 z ; value,
£1,189,377 ; divs. paid, £751,742, equal to £75 3s. 9d. per
share (10,000 th). In ’BB, the Oriental proprietary bought
the North Cross lea,se and plant, and have since spent
over £40,000 in plant and resultless prospecting. Now
driving for the Magdala-Moonlight reef, which they should
reach shortly.”
* * * *
W.A. Mines-Minister said “he could not
account for the shrinkage in the gold-output for Feb., but
he knew that 96000 z. were in the banks at Geraldton, and
were, therefore, not included in that month’s returns.”
Well, they will no doubt swell next month’s production.
Next to nothing has been said about the extent of the
shrinkage. It amounted to 40,0000 z., or nearly 40 per
cent., and leaves Feb. with the lovvest return for seven
months. It looks, too, that some time will elapse before
the Jan. production of over 93,0000 z. will be beaten.
* * * *
Some mine-owners having started to work
aboriginals in W.A., Minister Wittenoom was asked if
it was legal, and replied that, while the blacks could not
hold miners’ rights, there was nothing to prevent them
working on a mine for wages. Surely the nigger’s claim
to the country is sufficient to let him have a miner’s right
if he can make any use of it 1

A Coolgardie paper asserts that A. E. Morgan,
M.P., has a scheme for the booming of that camp. It is
not at liberty to divulge details, but Morgan will trot it
out as soon as the alluvial trouble is dealt with. Babbits !
• * » *
“ The feature of the market was supplied by
Hannan’s Paringa shares which had a heavy fail, notwith-
standing the excellent position of the mine and its posses-
sion of two promising reefs. There is no news to account
for the drop.”—B. M. Herald. More correct to say there
never was any reason to account for the rise. It is
nothing but solid reason that has brought them down,
and scores of others as well. Let us again try to en-
lighten Granny. In her share-list of that day there were
others : Hannan’s Block 45 —ss. 4d. They were once
more than ten times that price. Golden Bar, Is. 9d.
They reached £1 once, not on developments, but because
of the way in which the newspapers, including the
S.M. Herald, were fooled, and the way they fooled the
public. Lake View South, 21s. 6d. The mine is
looking better than when they were 605., but the boom
has gone. The writer could go on through the whole list.
Some newspapers are the play-things and tools of mining
boomsters without knowing it.
* * * *
The opening of Perth mint has been put back
to July 13 ; and it is not likely that it will be ready then.
The original idea was to provide for an output of a million
sovs. a year ; but now, as nothing short of three millions
will do, the plans have been altered, and hence the delay.
* * * *
Perth, W.A., municipal assessment in ’97 was
£205,565. For ’9B, it is £296,207. An eighteenpenny rate
is levied.
• • * *
L. M. Cullen, sec. of Kalgoorlie Chamoer of
Mines, which issues some excellent local statistics, tells a
Perth interviewer that Westralian customs-returns of gold-
production ought to be sniffed at. As to parcels of ore
shipped, “ the agents have a very convenient way of pass-
ing entries. They say, perhaps, ‘ Here are 200 tons of ore.
We will put it down as worth 3oz per ton.’ The yield
may, however, not bear out that estimate.” Also, he
warns the public not to expect Kalgoorlie outputs to go
on increasing. “ The mines are now working to the limits
of their crushing power. It is an open secret, too, that
our free-melting ores are not as plentiful as they used to
be.”
• * * *
Boulder township—Boulder City it loves to call
itself—can’t make much headway against its rival, Kal-
goorlie, where vested interests lie. For months it has
struggled to get a bank, and at last, in desperation, begged
Govt, to give a site to any institution which would come
along. But the Govt, wasn’t taking any of that.

15,2180 z. were got from 8643 tons of Great Eastern stone.
But out there yields don’t give much indication yet of the

The holders of more solid wealth have been
arrested at Albany, Westralia, than at any other town
twice its size in Australia. Zeb. Lane was “had” there
on a civil claim ; and now Big Hill Brookman has had his
turn. The latter event is the culmination of the row
between the brothers; James issued a debtor’s warrant
claiming £14,206 for non-delivery of mining shares. A
beak attended at Big Bill’s hotel, took his cheque for
£lO,OOO and his bonds for twice £14,206, and let the
“ absconder,” who is in really bad hea'th, pass on per
Barbarossa.
• * * *
In Victoria, mining has come to be regarded by
thousands of people, in and out of town, as an industry
which can be conducted as honestly and as safely as
cabbage-growing. In Wood’s Point district there are
working miners who could draw you a cheque for a thou-
sand, and in some cases have plenty to keep themselves,
wives, and families for r.he rest of their days, without the
necessity of doing a day’s graft. The mining fever born
in the fifties and sixties is as fierce now as ever, and a day
above-ground to any of the veterans is a time of purga-
tory. They will tell you that the old hunger and craving
is still there—the yearning to strike another rich patch
(even for somebody else) lives and thrives, and success
would give them all the heaven they want to experience
either in this world or the next. The other day an old
Vic. miner, who found himself in possession of a likely
show, was willing to sell three-fourths of it to a co. who
would provide machinery and capital—“ all the shares,
including his own, to be made contributing.” Where,
outside Victoria, do you ever hear of such a thing ?
* * * *
The daughters of a deceased official lately called
a meeting of his creditors, and offered 10s. in the £, which
was accepted. Then they put in a claim of £l7O against
the estate for board and lodging for their father since
1893, which claim the rest of the creditors had to admit.

Adelaide was electrified, last week, by the
news of the engagement of Chief Justice Way.
oammy, ’ who is well over 50 and a bachelor,
was utterly given up years ago by Adelaide
maidens, and now (at Easter) marries Mrs. Blue,
widow of a once well-known country medico. The
U.d. is very wealthy, and has a magnificent home
at -North Adelaide, and local girls can’t conceal
c^a ff rin that a provincial widow with four
children and unknown in city society should at
one bound ascend to the second highest social
position in the land, with the prospect of acting
as mistress of Government House during an in-
terregnum !
• * » *
Curious that the Bible Christians—the
Methodist denomination of which Chief Justice
Wa T the leading lay light in South Australia,
and which not only has congregations all over the
province but also runs one of the largest and most
progressive colleges—has no footing whatever in
jVS. Males. The only two B.C. “chapels” in
the latter province are at Broken Hill and another
border town. For the denomination’s present
president in South Australia it has a layman,
Ashton, a lawyer of the firm from which Judge
Boucaut came.
* * * *
Gov. Buxton has made himself immensely
popular in Adelaide by growing a moustache. A
great many of the irreverent remarks formerly
made about his Ex. were provoked by his aggra-
vatingly forlorn face. The immense area of
shaven upper lip, and the inexpressible aspect of
sadness about the Buxton countenance, led to
the impression that he was merely a Worm
within the domestic circle. But, under new condi-
tions, his XXX looks more aggressive, and the
public will not be surprised now to see the Gover-
nor punting at the races, winking at Tottie
behind the bar, and getting home late o’ nights.
A Sydney D.T. correspondent suggests
“ Cromwell ” as the name of the Federal capital.
Why not “Bartown,” a treble-barrelled compli-
ment (1) to the Convention leader, (2) to the
large number of legal constitution-framers, (3) to
the whisky-interest.
* * * *
Gov. Brassey’s departure for Europe, so
far from eclipsing the gaiety of Melb. society, is
marked by all the Naice people with a sigh of
relief. The heavy foot of Uncle Tom is oppressive
in the gilded halls of Toorakia; while the light
and airy, mincing gait of the gay and giddy bald-
head who takes his place for the time-being, is
suggestive of better fun than lectures, sermons,
readings of poetry, and such other improving
diversions as the old-man-of-the-sea offered his
guests. Madden and his handsome wife will be a
welcome change. Mr. Madden does not altogether
make an ass of himself. He simply poses —smiles,
bows with a fine air, says pretty things to the.
innocent virgins, and whispers wicked jokes to
their seasoned mammas, and, as he scoops in a
good slab of Gov. Brassey’s screw, he can make
things hum.
* * * *
Gov. Brassey’s departure was the “sick-
est ” thing in the way of loyalty that Melb. has
seen for some time. A fair crowd rolled up in the
streets, but not a cheer was heard, and when at
last, on Port Melb. pier, one grey-headed enthu-
siast lifted a black belltopper and called “Three
cheers for Lord Brassey,” his voice was the only
one heard to respond. Just at the moment, too,
a fireman carrying a couple of parcels fell over
the edge. All popular interest was instantly
transferred to the man in the water, and he was
ultimately dragged out hanging on to his parcels
with a tenacity which made late arrivals imagine
he was the departing Governor himself.
* * * *
“ A Loyal Citizen ” to The Bulletin :
Couldn’t some law he passed or embodied in the
Federal Constitution Bill, or somewhere, to prevent
people from naming their pet poodles after popular
Governors? Next to -me resides a blue-eyed png-doc
named Brassey, whose habits, taken in association with
his name, lead to results embarrassing to my loyalty. Is
it pleasant, for instance, to hear a young lady say, “ O,
mamma, Brassey is simply full of fleas ! ” just as if she
had found the item in a society paper? Then we get,
“O, Brassey, Brassey, you naughty dog!” and,
“ Mamma, Brassey’s following a lady down the street!”
and, “ Brassey, you mean old thing, why don’t you let
the other dogs have a share of your meat?” I’m* think-
ing of buying a mastiff, whom I shall call Higinbotham,
and sool on to pug-dog Brassey with incitations to chew
the little beast up.

How to find out if a dog is registered.
Inspector hits the dog with his stick. “ How dare
you strike my dog, sir ! ” “Oh, is it your dog?
I am the inspector, and just want to know if he’s
registered.”

The affinity
between cancer
in man and can-
cer in cattle is
again exercising
the mindsof folks
up North. Can-
cer is undoubt-
edly on the in-
crease, and the dirty, uninspected slaughter-house
is, unfortunately, not on the decrease. The
trouble lies in the number of slaughter-houses.
The meat only undergoes a sham inspection at
best. One abattoir, fully manned, with proper
and minute inspection, would easily suffice for
most Australian towns—and the private slaughter-
house of the penny-farthing butcher, with all its
filthy abominations, should be swept out of exist-
ence.

Bananaland storms are responsible for a
queer variety of catastrophes. The disturbance
“ Eline ” (so Mackay papers report) lifted the local
constable off his feet, banged him down on the
pa vement with fearful emphasis, and left him with
a broken ankle. Reflect on the difficulty of lifting
the average trap an inch off his No. nineteens and
you have some idea of the vigor of Eline.

The Dean of Sydney wasn’t wise in his
generation in expecting N. S. W. Agricultural
Society to forego its Good Friday arrangements
out of respect for the feelings of those who regard
the day as sacred. For in a “Christian” com-
munity, more than any other, when sentiment
conflicts with dollars —sentiment goes down every
time!
A very smellful and unprintable case, of

N.S.W. Supreme Court will shortly have
before it a novel divorce case—between a Chinese
and his white wife.
* • * •
James Kidman, baker and general feed-
merchant, of Oxford-street, Sydney, has again
got all the contracts for feed in connection with
the N.S.W. Easter Encampment. James is now
in himself the whole commissariat of the noble
N.S.W. army, and as an army without a commis-
sariat is useless, presumably the best move on the
part of an enemy would be to lay siege on the big
shops in Oxford and George-streets, make Quarter-
master Kidman a prisoner of war, and prevent his
carts from dispensing feed to our gallant de-
fenders. The Bulletin hopes it is not giving
the enemy any information which should be kept
dark, but it trusts that this will be the means of
stirring up the authorities to at once arrange for
the defence of Mr. Commissariat Kidman. The
stoppage of his bread-carts would be a national
disaster.

* • • *
The people of Cairns have at last risen
in wrath and demanded the removal of its
Kanaka leper. Five months ago, when this
spptted islander was first diagnosed, the authori-
ties ordered him to be isolated in the yard behind
the police-station, pending further consideration.
Then they forgot him, and left him there. The
sun shone on the leper, and the rain fell on him,
and the germs blew off him into the town, and ti e
passing children poked sticks at him through the
convenient open fence, and he shook his swollen
hands at them through the fence in execration by
way of response. Also the dogs and the cats
crawled through to see how he was getting on,
and sometimes the lone wretch howled dismally
at night and disturbed Cairns in its pleasant dal-
liance with the brown woman, and sometimes he
climbed up and put his enlarged head over the
fence by day, and shocked the piebald town by
his appearance. And now Cairns has suddenly
risen up as one man, and come cut of the house of
the brown woman, and intimated to the Govern
ment that it won’t have its leper -ny longer.

* * • •
Brisbane police-court presents, at times, a
queer variety of strange sounds and strange
spectacles. The other day, “ Papa ” Pinnock,
P.M., waxed furious at the presence of a mongrel
cur in court, inasmuch as the “ human stench was
bad enough ” without any addition of canine con-
diments. Shortly afterwards a young man was
charged with loitering in Queen-street with intent
to pilfer, found guilty on evidence of two con-
stables, and sent up for six months. Five minutes
after sentence was delivered, two respectable
citizens of good standing arrived and deposed
that they were standing beside prisoner at the
time referred to, and that his demeanor was per-
fectly correct and innocent. Whereupon the
amiable P.M. remarked that if they had come five
minutes earlier the prisoner wouldn’t have got six
months at all—leaving one to infer that aforesaid
citizens had got him six months by their un-
punctuality. However, prisoner has got his little
term until the Home Secretary chooses to inter-
vene—when, it is presumed, those two constables
will get the promotion their zeal deserves.

ENGAGED.
My prince has come, and all my dreams of yore—
Splendors of crown and sceptre, gems galore—
Die as dreams should beneath love’s first caress,
To rise again pure of all sordidness
In new-found spirit-realms scarce named before.
I clear mine eyes, full eager to explore
This new-found kingdom ; there through his soul’s door
To find, not gemless, crownless, sceptreless—
My prince has come.
Now, love-enthroned, I’m strong for evermore—
A princess truly, whom the fates smile o’er;
For when plebeian days’ pale ghosts oppress !
And I need more strength, more than I possess,
Answers my heart, down-thrilling to its core—
“ My prince has Come ! ”
M. A. R.

The recent initiation of the Sydney Kosher
Butchering Association suggests that it wouldn’t
be a bad thing for the alleged Christians to follow
suit with something upon similar precautionary
lines—or, better still, appoint Moses as their
butcher. The private slaughtering practices of
Sydney are still as the pestilence which walketh
in darkness.

Melbourne butchers are now compelled to
wrap up meat in new and clean paper—the use of
newspapers, &c., is prohibited under stiff penalty.
* * * *
“ Ugh ! ” to The Bulletin :
Re unpleasant practices of Sydney food-purveyors.
Walking into the city from an eastern suburb, I passed
one of those butchers’ shops whose show of meat covers
the outside of the premises from pavement upwards. A
small and mangy dog-, after sundry samplings, made off
with a choice morsel, but not fast enough to escape a
well-placed kick from the butcher, who, upon recovering
the meat, coolly replaced it in its former position to await
purchase. Another time, returning from town about
11.30 p.m., I passed a ham-and-beef shop where the lady
in charge was undressing the window for the night. Pigs’
trotters were retiring as I passed, and were each subjected
to an intimate nasal examination before being passed in.
Ugh!
* ♦ * *
The history of a cablegram. It came from
London, reading: “Supposed tombs of the god-
kings Osiris Set Horus found Necropolis Abydos ”
—which is a plain statement to anyone with a
smattering of Egyptian mythology, or who will
spend two minutes on “Egypt” in a cyclopedia.
Adelaide Advertiser had it: “ The supposed
tombs of the god-kings Osiris, Sethan and
Horus are reported to have been found in a
necropolis in the ancient city of Abydos”
—quite correct. The Age rendered it: “ Arch-
aeological investigation in the necropolis of
Abydos, the cemetery of ancient kings in
Upper Egypt, has resulted in the discovery of
what is believed to be the tomb of the Osiris
dynasty, who were subsequently deified and wor-
shipped under the symbolical sign of a bull. A
set of bull’s horns ivas found in the tomb which had
en opened. ” Sydney Daily Telegraph launched
the news that “ Some tombs had been unearthed,
and they are supposed to be those of the god-
kings Osiris (the god of Hades, Leti, and Horus,
the last of the god-kings).” S.M. Herald :
“ Among the more important discoveries are what
are .believed to be the tombs of the god-kings
Osiris, Set I. and Horus (the last of the god-
kings).” The mutilations look as if some of the
night-editors had sneaked home and left the late-
cable translations to the night-watchman.

A coming libel-case will finally determine
whether, under existing N.S.W. law, a newspaper
is privileged to report the preliminary (police-
court) proceedings in a criminal case.

Button y. Button, in Sydney Divorce-
court last week, presented some of the usual casual
features. Hubby, she said, deserted her several
years ago, and she had since only sighted him
once at Surry Hills. It was in the street, but he
apparently drifted away before petitioner had
time to overtake him. She told the court she
couldn’t positively locate him now, but had
heard that one Button held a billet at a Botany
bootshop who might be the one in question. So
she journeyed to that dismal suburb, and dis-
covered that it was really her missing Button,
who told her he was too tired, or something, to
appear on the suit. She related this to the Judge
on her return, and was duly un-Buttoned, so to
speak. His Honor wanted to give her costs, but
she didn’t wish any loophole left for troubling
Button again, and paid them herself.
* * * *
A recent sensation is the alleged elope-
ment of a Southern R.C. priest with a hospital-
nurse.
St. Kilda (Melb.) has been badly shocked
in the night of late by a bare-footed marauder who
breaks into houses and looks at ladies in bed. No
one knows how he gets in, but his luck in finding
an entrance is extraordinary. When he has got
in he is quite harmless ; all he does is to strike a
match, and look thoughtfully at the lady in bed,
and then bolt through the window. It is generally
supposed that he is looking for somebody, but why
he looks for her in this remarkable way is a
mystery. Several times he has been chased from
10 yards to If miles, but with no satisfactory
results. He has been known to break into three
houses in one night, and in each case he woke the
ladies of the family as they reposed in their curl-
papers, or with their independent hair hung on a
nail above the dressing-table, and made a hasty
inspection of them, and left. Altogether the bare-
footed man of St. Kilda is a rather amusing cuss
in his way.
* * * *
The most blasphemous utterance this paper
has struck for some time is a verse from Melb.
University-students’ anthem —
And now God bless our land,
Give the ’Varsity Council prudence,
And bless the noblest work on earth,
The Melbourne ’Varsity students !
Which recalls the yell of a grim, grey Scotch
person in the pit at Melb. Alexandra one night
when the students were acting the giddy ox. “Ay,
God made ye,” screamed the fierce old man, “ but
the Devil knows why ! ”

Ah, now, my dear desaver,
Your swate lips Oi’ll be missing ;
Though kissing goes by favour,
Yet faver comes by kissing.
So farewell to my darlint,
For till ye’re safe Oi’ll lave ye ;
And if ye live Oi’ll have ye ;
If ye die, the saints rasave ye !

London Sun, in a hostile critique of
“Deeds that Won the Empire,” says that “the
book spoils a good title.”
The inevitable Brian Boru had, of course,
to be dragged into Cardinal Moran’s recent
address to the military at St. Mary’s, Sydney.
“ In his last victory he held aloft the cross, and in
that sign he conquered.” Not considered neces-
sary to mention that while Brian the Bore was
prostrate before tike same cross afterwards the
heathen promptly knocked him on the sainted
head.
The stranding in the Red Sea of that great
show-ship, the P. and O. boat China was somehow
felt by everyone in Sydney as a personal calamity.
Should efforts to float the big vessel fail, the
company will lose at least .£300,000. Much sym-
pathy is felt for Captain De Horne, who, after a
faultless and fortunate career of so many years,
will no doubt be unceremoniously shunted, for the
laws of the P. and O. are like unto those of the
Medes and Persians ; no man who from any cause
once hits ground ever again takes command of a
P. O. liner.
On Monday the Atacama case shuffled
along another stage before N.S. W. Marine Board.
Since its last meeting, the Board has apparently
looked up its Constitution Act, and discovered
that it is the Board’s business to subpoena wit-
nesses who have material information to give;
for, without any report as to whether the wit-
nesses asked for by the Crown had consented to
turn up or not, they announced that subpoenas
would be issued. Then the case was adjourned
for yet another week.
The prospectus of the Tasmania Gold-
mining Extension, Beaconsfield, appears in our
advertising columns 'to-day. The leases, com-
prising 106 acres, are an amalgamation of the East
Tasmania Company and the Consols properties,
as recommended by Mr. H. H. Sehlapp, and cover
the whole of the area on the eastern end of the
Tasmania gold mine.*
The “Smokers’ Retreat,” conducted by
Miss Lilia Whitmore at 132 Pitt-street, Sydney,
aims at being the highest-class tobacco and cigar
establishment and the most comfortable lounge
and reading-room in Australia. If it doesn’t quite
get there, it at least gets near enough to it to
satisfy the most fastidious mortal.*

A WOMAN’S LETTER.
T . Sydney, March 29, 1898.
My Dear Myee—
If Festina Lente means make haste Lent, I
aont wonder they used it so often in ancient
history. The slowness of things in general was
not enlivened much by Rosehill races last Satur-
day, or by the bicycle sports the same afternoon.
At Kosemll bookies’ babies swarmed all over the
grand-stand and made hay of the lawn while
their mothers and grandmothers punted with the
superior nonchalance that belongs by right to
bookies females. And the dressing struck a
horse-note, high up in the treble, where chroma-
tics yell in gay greens, and saffrons are repre-
sented. Crow s-feet and attenuated noses are the
divine inheritance of women who bet, so I may be
mistaken in guessing that the lady was 97 who
u f mumbled to me in the platform crush,
bix seconds to-day.” And the little girl in a
sunbonnet who grasped the tragedy and cried
Go on . before I realised that the events of the
day were six, had seen 12 fleeting years according
to her sunbonnet, and 30 racing ones by the lines
about her mouth. The joy of the stand was the
two-year-old who toddled along at a leading poli-
tician’s side lisping “ two to one on ’tretch ” with
a tiny purse in her tinier fingers ; and the moral
reflections these things let loose would be as out
of place at Lent as one man’s gout when his friend
has sciatica.
As for the bicycle sports, they were largely
populated but you know the sort of populace I
m ®^ n - Lilia M’Carthy was there with her own
and Alaud Jefferies’ share of observation, and
opinions differed fiercely. Or, perhaps I ought to
Sa j t • auc * Jefferies was there with her own
and Lilia M'Carthy’s share of observation. Any-
way, opinion differed fiercely.
One day last week I went to see the statuette
of Parkes with which Mr. Nelson Illingworth—
all told has made his latest bid for fame, and,
by some Hibernian freak, the likeness is more like
Parkes than Parkes was like himself. The old
man eloquent is cast in a erimson-kinship atti-
tude, one hand aloft, &c., and the crimson thread
itself is bound about the statue’s base. I’m not
taking much in the way of statues just now—
they don’t move off in a hurry—but this one gives
the most absolutely-unexaggerated likeness on
record of Parkes, with the shadow of failure heavy
on his poor old eyes. Tom Roberts put away his
pictures, and lent his studio all the week to tea
and statue-gazing, and many came and went away
delighted. J
But did you see that a plaster-cast of Reid and
eyeglass was sold in the market-place last week
for threepence ! Take away the threepenny eye-
glass, and another great man has gone for nothing.
Thnppence isn’t much of an inducement for any
man to be great, even under the most promising
circumstances, but when he has to spend the
whole of that honorarium on an eyeglass, it’s tin.e
greatness was fixed at a market-value. Well, as
things are at present, it seems unkind to say that
Nelson Illingworth may be a great man some
day. _ But what I am going to remark may have a
happier application when times mend, or eye-
glasses go out of date. For the thousandth time
in the history of this island-continent, an art-man
observed the other day, ** If I could only get away
to London ! ” But, probably for the first time in
the history of the world, he finished up his
•sentence with, “ I don’t know anything about my
art, and I want to go home to learn.'' That man
was Nelson Illingworth, whose name may not be
too big for him some day.
From statuary to Addie Conyers is not suen a
long leap as it seems, but Jack the Giant-Killer,
at the Tivoli, is a less killing young man than the
Theatre Royal Jack used to be. Addie Conyers
filled the buff suit with a shapeliness that Edie
Hay ter, or any other leading-lady I remember, falls
short of. Addie Conyers’ symmetry could hardly
be improved on ; line upon line and precept upon
precept have induced her proportions into the
perfection of up-to-date grace, and the second
Jack in buff is too reminiscent of Jack the First,
whose buff suit haunts me still. Silly Billy makes
you as young again as ever, and I thought that
you might like to know that the miniature lady
and gentleman at the right-hand corner of the
stage are sisters. The brightest spot of color in
the legend is Bella of The Cry, in a scarlet satin
redingote that howls appropriately.
Raiment and the gauds of trimmings are not
always what they seem. A deplorable instance
had its origin in a Sydney mansion, the other day,
where paterfamilias is a merchant prince and
“ self-made gentleman,” and the mistress is the
only daughter of an only son whose father boiled
soap in the good old gilt-fat days. One wet even-
ing a feminine friend, who was dining with the
hostess in the absence of the gilded host, was kept
there all night by bad weather, and slept the night
with the Dives’ family. Bed-time came, and she
wanted to do her hair. The Dives’ dressing-table
was strewn with silver-backed brushes in silver
cases, and she asked where she would find a comb.
From the drawer of her priceless dressing-table
Madame produced a remnant of the middle ages
in the shape of a worn and weary comb, with four
teeth and half a backbone. This she handed to
her friend without a blush, and did her own hair
with it afterwards. More tragic difficulties oc-
curred when the visitor wanted to wash her
hands. There was no soap in the Doulton soap-
dishes, no towels on the rosewood horse, and the
silver toilet jugs were dry with an old, .old
drought. And the hostess remarked in querulous
tones : “ When I want to wash I always go down
to the bathroom.” Just as Mr. and Mrs. Retail
Jones wash their faces at the kitchen-tap.
It is circulated at Govt. House that the million-
aire, Miss Walker, is engaged to Archbishop Smith.
If that’s so, there ought to be a good time ahead
for the cabbies.
The women’s penny-papers have struck oil at
last in a series of articles that provide an adroit
excuse for interviewing the whole of the human
race > if time lasts long enough. Home Notes is
the happy journal that has been hit with the idea
ofwriting up the. lives of “ Men Whom Women
Admire,” beginning with Pinero, and ending
with—— What? The ticket admits masculine
•c'at ,n i —f rom Tummy Wales to Quong Tart.
Men Who Admire Men ” might slow the series
down a little,. but “Women Who Admire
Women ” is positively the only chance of killing
it. It is not probable that the proprietors will
ever come to the end of their first lot, but if they
once get into the clutches of “ Men Who Admire
Women ” and “ Women Whom Men Admire,” the
millennium itself won’t stop ’em, and interviewing
will die a natural death next cycle.
Interviewing was responsible for the slip of a
person in high places the other day—a venerable
’ a dy journalist, who was seeking copy about one
of the Powers That Be, received a note from
an aide, inviting her to meet him at the
Power-house .at 11 p.m. next day, when he would
arrange the interview. Her age alone prevented
her from writing to ask him to make it midnight.
Four compositions by Louise Clarke (Glen and
Co.) might easily have been condensed into one.
Ihe graceful. dance that languishes under the
phenomenal title of “ Two Wooden Models and
Two Little Dogs ” is an attempt to bring the
Battle of Prague up to date. But after the open-
ing bars have inquired, according to printed direc-
tions, Hist, hist, who goes there?” the com-
poser quailed before her terrible task, and forgot
to mention when the models sounded the call to
arms, or when the dogs barked, or the guns went
off, and left us to believe that the Cries of the
Wounded will be heard as long as the piece is
played. Of the three songs by the same writer,
two ■ A Pagan’s Prayer ” and “A Laplander’s
hong —have such a similarity of motif that I
don t know now whether I’m a Lap. or a Pagan.
The Prayer is dedicated to Mrs. Grant Allen,
whose husband’s words are used by kind permis-
sion, but it isn’t Pagans who pray so
monotonously. The “ Shining Path,” with the
composer’s own words, is the most pleasing of
the bunch, although it doesn’t differ remarkably
from other Shining Paths and Golden Shores. Its
pathetic prettiness would suit a mezzo with a
limited range. Edward Parlove’s “Victoria
Polka ” opens brightly enough with less tin-tinni-
ness than the end-of-the-century polka, but the
trio relapses into a commonplace indistinctness
that relegates it to the shelf of after-supper-extras.
The world is very good-natured after supper.
Wesleyan ministers, who are noted chiefly (as
they say in geography books) for the smallness of
their incomes and the largeness of their families,
have fallen on such a bad time lately that “a
Tj system of celibacy ” is under serious con-
sideration. . The average Methodist clergyman, as
soon as lie is out of his Articles, and ceases to be a
Divinity Student, is sent up country to a parson-
age where the house and cow are not by any
means the only articles of furniture. Horsehair
suites and a prehistoric harmonium are found in
every well-conducted parsonage drawing-room,
and over every front bedroom mantelpiece an
engraving of the “ Day of Wrath ” hangs side-by-
side with a photograph of parsons at the first
Wesleyan Conference. (If the parson’s wife
sees any humor in the juxtaposition of the
two, she. doesn’t let on.) The night of arrival at
a new circuit, the chapel-folk come in and lay
the table for the parson and his wife, and cold
corned-beef and pickles flake the board, and all
goes merry as a marriage-bell. But, next day, the
ministers wife’s bonnet is under consideration,
and, if she wears roses in it, or an aigrette, or even
a paste brooch at a shilling a dozen retail, her hus-
band will have to work all he knows for every
penny that comes in, and for innumerable pennies
that never come, and, at the end of his three
years, he and his wife will retire with more years
than three added to their calendar. But if the
bonnet’s a brown-black hat, where brown-black
strings take the place of roses, a goose may be
given at Xmas, and the stipend will probably
come in without more than one appeal weekly.
As one in the know, I may here say that par-
sons’ wives must have been misbehaving them-
selves of late years, and dressing in cotton-backed
silks or satinette, and wearing light kid-gloves
and perhaps a bracelet or two, and having their
bonnets trimmed at Madame Chignonerie’s instead
of entrusting them to the tender mercies of the
draper’s lady who leads the choir three notes
away from the.right one. This would account for
the dismal falling-off of the Methodist ministers’
stipends, some of which now average =BlO a year.
Congregations kick against contributing to the
keep of a- parson’s ladv who wears trimmed bon-
nets and “ eddicates.”' But the threat of celibacy
will bring things round again. The feminine ele-
ment of the congregation will attend to that, for
it is quite unlikely they will tolerate celibacy in a
church whose jenncsse dorie is wholly represented
by its young unmarried parsons.
. What a pity to see clever Elsie Hall burdened
with hideous spectacles ! Except for them, she was
very pretty the other night. As she satgracefnllyat
the piano in her quaint, short-waisted white frock,
she looked like some dainty old-world maiden
dreaming at her spin net. Her touch is really
exquisite, like silver and pearls, elves, dewdrops
and so on.
The Brasseys last weeks in Melbourne were
saddened by a certain concert song. An epidemic
of ‘Simon the Cellarer’ broke out, and sassiety
soon learned that the tuneful reference to Simon’s
leading feature, which shows ‘ how oft the black-
jack to his lips doth go,’ was highly distasteful to
the Govmentouse party. Lady Brassey thought
it most vulgar and out-of-place. So did Lord
Neville. The Governor didn’t openly protest, but
a careful observer could see, from his manner of
using a handkerchief when the objectionable words
were about to fall due, that his Excellency had no
sympathy with the vocalist. At the Elsie Hall
concert, when Signor Rebottaro—he fancies him-
self as 9' humourist gave ( Simon 9 as an encore,
the whisper went round that Lady Brassey
wouldn’t have come if the ‘dreadful song’ had
been programmed.
Miss Sylvia Madden’s pleasant-faced intended
captive is of the attenuated, lengthy order—in a
word, lanky and somewhat crane-like. He has the
marked clean-shaved visage usually associated
with the stage, and plentiful, not to say bushy,
eyebrows. The wedding is to take place while pa
is Acting-Ex with swell doings at Guvment House,
and doubtless an array of presents will adorn the
semi-official function.
The Mayoral farewell to His Ex. came quite
up to the M'Eacharn reputation for doing things
m style. The Town Hail, which, during the pre-
sent regime, has. been brushed and spruced-up
generally, and crimson-carpeted on an extensive
scale as to corridors, was made glorious for the
occasion. To hide the sticky floor from view there
were many square yards of matting, and some
dozens of rugs whereon tables and chairs were
planted d la drawing-room, with an excrescence in
the shape of a platform along one side. The
Mayor and Mayoress pump-handled the guests as
they arrived, first-comers sprinting for the cosiest
seats and holding a mortgage on them all the
evening. The vice-regal party arrived about three-
quarters of an hour after invited time, as usual.
The Mayoress bouquetted Lady 8., and when the
civic couple led the exalted ones to the reserved
furniture the band started to drown conversation
Ahce Coy and Maggie Stirling chortled, and
Attilio Buzzi gave his parting song, Gounod’s
JNazareth. A cmematographe show burst forth
at intervals up on the portico balcony, inter-
spersed by magic-lantern views of a decidedly
sick and childish order. The funniest of the
former was Ranji practising the machinery
worked at express speed and caused the Indian to
hit in time, at the rate of about one swipe per
second. In another, ‘Ranji and Hayward bat-
ting, trie rapidity of the hits and the marvellous
spruits between wickets were most sensational,
lhe women had sported best bib and tucker and
dragged their hair up on top irrespective of
whether it was becoming or not, and that, together
with the return of barbaric ear-rings, added quite
10 years to the appearance of some of the Naicest.
If some folks, too, would but glance at the back
view they would not adopt the mode , for it reveals
the badly-shaped head and seems to accent all
the shocking bumps of which phrenologists prate.
Lady B. aired yellow satin with splotches of black
lace; the Mayoress was in grey satin and plenty
or lace. A jovial councillor’s wife, who goes in
for color, paralysed the crowd in a fixment of
bright pink with green. The ex-Mayoress affected
green touched with red. Mrs. S. M'Culloch wore
a daring combination of heliotrope and pale blue :
golden-haired Mrs. Percy Chirnside sported cream
satin with black sleeves and plenty of bullion
touching up the body-parts. One councillor’s
wife aired quite a decent little fortune on her
person in the form of lace.
“Mayor M'Eacharn is desperately ambitious
for his olive branches. At present, the juvenile
trio are en route for England, the boy consigned
to Harrow, his sisters to school, preparatory to
Girton later on. When the girls were fragile,
flaxen-haired mites barely out of the nursery they
took lessons in Latin and singing from male
experts, though subsequently educational pressure
was eased off temporarily—perhaps because of the
death of the then son-and heir from some childish
brain-ailment.

“ Peachey’s sufferings on this score call up
memories of a fat, old lady so anxious to be
thought a great traveller that she always ordered
curries, mulligatawney, chutney and Chow-Chow
for dinner, when company was expected, and
wore a sort of Turkish turban with a trailing pea-
cock s feather. At table, as sure as ever the hostess
told her ad ventures by sea and land with explana-
toryhead-and-arm-wavings, that beastly feather-
tip dipped into the most peppery plat' available
preparatory to ‘ wiping the eye ’ of some wretched
guest later on. In later scenes, Miss Leith
carries off the huge checks of her mountaineering
Buit less high-handedly than did swagger Juliet
Wray. And, as heretofore, she apparently lives
moves and has her stage being in the trammels of
immaculate gloves.
