“ Publisher Geo. Robertson, deceased last week,
began his Melbourne career as assistant in Pullar’s
book-shop, which stood on the site of what is now
the old Exchange, in Coll ins-street. The next
year he spt up for himself, laying the foundation
of his afterwards mammoth business in a little
shop in Russell-street. David Blair was his first
customer. His first wife was a beautiful Miss
Baxter; his second marriage was with a widow
with two children. Altogether the combined
families under his roof numbered twelve, and
G.R. used to introduce the three groups in batches
to visitors: ‘ These are mine, these are hers, these
are ours.’
_ “ Now that the Conventionalities are over,
Melbourne comments freely on the studied niggard-
liness of the departed Gov. Brassey in connection
therewith. His cheap garden-party and his ball
exhausted his hospitality. He did not even come
up to the level of Gov. Buxton during the Adelaide
Convention. Apparently, Uncle Tom has been
saving up to pay his expenses to England. It is
a pity that he didn’t go before, for Chief Justice
Madden as acting-Governor would have done the
thing handsomely.
“ When the Brassey people go to bazaars and
win raffles, at which, by the way, they are more
fortunate than in backing horses, a direction is
invariably given for the article to be wrapped up
in brown paper and taken to the carriage. The
real vice-regal attitude in such matters is, of
course, to airily disown the prize, and let it be
sold for the benefit of the show. But, then, the
Brassey crowd conducts the Governor - business
without any demned sentiment.

Ages of Australian Governors—Buxton 65,
Brassey 62, Gormanston 61, Hampden 57, Smith
54, Ranfurly 42, Lamington 38.
That well-known boating enthusiast, Father
Gartlan, S.J., of Riverview College, Sydney, is a
nephew of the present Lord Chief Justice of
England.
Arthur Hunter Palmer is the third Presi-
dent of Queensland Leg. Council who has died in
office. O’Connell and Bell died suddenly. The
first President, Dr. Nicholson, still survives.
A Condobolin (N.S.W.) witness in a law
case, last week, informed the court that his general
consumption of ardent spirits reached 50 drinks a
day, of which 20 were taken before breakfast.
FELICITOUS QUOTATIONS.
“ Too much of a good thing. ”
Stoddart stated, on the eve of leaving
Adelaide, that Australia will see him no more.
He marries Miss Phillipson, sister of the wicket-
keeper of his previous team, on his arrival in Eng-
land.
Mr. Malaprop has a seat on a Sydney
suburban Council. On a recent trip to Glasgow
he saw a garbage-crematorium worked by dyna-
mos—and this he described as a “creamery worked
by dynamite.”
The marvellously virile Gladstone, it would
appear, is now dying fast. He is afflicted with
severe neuralgia—variously said to proceed from
facial cancer and from necrosis (mortification) of
the nasal bone.
A couple or three years ago every Governor
in Australia was a G.C.M.G.—Duff, Norman,
Kintore, Hopetoun and W. Robinson. Now not
one is a G.C.M.G.—Hampden, Brassey, Laming-
ton, Buxton, or G. Smith.
Dan O’Connor has been lecturing on
“ Great Men Whom I Have Met.” “ Mr. O’Con-
nor talked principally of our own Australian great
men.” Why, certainly. “Great men whom I
have met in (the) Australia.”
The claim made for the late Chief-Justice
Dobson, of Tasmania, in re appeals to the Privy
Council, is wholly justified in the case of the late
N.S.W. Justice Faucett. No decision of Faucett’s
was ever over-ruled by the P.C.

man took a woman from her husband and married
her. Now he is trying to divorce her, and she
wants <£looo a year alimony pending the suit,
alleging that his income is <£14,000 a year.
A certain dark-haired, pale-faced mining-
market operator who did pretty big things in Aus-
tralia and has since made a European reputation,
is, it appears, a quarter-caste Chinese, which
accounts for a lot of things. Here he was always
set down as having far-back Red Indian blood.
The son of a popular Australian comedian
married against his father’s will, but boldly took
his new wife to the paternal door and knocked.
Dad opened it. Son: “ We’ve come.” Dad
(shouting upstairs to mother): “ Mary, they've
come. And now you can go away again.” (Slam-
ming the door).
Melb. Age on Liord Brassey: “At both
ends of the world the Victorian Governor is
recognised as an expert authority on the naval
affairs of the world.” This statement may be
disputed, but nobody can deny that his steam
yacht knows enough to come in out of the wet as
often as possible.
Lancet: “The operation just undergone
by Premier Turner is, doubtless, necessitated by a
lifetime of inattention to the laws of hygiene.
From almost the beginning of his Premiership, his
health has been bad, physicking constant, pain
chronic. Yet I suppose men adjudge the game
worth the candle.”
Ex.-Vic. Premier Jimmy Munro, who dis-
appeared beneath the waters of Oblivion at the
bursting of the Boom, is slowly emerging once
more. The top of his head is, so to speak, visible
already. His auctioneering business is improving
greatly, and he is even beginning to take an in-
terest in Federation.
Old Amos Burr, of M.L., who recently
cameto serious grief over an allegednative land pur-
chase, is another evidence of how a promising dodge
may be wrecked by want of attention to small
details. Burr produced an aged, faded receipt in
the Maori language, showing that he had bought
and paid for the land in question in 1872. And
the receipt on the stamp belonged to an issue that
was first printed in 1882. It was a beautiful
receipt in every other respect.
George Higinbofcliam’s punctilious con-
scientiousness proved a great drawback to him as
a journalist. When Argus editor in the Fifties
he so carefully read the proofs and checked the
correctness of whatever was printed that publica-
tion of the paper was often delayed till six and
seven o’clock in the morning.
John Norton has publicly registered an
oa,th that when he enters Parliament he will deal
with Mr. (N.S.W.) District Court Judge Docker.
A full catalogue of Judge Docker’s judicial
vagaries during the last 12 years—beginning, say,
with his alleged comparison of a certain juryman
to Judas Iscariot—would make interesting read-
ing.
The death of James Payn, the novelist, at
the age of 68, is cabled. He lived a queer life,
and always prided himself on never walking more
than a few yards if he could possibly help it.
Apropos, an Oscar Wilde story. “Do you never
take exercise ? ” someone asked Wilde. “Oh,
yes,” replied the fat aesthete, “I sometimes talk
a little.”
The text selected by a certain holy S.A.
local-preaching defaulter for the Sunday before
his disappearance was Psalm lv., 6 —“ And I said,
Oh that I had wings like a dove, for then would
I fly away and be at rest. Lo, then would I
wander far off and remain in the wilderness. I
would hasten my escape from the windy storm
and tempest.’”
Told of Judge Yirgil Power (Q. ) that once,
as a barrister, he couldn’t prevent a popular young
fellow about town from getting five years for

whipped a cocky little attorney’s messenger with

off yesterday. Hard cheese, eh ! ”
It is only the “ religious ” papers that are
truly comic. Sydney Life Words takes upon itself
to describe Rev. Congregational-Union-chairman
Binns as “one who has power with God, and who
dwells in the secret place of the Most High.” It
somehow forgot to add that, on the whole, Mr.
Binns is disposed to take quite a favorable view
of the Deity’s disposition and intentions.
Edward Wilson, the original proprietor of
the Argus, always gave his editors one piece of
advice —“Don’t go into Society : you will then
avoid even the appearance of belonging to any
particular coterie lt was sound journalistic
counsel, and has been pretty consistently adhered
to by those to whom it was tendered. Editor
Haddon, for instance, has chiefly associated
with his own well-bred dogs.

The late George Robertson rather surprised
some people by living for seven years after his
retirement from the Melbourne publishing busi-
ness. He started his life of ease in a gloomy
spirit by giving a <£looo-order for a family-vault at
St. Kilda cemetery. It is a beautiful, roomy struc-
ture, fitted with a ventilation-pipe that extends
way down to one of the main walks of the
cemetery. This provides an opening for any
gruesome joker, who, having left his victim to
admire the exterior of the vault, proceeds to the
pipe-mouth and groans into it. The groan swells
into resurrectionary rumblings, which, as a rule,
turn the other fellow pale.
The “ Cheap-Jack of the battlefield ” is
what the malignant Saturday Review styles par-
son Fitchett, upon whose hysterics of style and
catch-phrase tricks it lavishes unsparing ridicule.
Had “Deeds that Won” appeared under Fit-
chett’s nom-de-guerre of “Vedette,” it is re-
marked, the work might have passed for that of
some callow military enthusiast, “ finding relief in
the gush to which the amateur is prone.” Ama-
teur ! —ye gods !
The slaughter of Woolf Joel, the Trans-
vaal gold-bug, judging from the evidence of his
business manager, would seem to have been the
unpremeditated outcome of a blackmail scheme.
Wertheim, the assassin, was endeavoring to lure
Joel (and a sum of .£2500) into a plot, bogus or
otherwise, for the “removal” of President Kruger,
and Joel, being quite alive to the fact that any
sort of connivance in said plot would put his mil-
lions at the mercy of his accomplices, told Wer-
theim to clear out of the office.
A notable event in this Age of Steel
should be the recent passing away of the man
who made it possible. Before Bessemer’s dis-
coveries the price of steel was almost prohibitive,
but when, as the result of his despised investiga-
tions, the minimum cost of steel rails leaped sud-
denly down from £5O a ton to less than <£s—the
enchanter’s wand had been waved that was to
revolutionise bridge-building, cover the civilised
world with railways, and load the waters with the
tremendous fighting-machines of to-day.
From Melb. Age leading article :
Lord Canterbury, when Governor here, entertained, ac-
cording to custom, the members of the Assembly in
batches. Turning on one occasion to one of his political
guests, with an air of superb graciousness, he said, “ I
believe, sir, you were originally a tailor?” “ I was,” re-
plied the M.L.A. “ And pray, sir, how are you employed
now?” queried Governor Canterbury. “At present,”
replied the member, “ I am engaged in taking your Ex-
cellency’s measure.”
Good—but what was the name of that member ?
Pump Court: “ Melb. demise, octogena-
rian Barrister Pridham, once a London Times
special commissioner. Amazing what a reputa-
tion a man can get, if he only lives long enough.
Obituarised, Pridham almost inserts himself into
the line of Dick Ireland, Miehie, Aspinall, Fellows,
Dawson —not to speak of such moons to the suns
as Purves, Madden, and Hodges. In sooth, Prid-

appeared, the reporters knew that the other fellow
would get the verdict.”
“ C.0.W.” to The Bulletin :
You have often alluded to “ Philosopher ” Smith, dis-
coverer of Mt. Bischoff tin-mine, and allowed corres-
pondents to call upon people across the water to erect a
monument to his memory ; which is as it should be. But
not a word has ever escaped you in favor of the man who
really made it the biggest and best-managed concern of
the kind in the world. When Ferdinand Kayser took
charge of Mt. Bischoff some 25 years ago, no one ever ex-
pected it to become a success. He came recommended
by such men as Newbery and Ulrich, and, after examin-
ing the mine, made certain proposals to the directors
which were vigorously opposed by Smith aforesaid, but
were, after a great struggle, accepted by a majority of
one. Smith then sold the greater part of his shares,
Kayser buying as many as his means allowed at £1 and
less. Since those days Kayser has built up a township
connected with the coast by a railway, where formerly
all was but bush and swamp. The mine has paid nearly
1J millions in dividends, and Kayser’s earnest wish is to
live long enough to reach the two millions. Australia
badly needs more men of his stamp.

Man-who-is-dead Drury was a frozen-fish
kind of “ toff ”in his way. Said a colonel once
who sat beside him eating at the club : “ It is a
pity so few members come here to luncheon.
Look at the empty seats. Our charge is much
too great and should be lowered. Do you not
think so, Drury?” Drury transfixed him with
his eyeglass : “ Haw ! I have been infawmed that
a—aw —luncheon may be procuah’d in Brisbane
faw—aw—sixpence—haw. And I have no doubt
—aw —it satisfies those-—aw individuals who
prefah that kind of thing—haw.”
When an English Admiral cables the
Admiralty, the operators are sworn to strict
secrecy, although a special code is usually em-
ployed. A few years back an Admiral on the
Australian command was dictating an important
message to a telegraphist up Q. way, and at the
conclusion exclaimed, “ Thank heaven, that’s the
end! ” Operator saw the chance of a joke, but
had hardly clicked off the first word of the ex-
clamation when the naval man interrupted—
“ Hm-m ! No necessity to send that. They are
not humorists at the other end, you know.”
John Pope, of the big Sydney firm of
Farmer and Co., has gone to England, after
having lavishly farewelled and been fare welled
by his numerous employes. People with long
memories have bethought themselves of John’s
old-time familiar and startlingly original wheeze,
about how he owed his first real advance in com-
merce to the fact that his employer noticed him,
careful man, pick up a pin which lay on the floor.
But it is now a long time since the opulent
merchant has trotted out this story of Pope’s pin.
Judge Lutwyche (Q.) was a fowl-fancier.
One day, a witness said that he had risen at cock-
crow. The Judge observed that, as far as that
went, the Australian rooster crowed just when
the fit took him. At which Blake, a barrister, re-
marked, “ Ah, yer ’anner—the spirit av St. Pether
is abroad in yer ’anner’s hin roosts.” “ I can’t say

Lady Fitzroy, Governor’s Fitzroy's wife,
was rather homely-looking. One day a Sydney
grocer sneaked in at the “tradesman’s entrance ”
at Government House. He encountered Lady
Fitzroy, and mistook her for the housekeeper. He
edged up and slipped a tenner into her hand.
“Get me the business, and I’ll double it,” he
whispered. Next day the grocer man received his
appointment as providore to the vice-regal house-
hold, with an intimation that Lady Mary Fitzroy
would be glad to receive the promised <£2o, for
distribution in charity. And then the grocer man
tried to hide his delighted shame in a butter keg.
Deffell, an amusing old fossil, who was
once N.S.W. Insolvency Judge, spoke through
his nose. One time, a sailor was witness in a case
arising out of the alleged felonious scuttling of a
brig. The sailor testified that the captain had
gone into the hold during the night-time. “Did
you—er—see him—er —go into the hold ? ” asked
Deffell. “No, your Honor.” “Then—er—how
did you—er—know it was the —er—captain?”
“Iheer’d his voice, yer Honor.” “You are—er
—shuah it was his voice ? ” “ Sartain, yer Honor.
It’s a pecooliar sort of voice. Suthin’ betwixt a
penny whistle and a snore, yer Honor.” “Ah !
a sort of—er—snuffle ? ” asked the Judge. “ Jes-
so,” said the witness, “ suthin like yer Honor’s !”
The late Tas. Chief Justice Lambert Dob-
son was an illustration of the fact that a man need
not possess exceptional ability to secure a reputa-
tion for it. From his school-days the late Judge
moved in a groove, from which he never swerved.
Essentially commonplace, conservative and highly
respectable, he had no capacity for great virtues
or vices. His intellect belonged to that average
order which enables a man to confine his reason-
ing to accepted facts, and sustain his faith with
accepted theories. A careful lawyer, with no
instinct in the direction of the initiative or the
speculative, a man of decent habits, a churchman
of the Thirty-nine Articles school, and a proof of
the value of passivity as a propelling force.
Thirty years ago a Scotchman named
M'Kay, on the staff of Brisbane Courier, was
a crank on the subject of Carlyle. He would lug
the sage into even a three-line paragraph, and his
talk was punctuated with Carlylean dogmatics.
The staff got to be tired of it, but endured it
for Carlyle’s sake. But, one day, M‘Kay fastened
on to a new man with Carlyle. “Oh, d n
Carlyle ! ” said the latter, rising and leaving the
office. M‘Kay sat aghast for some moments.
Then he went to Jones, the sub. “ Hae ye on.y
reliable knowledge o’ the new reporter’s character,
Mr. Jones?” “Well —yes. Why do you ask?’
“ Oh, joost for this :he d ns Carlyle.” “Does
he?” cried Jones, eagerly ; “then, by God ! I’ll
raise his screw.”

Aboriginalities .
At Wahgunyah, the other day, the police
arrested two poor devils suspected of having
snapped up certain trifles from the domiciles of
townspeople. Amongst the articles on the police-
list were several rolls of beef. Accused’s swags
were searched and the officers found what they
reckoned was the pickled bullock. The tramps
stoutly affirmed that the meat was that of a fat
dog which they had poached, pickled and skewered
up for use on the wallaby ; in proof of which they
actually produced the unfortunate tyke’s skin and
part of tlie carcase from which they had been
cutting steaks. “ Tinned-dog ! ” When the dog-
supply runs out, swaggies of this kind will be
pickling fat policemen.

Writes “ 6 x 8 h to The Bulletin
I m weary of shrieks about fatmen, crushers, tyrants,
&c. After all, the "rafter in general, and bush-worker in
particular, is the, boss-sweater of the lot. Squatters,
managers, mill-owners, and others don’t know how to
sweat- until the “ sat-upon-toiler” conies along and shows
them. Bill takes a contract of fencing, ring-barking, or
drain-cutting at a decent figure, but when hard-up Jim
happens along, Bill puts him on at, say, £1 per week and
tucker i.e., damper and mutton. Bill makes from 12
to 15s. per day, while Jim, who does the same amount of
graft in the same 12 hours a day (there’s no 8 hours about
Bill when he is the employer) receives the magnificent
sum of 3s. 4d. aforesaid. The squatters see all this, and
justly argue—“ If Bill the toiler can get Jim the toiler to
work 12 hours a day for 3s. 4d., why shouldn't I? ” The
most barefaced sweating of this sort is done in the shear-
ing sheds (union or otherwise) by wool-pressers and
cooks. Fencers, tank-sinkers, &c., often own plants and
horse-power, and are entitled to a bigger share of the
contract; but wool-pressers and cooks enter a shed with
nought but their hands. In fully 75 per cent, of the
sheds Bill takes the wool-pressing by contract at the
average price of Is. fid. per bale, and emplo3's Jim at the
union rate of 255. per week. Both start on equal terms,
and both do exactly the same kind and amount of work.
Yet Bill coolly pockets four times the amount that Jim
does. Station-owners’ eyes are beginning to open, and
when wool-pressing (flic hardest work in the shed) drops
down to 255. per week, Bill the sweater will be the first
to shriek about crushers and tyrants ! In many cases,
cooks make even nowadays from £8 to £lO per week (4s.
per man is the average pay to a shearer’s cook), and pav
their “ off-siders ” (assistants), who do all the laborious
work and are on their feet from 4 a.m. to 11 p.m., 255. or
30s. per week ! Naturally, of course, the cook’s wage is
rapidly going down-hill. Shearers, who snort and bite
and battle for 8 hours and £1 a hundred, and union
agreements for themselves, are the hardest and worst-
paying employers when they take contracts. Also, many
of them leave a 16 hours-a-day and 12s. per-week-slave on
their selections when the shearing begins. Bill and Jim’s
alleged mateship and talk about equality and brother-
hood of man is ghastly satire to the man who has worked
with and for them Sweat!—Fatman’s not in it !

The average bushllian has far better teeth
f ha « the average townsman and a dentist out
back does not make a pile. It is surprising what
some bushies can get through with their teeth.
Many can bite pins in half like cheese, and they
have a selector on the Lachlan who can bite
through fencing-wire, and who wasonly beaten onco
—by a Sydney hash-house rump-steak. On the
other hand, a bushman with toothache has a bad
time, having little or no remedy save of his own
invention. A favorite bush cure is to shove a red-
hot needle or piece of wire into the tooth ; which
is supposed to “ kill the nerve,” and often goes
near killing the man. Some bushies like to have
the tooth out, and there are many ways of shifting
it. A common way is to dig it out with a pen-
knife, but others do the job quicker and better
with hammer and chisel. The latter is a good
way, but a still better is a team of bullocks and
ten yards of wire-rope,

“ Jeff ” tells
The Bulletin
he has dicovered
another Chinese
industry :
Some time ago,
after a cattle-drov-
ing job, I was coming down one of the western Q. rivers.
For first 80 miles there were some fine patches of
pie and water melons, and sometimes pumpkins, grow-
ing wild on the T.S. reserves, as a result of floods
washing the melons out of gardens and depositing
them along the river road. After a long stretch in
the saddle behind a sluggish mob of bullocks, even the
curseful drover will call it a streak of luck when he
comes on one of these patches. But when I got within
20 miles of C township I found patch after patch
ivstroyed. Almost everyone I met
blamed the unfortunate “battler,” and
I put it down to some of the Sydney
“talent” until one evening, about dusk,
I caught two Chows vigorously destroy-
ing melon-vines. When they sighted
me they bolted off, but 1 had two good
cattle dogs that weren’t very partic-
ular about what they chewed, and
they ran the Chows up a leaning tree.
The explanation I got was -that while
people could get their melons for noth-
ing they would not buy the Celestial
melon.

“Theßleafcer” to The Bulletin, re the
“ useful ” dodge :
Following is modus operandi of the proprietor of a
certain country woolscouring mill. He advertises in
one of the dailies :—“ Wanted a dozen lads to be
“ useful” in woolscouring mills; Bs. per week and keep.
Apply So-and-So’s registry.” As there is always a number
of willing lads unable to get work in the metropolis, they
simply jump at the chance. After parting up 7s. fid.
(usually borrowed for the occasion) as the labor-agent's
fee, they sign an agreement binding them to stay the
season of about five months. Then, with Labor Bureau
tickets, they start North and arrive about dinner-time
hungry and sleepy. Boss meets them and directs them
to the mill some distance outside town ; and next day it
doesn’t take them long to find out that they’ve got to
work just as hard as the “regulars’ who get their 255.
per week. And, what with the purchase of necessaries
and so on, when emancipation day arrives their cheque
wouldn’t pay for a round of drinks. Annually this dodge
is worked on numbers of lads, and yet the perpetrator
contrives with a little surface bonhommie to maintain the
reputation of a “ real good-hearted fellow.”

Perhaps the most pathetic thing about the
Warra Yadin (Vic.) murder was the place where
the victim resided with his red-haired flame. The
“ house ” was one-roomcd and built of sheets of
odd sizes of galvanised-iron nailed and tied
with wire to unsquared stringy-bark poles. The
roof was a lean-to of iron and bark, and the num-
ber of directions it leant to would be hard to say.
Frequent apertures in the “walls” had been
choked up with boards, bits of tin, and discarded
wearing - apparel. There was an old bedstead
covered with a quantity of antique bedding, and,
at the foot of it, stood a row of kerosene-tins con-
taining brine, in which floated portions of mutton
and rabbit. Above where the pillows should have
been—in the place usually occupied by “ God
Bless Our Home ’’—depended from the roof a
sheep’s pluck with congealed blood hanging from
it in long, horrible strings, which threatened to
fall into the mouth of any sleeper below. “Go on
the land, young man ! ”


“ SURPLUS.”
The term “surplus ’’ is applied, in life insurance
affairs, to the balance, ascertained periodically,
between the assets and the liabilities. Out of this
balance or surplus, distributions of profits are
made to the policy-holders entitled to participate.
The natural tendency of distribution is to diminish
the surplus for the moment ; but in the case of a
prosperous company, the decrease is speedily more
than covered by accessions of profit from various
sources. It is clear, however, that the more
liberal the periodical distributions of profits, the
more gradual will be the growth of the surplus.
Conversely, if a life insurance company withho'ds
from its policy-holders the full benefit of the pro-
portion of profits immediately available, and
prefers to accumulate in the interest of future
participants, it follows that the surplus will in-
crease more rapidly in proportion. Of course it
is easy to keep in hand more than the future will
require under any possible circumstances; and,
when that course is followed, injustice to present
participants is inevitable.
Now, The Mutual Life of New York, while
careful to make abundant reserves for future
bonuses, has always been equally careful to dis-
tribute to its policy-holders the full immediate
benefits which they are entitled to claim. The
Company has never sacrificed the interests of
policy-holders entitled to immediate participation,
to the formation of an imposing but empirical
surplus, never likely to be required even in the
remotest future. The surplus shown by The
Mutual Life at the end of 1896 was .£6,105,444.
It is a steadily increasing surplus, which, need-
less to say, is more than ample for all the
demands for which it has been created.
But, supposing the Company had, throughout
its history, acted on the principle which
has been adopted in, at least, one prominent
instance—of reserving a large proportion of the
surplus at every distribution, and, in addition,
reducing the surrender-values allowed for discon-
tinued policies, what does the reader suppose
would have been the amount of The Mutual’s
surplus in 1896 ? Not six millions, but upwards of
24 millions sterling ! This is not guesswork. The
figures are based upon the official report issued hy
the Insurance Department of the State of New
York, which gives annually the exact record of
every company doing business in that State.
Since its organisation, The Mutual Life has paid
in bonuses and surrender values to its policy-holders
£44,445,244, which is more than twice the amount
paid for similar items by the next largest company.
Observe, then, what a magnificent surplus The
Mutual could have exhibited to-day, had an
illiberal policy been pursued in relation to the
assured —a surplus exceeding that of any other
office by more than 150 per cent. But, fortunately,
the Company preferred to sacrifice a mere useless
and vainglorious display to the solid interests of
the policy-holders. Who shall say that, in taking
thiscourse, the Mutual Life of’ New York has
not chosen the better part? and that, in so doing,
it has not well earned the high reputation which
it has attained in every part of the world as “ the
Policy-holders’ C 0.,”“ the Co. which does the most
good i ” The intelligent reader will not allow him-
self to be misled by the parading of sums of no
use to the present generation, and in excess of
any possible needs of the generations to come.*

THE GRACE OF THE CRINOLINE.
This is a scene from Mr. Pinero’s latest comedy,
“ Trelawney of the Wells,” clipped from London Daily
Chronicle. The scene is laid in or about 1861—and the
women loolc just as lovely as this !
This is the time of year when the Sydney waxworks
man takes an assortment of the Aggers round the country
on their annual tour to enlighten the bush intelligence.
There are several hundred murderers and others in the
waxworks, and about 60 of the most portable and ferocious
are selected once a year for a season out-back. There are
no Federal delegates among them —the management re-
ports with regret that the wax image of a Federal delegate
wouldn’t draw worth a cent. Butler is expected to be
the chief attraction, and George Dean is also worth a
good deal even yet, in a business point of view. Reynell,
who goes to pilot the statue of Dean round the country,
once acted as manager for Meagher when that sad, dry
person delivered his incoherent lecture to audiences that
consisted mostly of the four winds, so he will have a
chance to look at both sides of the question. The tour
starts at Newcastle, and goes as far North as the wax
murderer can be taken without melting. If, by any
chance, Butler should melt and begin to bob ridiculously
forward, the only thing to be done will be to put an axe
in his hand and pass him off as young G. Washington.
Pinero’s new comedy, wherein Dot Boucicault, Hilda
Spong, and Pattie Browne appear, is deliberately dated
back to the sixties, and dressed in the hideous fashions of
that dark age. Pretty Hilda Spong, for instance, is en-
compassed by a mauve silk-dress that can stand alone-
on its crinoline—when she gets out of it. A black straw
hat, pork-pie shape, with a yellow feather sticking from
a crimson tuft, is said to be not so very unbecoming,
ditto the golden hair a-hanging down her neck in a
beaded net. But the single-button mauve kid-gloves and
small mauve parasol are as essentially ridiculous as the
dundreary whiskers and peg-top trousers of the male
“toffs” in the cast. Pinero’s idea in reviving these
thirty-year-old atrocities has not been confided to the
public. It can hardly be that he looks back with admira-
tion to the figures that he and his girl presented in his
young love days. The author of “ Sweet Lavender” must
have an artistic hatred for crinolines. Prob bly he was
merely aiming at an advt. for the show. Thousands of
women will want to go and see how “frightful’ they
looked of yore in hoop-de-doo-dedoos.
Dear Bulletin.— Paid ss. at Sydney Town Hall to
hear Madame Albani. Heard Trebelli in same hall
some months ago for a modest “ bob.” Now, I may
be a mug at music, but I say that if Albani’s warbling is
worth five shillings, Trebelli’s is well worth a pound. Yet
the latter, as far as I can remember, received no mayoral
reception in either Melbourne or Sydney, wasn’t inter-
viewed by the “ leading defies,” nor even cabled to by
royalty.— Sancho Panza.

exits, with a total disregard of the front.

of his art.’’

to America, it entirely broke up the little
nig. he brought from Central Africa.
“ When I was in England,” he said, “ I
used to be kissed all day by ladies; here

A plea for the theatre-band — re a recent
Bulletin paragraph. The fiddling contin-
gent is generally looked upon with con-
tumely as being the first to “ strike ” when
a show is in difficulties, and so bring about
the inevitable “close-up.” But An
actor who goes on shares, say one-quarter,
in ratio to salary, generally gets something
out of it; the hapless instrumentalist rarely
gets anything at all. £2 10s. a week is good
pay for an orchestra-man these times;
most of them don’t get anything like it.
In a recent case (a pantomime, whereof
details are known to the writer), artists at
£2O per week didn’t do so badly out of the
percentage system, but the music-grinders,
with about 10s. Od. a week eventuating,
found it a tough job to live on that.
Moreover, in a panto, show', for instance,
the orchestra-men have to work harder, take
them individually, than the actors. The
latter have their “ scenes ” and their inter-
vals, but the catgut-scraper has to plug
along all through the evening, every num-
ber, with no pause or rest whatever. An-
other big point is that an immense amount
of music-writing has to be done before the
show starts, for which, in the case of a “run-
on-credit ” concern, the luckless “ ar-
ranger” (who “trombones” at night) is seldom o
never paid, though he provides his own “ score ”
paper and et ceteras. Taken all round, the band-
folk are more often cheated, and looked upon as
fair game, than the acting-members of a 00., and
it is small blame to them if they stick out for their rights
in the only effective way—by concerted and prompt action.
The “striking” band is a benefaotor to the community,
and administers the coup de grace to many a show which
ought to be in its grave. If the mummers themselves'are
fools enough to prop up a plainly moribund entertain-
ment, why should the “great conducted,” with more to
lose and less to gain, be expected to make fools of them-
selves too ?
Maoriland has been no less prolific in mummers than in
musicians. Well-known pros, from the province include
Harry Roberts, Jewett, Walter-r-r Bentley, Bland Holt,
(both spent most of their youth in Dunedin) Montgomery,
who cuts a fair figure in B. Holt’s co., and Harry Mar-
shall. ’Most every Maorilander frets an hour or two upon
the amateur stage.
Horace Hodges, of the Wilson Barrett co., has, to a
considerable extent, the rare Dot Boucicaultian knack of
changing his identity on the boards. His Belos in
“Olaudian,” Ross Christian in “The Manxman,” Truma-
torius in “ Virginius,” and Begg in “Ben My Chree” are
distinctly different people.
Charles Arnold talks of coming again to Australia with
a said-to-be roaring farcical comedy—“ What Happened
at Jones’s.” The principal character in this clumsily-
named joke is a sporting bagman who disguises himself
as a bishop. Difficult to imagine Arnold revelling in such
a part.
The Academy of Music, Ballarat’s only theatre, built by
the late Baronet Clarke at a cost of £12,000, was offered
at auction the other day and withdrawn —£5750 being the
highest bid. Stated that the Salvation Army authorities
are likely to buy it. If Ballarat prefers Blood and Fire and
walloping of the Big Drum to the drama, it is Ballarat’s
own funeral, but one can’t help feeling sorry for it all the
same.
Printed that Mrs. Langtry has been dropping much
money at Monte Carlo. Lily had best go slow. The
“ oofless ” has-been society beauty doesn’t finish up over
weU, as a rule.
Jack Costello has managed to earn bread and butter in
England, up to date. Jack was the old Australian actor
who adopted Salvation for a while, but went back to the
stage. He journeyed ’Ome on spec., and soon got a show,
as Uncle Matthew in “ A Night Out,” with a provincial
co. At latest, he was appearing at one of London’s halls
in dramatic-sketch pieces. All the same, Jack warns the
boys out here not to follow his example. There are
20,000 actors, unemployed as a rule, in Great Britain.
Paradis : “ Melb. show business has brisked-up so
amazingly that we will hear whispers again, in the next
century, about a new theatre. In boom-time, only nine
years ago, there were plans drawn for two. One, a
superb place, to be called the Imperial or National, was
to be at the south-west corner of Bourke and Russell
streets, and was to take the shine out of the grand Prin-
cess’s. Another, in Collins-street, was for Brough and
Boucicault, Melb.’s Vaudeville, or Criterion. Vanished
dreams ! ”

Thus severely “M.F.K.”: — Be “Mummer Morality.”
Wilson Barrett’s defence is much more convincing to the
well-informed than Thk Bulletin’s attack. If the laws
of Mrs. Grundy do not exercise a tremendous influence on
theatrical life, why should so many actors and actresses
take the trouble to marry at all ? That a large proportion
of them are united by a legal tie The Bulletin will
scarcely deny. Women of scandalous notoriety have in
most cases been pretty considerably smirched before ever
they set foot on the stage, so their loss of virtue cannot
be put down to the corrupting influence of “ fierce com-
petition for public applause.” Most women who are
compelled to hustle for a living have, at one time or other,
suffered annoyance from the importunities of their
employers. Has The Bulletin never heard of the manager
of a softgoods store using his position to assail the virtue
of his female employes ? Has it ever heard of an actress
gaining and keeping a position on the stage merely
through selling her decency? The favors of a notorious
woman are not (inside a theatre) such marketable com-
modities as The Bulletin appears to imagine.
“ E.” : As to the alleged swindling propensities of actors,
boarding-house and hotelkeepers’ statements should be
received with a certain amount of reserve. The landlord
remembers all about the actor by whom he has been
duped, and loses no opportunity of advertising him as a
rogue, but he is apt to forget the stranger in another
walk of life, who has enjoyed his hospitality and then
vanished mysteriously into the unknown. That actors do
not, as a rule, desert their wives is best proved by a
reference to their children. Ask almost anv actor’s child
about its parents, and it will speak of both with affection
and reverence. It is new 6 indeed that the actor is ten
A BACKBLOCKS SKETCH.
The long—and SHORT of it!
'tunes as shamelessly dishonest as the commercial-man
who embezzles trust-money confided to his care. A few
so-called actors do, at times, cadge, bilk, or borrow, no
doubt, but the majority honestly pay their way, frequently
under difficulties which would appal an ordinary wage-
earner whose income is secure. The special circumstances
of an actor’s life therefore often bring out real grit instead
of fostering vanity and conceit. The world may or may
not take the actor’s deficiency in moral principle for
granted, but if ever it sees a Poverty Pointer borrowing
half-a-sov. from a pressman or a gentlemanly lover, clad
in a suit made by the tailor to Tim Bulletin staff, it will
probably do more than smile.

hands who would plump for him before any of those you
mention.—F.

Apropos a certain memorable
theatrical benefit. Stern moral-
ists may deny the pathos of the
lady’slastappearance before the
world, but the pathos is there,
nevertheless. Her palmiest days
were, of course, in London, long
ago. The gout had already got
hold of her nimble feet when
she came to Melbourne, and
astounded the waiters at the
Cafii Anglais (defunct) by taking
a full fourth share of the liquid
refreshment served to a joyous
quartette. The bill showed : one
bottle claret, four quarts of
champagne, one bottle old port,
and one bottle brandy. All the
bottles were “ dead-men” when
they left the table.

Addie Conyers left for Eng-
land by the Oruba, considerably
thinner than when she landed
here six months ago. Addie
is not the vigorous Conyers of yore, and when, in
the middle of a Melb. heat-wave, manager Rickards
talked of sending her to Westralia with his travelling co.,
she broke out into a profuse perspiration, and begged
him to excuse her from renewing the engagement. Addle
has The Bulletin’s best wishes for a rapid recovery of
her lost proportions.
Ambrose Manning, of Wilson Barrett’s co., must know
more about the text of the “ Silver King ” than even his
chief. The writer saw him play the part of Mr. ’Enery
Corkett in England at least ten years ago, and he had
then been playing it for several years. Now he is a
slightly exaggerated Elijah Coombe.
R. W. Carey, who died lately in Melb., leaving his
family poorly off, originated Saturday-night popular con-
certs in the Smellful City. Which reminds The Bulletin
that fully 50 per cent, of the Vic. burnt cork artists (?)
owe their introduction to Saturday “ pops.” “ Bob ”
Carey was in the field with juvenile opera long before
Tom Pollard, and toured the whole of Maoriland over and
over again, coining money, and spending it recklessly.
His last years were spent in the service of bill-poster
Roff, superintending the distribution of ads.
“ Nothing has covered Imperial Federation with more
ridicule.” writes The Bulletin’s London correspondent,
“than the so-called Imperial Institute. ‘Destitute’
would have been the more fitting title, for it is-of
reason, use and dignity. Certainly it provides a few
billets for Wales’ pals, and well they stick up for the
jam-ladler, but otherwise it is the laughing-stock of every
intelligent person who takes any note of its doings.
Now, a cheap variety show, next a popular concert,
always a fair feeding restaurant, it has in the season the
inevitable frightened - haired, guttural-eyed orchestra
blazing away in reckless fashion for the benefit of con-
versationalists. In this matter of music the management
got badly spoofed by a crack violinist, who, with an excel-
lent English orchestra, had been refused a season’s en-
gagement, at the 1.1. He simply re-labelled his party the
‘ Scandinavian Orchestra,’ called himself Sigvard Eriksen,
broke up his English to suit, and was engaged immedi-
ately upon application under these conditions.
H. : The notification of London Critic that it will
accept no theatrical passes or free copies of books for re-
view is good. There must always be a lingering scruple
about slating a play at which the reporter is virtually a
guest, or telling the unpleasant truth about a book which
comes with the author’s compliments. [“ Publisher’s
oompliments” mostly.— Ed. B.] Apropos: A Maoriland
paper once remarked re a locally-written novel: “As the
work is dedicated to ourselves we can hardly say any-
thing likely to prejudice it in the estimation of the
public.” What a gem that would have been with the
word “ further ” before “ prejudice ! ”
T.D. : “ When will Australian editors ban the unutter-
able anti-climax of the last paragraph in the ordinary
dramatic notice? One reads', for instance, a column of
well-rounded description and interesting comparisons
anent ‘ the Manxman ; ’ then comes the dreadful intima-
tion that ‘ the first matinee will be given on Wednesday
afternoon, at which children will be admitted half-price.’
Immediately gives the show away as a column, more or
less, of advertiser’s gush.”

A.A.G.” from Maoriland :
Dear Bulletin, —Having read “ Lost and Given Over.”
by E. J. Brady, in Bulletin of Jan. 22, I wish to remark
that of all the “ chanteys ” I ever “ read of in books or
dreamt of in dreams,” or have heard on the long voyage
round the Horn, this of Brndy's is the best. Kipling’s
“Bolivar” is good, but it carries a moral, which a
“ chantey ” should not. In “ Lost and Given Over ” you
have the incoherent, vociferous, blatant sailor-man (just
home from a cruise) sitting on top of an empty, up-turned
barrel in a rowdy pub., with a pipe in one hand and a
mug of beer in the other, singing with all the might of
his raucous voice, and kicking his heels on the resound-
ing barrel in time to his utterly immoral song. But you
love the sailor-man, all the same ; and Bradv shows you
exactly what a good-hearted, easy-going, candid sort of
devil he is. I have read “L. and G. O.” a dozen times;
I have sung it to all sorts of improvised tunes, till I am
almost drunk with the spirit that is in it, and my verdict
is that it is the real thing, reader than anything even B.
himself—our poet of the fo’c’s’le—has yet invented. It
is the ideal “ chantey.”—A.A.G.

to the N.S. Wales Governors for many years past,
has conferred a blessing upon the citizens of his
country by discovering a wonderful cure for head-

effectual, no matter what the cause of the head-
ache may be. This cure, the result of years of
experience, can be had in 3 sizes, by post i/4, 2/6,
3/8. Senior, chemist, George-street, Sydney.*
THE AUSTRALIAN GEM TRADE.
H. Newman, the well-known jeweller of 175
Elizabeth-street, Melbourne, is the largest buyer
of opal in Australia, and opal miners will find it
to their advantage to send their opal direct to
him. The firm has a reputation for straight deal-
ing extending over a period of 40 years, which is
a sufficient guarantee to consignors that they will
be honorably treated. H. Newman is also a
buyer of all other Australian gems.*

HAMLET—YE GODS!
"Say, Bill, they must ev ’ad a run on special editions in them days!”
Here’s a novel clip from Cardinal Moran’s
“History of the Church in Australia.” The
country visited by Torres and I)e Quiros could not
have been (the Cardinal argues) the island of
Espiritu Santo, inasmuch as De Quiros’ narrative
says the length of the land was greater than that
of Europe—islands included. De Quiros gave the
harbor visited the name of Holy Cross, and de-
scribed it as being large enough to hold a thousand
ships. Also (he asserted) there were two large
rivers flowing into it—one of them as large as the
Guadalqui ver. The explorer’s account also states
that the land stretched from east to west ; that
the harbor had a row of islands in front and a
range of mountains in the background. Further,
he adds that the first thing the Spaniards did on
landing was to erect a cross and build a church to
our Lady of Loretto. . . . Now, the Cardinal
argues from the description given of the harbor,
and De Quiros' probable whereabouts as estimated
from his own mention, of latitude, that Gladstone
occupies the site whereon the Spaniards landed.
His Eminence, however, falls badly into the soup
in specifying the Calliope and Burnett as the two
rivers mentioned as flowing into the harbor of
Port Curtis —for the latter river, as most people
know, is many a league southward. And whither
away with all this geographical digression from an
ecclesiastical pundit? Only this : the Cardinal
would fain convince us that mass was celebrated
on the shores of Port Curtis three hundred years
ago. May be—who knows ?


THE ATTITUDE OF RUSSIA.
“It is well understood now that the Russian game is a long game: its method, to creep on awhile. to spring a stage at fair opportunity, then to halt and look pleasant, again to creep on. and so
to proceed till the time arrives when a rush may be made from more than one well-prepared post of vantage. Unless that time is supposed to have come, occasion invites to another halt, and to looking
pleasant again.”

The Minerva Company is proud of the high
character of its testimonials. Among those who
have expressed their appreciation of preparations
by this company are : —Madame Albani, his
Eminence the Cardinal-Archbishop’s Private
Secretary, Hon. M. H. Moreton (Queensland),
Mr. Wm. Stevenson, manager of the Presby-
terian ; Mr. Hart, late Government Photographer
to the Colony of New South Wales; Mr. Selby
(Feldheim, Gotthelf and Co.), Dr. Davidson, Bal-
main ; Dr. Kane, Bourke ; Dr. Purcell, 13 Jamie-
son-street, Sydney ; Mr. Cole, “ Rosslyn,” Fraser-
road, Lewisham ; Dr. Ekin, Visiting Physician to
Thirlmere Home ; Dr. Ashwell, Glebe ; Dr. H.
Brown, Molong; Dr. Piercy, Strathfield; Dr.
Elliott (Elliott Bros.); Mr. Blackett, Govern-
ment Analyst, Victoria ; Mr. Dixon, Analyst,
Sydney; Mr. Stutchbury, Chief Clerk H.M.
Customs, Newcastle ; and many others in equally
leading positions, whilst most of the best-known
newspapers in Sydney, including Evening News,
Sunday Times, Freeman’s Journal, &c., have
published authoritative paragraphs containing
the highest possible praise.
One of the latest of the Minerva testimonials iB
from Mr. R. A. Luckham, one of the gentlemen
who so ably represents the commercial interests of
the Sydney Bulletin in Sydney, and who writes
to the Minerva Company as follows :
“I have taken Minerva Tonic, and found it
admirable in every way. I had slept badly, had
eaten badly, was, in fact, not well at all when I
tried Minerva Tonic as a restorative. As a re-
sult, I can congratulate the proprietors in putting
such a first-class tonic on the market. In my case
it promoted a good appetite, made me sleep well,
put new strength into me, and did me a lot of
good generally. lam also in a position to state
that Minerva Tonic has performed wonders in
other cases.
“ (Signed) R. ADAMS LUCKHAM.

INDICATIONS WHEN YOU WANT
MINERVA.
Anaemia, headaches, drowsiness, undue fatigue,
weak eyesight, uncertain temper, broken rest,
failing memory, lack of interest, poor appetite, in-
Bomma, influenza, ague, hysteria, physical debility,
neuralgia, toothache, night sweats, nervousness,
dislike of company, melancholia, unfounded ap-
prehension of trouble, irregularity of the bowels,
fulness after meals, sour taste in the mouth,
general listlessness, faint feeling, &e., &e.
WHAT MINERVA TONIC IS.
Minerva Tonic is a phosphorised iron syrup
combined with rare recuperative ingredients, and
is compounded from a formula approved by the
highest medical authorities, under the supervision
of legally qualified chemists of the first standing.
Taken in the prescribed doses it will speedily and
assuredly quicken the appetite, purify and enrich
the blood, renew the elasticity of youth, promote
a confident and happy feeling, stimulate the secre-
tions, harden and steady the nervous system, and
indirectly make bone and muscle and good healthy
fi dah.
WHOM MINERVA TONIC IS SOLD BY.
Minerva Tonic, which is offered to the public
under the registered trade-mark and name of that
famous goddess, is sold by all Chemists and
Dealers throughout these colonies, or will be pro-
cured by them on customers insisting on having it,
and refusing all substitutes. The price, New
South Wales, is 2s. 6d. per bottle, or it will be
mailed direct from the Sydney Depot, 56 Mar-
garet-street, on receipt of 3s. 6d., which includes
postage.

Reference to the accompanying plan will show that the
leases consist of 106 acres, and which are an amalgama-
tion of the East Tasmania Company and the Consols pro-
perties, cover the whole of the area on the eastern end of
the Tasmania Gold Mine, into which an extension of that
company’s reef could be carried.
The phenomenal success of the Tasmania Gold Mine is
well known, the company having taken out 417,536 ounces
of gold, and paid to date over £667,000 in dividends from
their reef, which is 22 feet in thickness, and is now being
worked at a depth of 700 feet, the stone extracted having
averaged throughout over loz. sdwt. They are now en-
raged in sinking a new main shaft to a depth of 1156 feet,
and erecting thereon a hauling and pumping plant, which
will be one of the largest and most effective in these
colonies, and which will completely drain the surrounding
area.
Mr. H. H. Schlapp, recently examined the property,
and it is upon his recommenation that the present amal-
gamation has been effected. He is of opinion that the
general indications justify the capital expenditure pro-
posed in an effort to discover the extension of the emin-
ently-profltable Tasmania lode, and in that view the pre-
sent scheme is a legitimate mining venture.
It will be note l that the owners of the amalgamated
liases received their consideration in shares only.

ACTION ON THE HEART.—Professor Heckel states
that Kola, by the Caffeine and Theobromine which it con-
tains, is a tonic of the heart, whose pulsation it accele-
rates, while it increases its power and regulates its con-
traction§. Professor Heckel further points out that the
principle called Kolaine contained in Kola prevents rapid
waste of the tissues during fatiguing exertions, while the
Caffeine stimulates the muscles.
PNEUMONIA.—Worry kills businessmen. Heavy losses
ana hard times make them depressed in spirits. The brain
and body become weak ; unknown to them they are de-
bilitated. It is then that the least chill strikes them, and
pneumonia carries them off. To fortify the system and
make the spirits buoyant, nothing can equal Fisher and
Co s famous Kola Nut Tonic. It is the most remarkable
mvigorator and sustainer known.
DIARRHOEA.—Dr. Durian and Dr. Cunio have both re-
ported cures in rebellious diarrhoea under the use of Kola.
A recent issue of the Planters’ Monthly contains a
description of this remarkable Nut and its uses:—“Kola
has the property of staying hunger, whilst supporting the
struggle for existence. If a supply of nuts had been in-
cluded in Mr. Stanley’s outfit, his men would not have
suffered so terribly for want of animal food. As might be
expected, the value of Kola increases as it makes its way
into the interior of Africa, and some of the tribes furthest
removed from the sea pay for the dry powder an equal
weight of gold-dust. In a despatch from Mr. George
Alexander Stevens, Her Majesty’s consul at Bahia, he calls
attention to the great powers of endurance in lifting
heavy loads and transporting them to long distance in the
tropical climate, attributed to the free use of Kola Bean.
An old African, after biting a piece of the magic bean,
luted 179 English pounds weight on to his head and
carried the same for a distance of four miles.”
“ Still another merit is named, however, which will in-
terest the temperance party. The negroes who chew
Kola Nut have no desire to drink rum, and hence save
money and retain strength.”
Dr. LEON ERNEST MONNET, Paris, reports “ I
gave a strong infusion of Kola Nut to a railroad track-
layer, who was suffering from ‘blue devils’ (hypochon-
dria) extremely, and within two hours he felt like going
to his hard duties, and laughed and talked.”

POLITICAL
POINTS
M Gowan, leader of N.S.W. Labor-party,
was born at sea. He is still there.
Tasmania, according to Ben Tillett, is,
barinig the West Coast, “a land of prigs and
Thursday Island suffered a census during
March and panned out thusly :—Total population,
1702. Luropeans, 608; Japs, 619; Philippine
Islanders, 60 ; aboriginals, 60 ; Chinese, 58 ; &e.,
&c. Japanese increase for year, 288.
IN “PUSH" SOCIETY.
sis : “So yer goin’ ter chuck fightin’?”
STOUCHER: “ Yes; knocking me ehiv all out of
shape !”
SIS : “If yer ask me, I think every scrap yer ’ave
improves it!”
Drake, the lone, lorn Mrs. Gummidge of
Q. politics, is rapidly taking his place as a “back
number.” Although able, gritty, and sometimes
earnest, he never seems to catch the current when
it serves. Like Nelson, he neverminds.
When Federation is really accomplished
it should be the earnest effort of every good radi-
cal on the continent to convert Tasmania into a
democracy. The conversion of W.A. is pretty
well assured. This is the best way to euchre Fat-
man in the Federal game.
Is Peacockism contagious 1 B. R. Wise,
since his return from the last sitting of the Federal
Convention, breaks suddenly into such sustained
cacchination at the mention of Premier Reid’s
attitude towards the Commonwealth Bill that
people say, “That’s Peacock No. 2.”
S. A. Laborites and Ministerialists threaten
to take different ways over Federation. The
Kingston Ministry warmly supports the present
scheme, but the Labor-party, so far, intends to
solidly oppose. The latter, by the way, has not
elected a leader to succeed M'Pherson.
The present is a time when every N.S.W.
elector should see that he is on the roll and hustle
for his elector’s right. The Federation poll will
shortly be due, and, when 80,000 affirmative votes
are required, every adult male white citizen who
can be rolled up to the poll will be badly needed.
Governor Brassey fired off a most unneces-
sary confession before leaving Melbourne. He
told a Ministerial gathering that “ during his
residence in the colony it had been his chief duty
to speak well of the great Motherland which he
represented.” As though the beastly kawliny
didn’t know.
Edmund Barton—the man of Australia.
A couple of years ago Barton’s Federation ad-
dresses were always compressed by the Sydney
dailies into a quarter-column or less. At his
meeting, last week, one morning paper was repre-
sented by half-a-dozen of its best reporters, with
the result that between six and seven columns
were recorded next day in the first person.
Most N.S.W. M’sP. would as soon become
members of the fatly-paid Public Works Com-
mittee as join a Ministry. Works committee-men
are just now drawing 15 guineas a week, in addi-
tion to Parliamentary screws, for about 10 hours’
work, whilst Ministers only receive .£1370 a year,
and bear a heap of worry and quite a number of
expenses not incidental to the other billet.
Should Jock M’Kenzie and Jerome
Cadman retire, as rumored, the present M.L.
Govt, will set up a chameleonic record in point of
personnel. Ballance and Buckley are dead, Ward
is not dead but gone before, Reeves represents his
native land in London, and the only men left of
the original crowd, if M‘Kenzie and Cadman step
out, will be Kumara Dick himself and Minister
Carroll.
The matter of exploiting Papua for cheap
labor is again being mooted in Bananaland. Th
time-expired kanaka is too expensive; the raw
kanaka is too scarce. The fact is New Caledonia
is drawing off the best of the Polynesian laborers
to its shores. A “ free ” laborer in Noumea gets
J&5 a month ; a kanaka can make as much as .£6.
No wonder the “boys” buck at .£2O per annum
in Queensland.
The ultra-Foreign-trade party in N.S.W.
is against the Federation Bill on the ground that
it commits the Commonwealth to Protection, and
the ultra-Protectionist party in Victoria is against
it on the ground that it commits the Common-
wealth to Foreign-trade. A pretty sure proof
that the draft Constitution really leaves the fiscal
question quite open, and doesn’t commit the
country to anything.
Holdar M. : “ Admired the temerity of a
Labor member in town to-day sprinting past on a
bouncing bike. He handled a tennis-racquet and,
arrayed in spotless duck, cummerbund and a
Butler cap, presented quite a jaunty effect. This,
too, on the eve of a general election and within
sight of Sydney Star office.”
Apropos Federation and the border Cus-
toms duties, the story goes that when 10s. per ton
of chaff was collected on the S.A. border, near
Broken Hill, a timber-getter, working inN.S.W.,
rigged up a manger on the S.A. side of the rabbit-
proof fence, and his horses, standing in N.S.W.,
ate comfortably out of it. Moreover, the story is
true.
The Protectionist electors of Phillip Divi-
sion, Sydney, have chosen John Norton as their
candidate for the Leg. Assembly. However, as
many seats are open to Norton, it is presumed
that he will not stand against that straight, able,
and well-tried Protectionist, Copeland. If he
does, a Freetrade alderman will, of course, step in
between.
The N.S.W. Ministry was neither repre-
sented nor apologised for at the recent Sydney St.
Patrick s day celebration; but the Opposition
vvas there in full force. Such an over-
sight is not characteristic of Premier
Reid, though it fits several of his
colleagues like a glove. It was
all the more injudicious because
no member of the Ministry is of
Irish extraction.
Tozer’s pocket borough of Wide Bay has
dropped into Opposition hands, notwithstanding
there were two Oppositionists to divide the vote
and the Ministerial candidate was plainly branded.
But Jenkinson, the successful candidate, is a
smart young pressman, and personally popular;
and Chippindall, the Ministerialist, is a tired old
farmer, seasoned in local squabbles. So the elec-
tors practically darned politics, and chose the chap
they smiled with.
Of the Seven District Court Judges of N.S.
Wales, four (Murray, Backhouse, Rogers, and
Gibson) have at various times held acting-Supreme
Court commissions. Yet, when a permanent ap-
pointment is made, a man is always selected from
the Bar. If Judges of the D.C. can be entrusted
with acting appointments, why not consider their
claims when making permanent appointments ?
Such a course would tend to improve the personnel
of the District Court.
Comparing notes during the Convention
session in Melb., it was observed that N.S.W.
Hansard staff, though harder-worked, has some
more independent methods than the Vic. For
instance, N.S.W. men don’t hesitate to report an
interjection, and sometimes even a speech, as “in-
audible,” while “quotation not supplied” often
figures in the reports. But the Vic. men would
shudder at anything of the kind, and go to no end
of trouble in hunting up a missed interjection or
sentence.
Ed. Bulletin.—lt is written in Sydney
that while the Jews and the Scotch are clannish,
the Irish, like the English, will not go out of their
way to help each other. Wrong, both ways ! The
Englishman will go out of his way to help the
Englishman, although-to his credit be it said—
he’s just as ready to assist a Portuguese or a Pata-
gonian. And the Irishman—or a good many of
him—has that worst kind of clannishness which
to a comparatively harmless conviction of the sur-
passing merits of your own crowd superadds a
smouldering malignity towards those who don’t
happen to belong to it —Llham Dearg Aboo.
PUT YOURSELF IN HIS PLAGE.
BARMAID : “I don’t know why you want to get so beastly

AQUIFFY OLD PARTY : “Of course you don’t , my dear ;
YOU haven’t got a wife! ”
If leaden-footed Justice only delays a little
longer the Q.N.B. business will be beyond legal
touch by reason of the demise of most of the
parties concerned. Drury’s dead ; Palmer’s gone;
MTlwraith’s tottering on the brink ; and a certain
once all-potent ex-director is at present in a de-
plorably shaky condition in Brisbane.
DEEDS THAT WON THE
UMPIRE.
A sketch for the approaching football season.
Mayor Harkness, of Bendigo, refuses to
act as chairman at a meeting to hear a lecture, to
be delivered by Ben Tillett. Mr. Harkness says
that Mr. Tillett is not a desirable visitor. How-
ever, Tillett can open his own mouth ; he is under
no necessity to have his jaw blessed by an aider-
man.
J. H. Storey, M.L.A. (N.S.W.), the
garter-merchant who kept Barton out of the
Assembly, rose to a great rhetorical pitch at a re-
cent suburban function, in denunciation of Feder-
ation. “The whole scheme’s worthless,” quoth
Storey, working up to a final magnificent out-
burst. “It will impede our national progress !
I tell you it will ruin N.S.W., and disgrace us
for ever !! No Federation for me !!! ” But, in
the painful silence that ensued, a very crushed
and nervous Storey said “h-m-m” and “ha,”
mopped his perspiring brow, and wondered why
the thunderous applause had failed at the psycho-
logical moment. Federation isn’t so dead that the
Storeys of Australian politics may jump on it.
The anti-Federal party in Sydney is now
wading-in in deadly earnest, and its daily shriek
fills a large place in the morning papers. The
worst shrieker of the lot, one “F.,” rose lately
to tell the public that Sydney is now an
absolutely free port (where about millions are
collected annually in Customs duties); that the
Federation is committed to a 20 to 30 per cent,
tariff ; that the rivers are to be handed over to the
Federal Government; that the estimated cost of
the Federal Government is .£6,000,000 annually ;
that the said .£6,000,000 is to be “ spent in another
colony ”; and that “we are to place our railways
largely at the mercy of another colony.” Con-
sidering that the Telegraph gives this awful per-
jurer space in its columns to spread these lurii
fictions, that paper evidently doesn’t care much
how Federation is smashed so long as the smash is
accomplished.
Agent - General Playford, who
returns to Adelaide in May, is reput-
edly the best - dressed Australian in
London. All the same, “Honest Tom”
sticks to his market-garden business in
Adelaide. In the long run there is more
money in cabbages than politics, as
Graham Berry found out.
Dear Bulletin.—A Sydney
paper snorts disgustedly over the fact
that under the present Factories Act
an inspector is at liberty to clank into
a church or convent if he suspects a
factory to exist there. And what-for-
no ? The law can clank into any church
or convent in pursuit of the criminal
who murders men otherwise ; and why
not after the one who sweats them to
death ’—Hermes.
H. : “ The Federal Convention
dealt with Protection, a mere skirmish.
The most vital aspect was not touched
upon, though Turner or Berry would
soon have broached it in a serious
debate. Indeed, Turner has wrought
it out effectually, this national view of
Protection, as bracing up a people,
helping to create a national sentiment.
How often the question has been ar-
gued, without this occurring to the
Liberals. Yet it is the only plank with
the defenders of the system in America,
Germany, and France. This is the
overwhelming thing to urge for Protec-
tion by All-Australia against the world.
No bonds like the financial, the steel in
preference to the silk.”
Melbourne Evening Herald’s
yell about the treasonableness and blas-
phemy of Vic. Govt.’s action in buying
cheap Yankee steel-rails instead of com-
paratively dear English ones, makes
people tired. For it raises the ques-
tion : why is the Herald printed on
cheap Yankee paper (a penny a pound
less 5 per cent, discount) instead of on
English paper at a penny and one-
sixteenth per pound ? How much is the Herald
proprietary itself prepared to sacrifice for the
“ Einpiah ” in the matter of dividends ?
There will probably be a split in Vic.
Labor-party over the Federation question. Tren-
with is in favor, while the bulk of the others are
either against it or silent.
‘ 1 Premier Turner has pressed a button in
Melbourne that has set a funeral-bell tolling in
London for the coming down of the Imperial
Institute. Good luck to his backbone,”
writes The Bulletin’s London corres-
pondent, “ for it will want staying to
meet the storm. Victoria’s refusal
to be any longer blackmailed for
the maintenance of the gorgeous
sepulchre known as the ‘ Free-
masons’ Home ’ presages a
scurried flight of the other gol-
den-crested cockatoos from
this leafless, fruitless tree
that only gives support to
the grubs inside it. A
soul - touching appeal,
most probably in Tummy
Wales’s name, is to be
made at once to Turner,
P.C., to fly back to the
Imperial perch, and
Australian officials
here will,mostly back
the appeal for the sake
of favors to be curried.
This action of G.T.’s is
the wisest, timeliest,
and strongest thing he has
yet done as a political
pilot—a thing that Gillies
and Patterson, with all their
bluster, would never have
dared to do, even if they had possessed genuine
Australian sympathies.”
The last hasn’t been heard of the Bulimba
Sunday-labor case. In this Nicholson, Melb,
P.M., decided that sailors didn’t come within the
protection of the Sunday Act of Charles 11., and
accordingly imprisoned them till the rising of the
court for refusing to put out to sea on Sunday.
An order to review has been obtained, and it is to
be hoped a harsh decision will soon be put right.
If not, fresh legislation is immediately wanted on
the subject.
Among the Sydney dailies the S.M.
Herald and Evening News are the only ones
which are honest and patriotic enough and suffi-
ciently Australian to give their cordial support to
Eederation. The Star was lukewarm all through;
and the shameful ratting of “ President ” Bus-
boss Carey’s Daily Telegraph— which turned
almost in a day from a cordial supporter of
Eederation to a bitter enemy—was a quite unex-
pected piece of treachery.
Melbourne Age on Federation :
She will be deprived of the power to further contest
with New South Wales for the Riverina trade, because
she will not be permitted to make special concessions.
For instance, the ordinary rate for the carriage of dumped
wool from Echuca to Melbourne is 355. 6d. per ton and
undumped 425., but wool coming from north of the Mur-
rurnbidgee to Echucads charged only 225. Wool coming
by river from the Darling or any of its tributaries is
charged 265. ; while the clips of the Murrumbidgee and
the wool from stations south of that river to a point half-
way between the Murrumbidgee and the Murray pay 305.,
south of that down to the Murray the same rates are
charged as to growers on the other side of the border.
Then the Age goes on to curse Eederation because
it carries with it uniform railway rates —in other
words, in implies that the Victorian railways
must not carry N.S.W, wool at about half the
rates which they levy on the Vic. wool-grower,
but must either reduce the rates to their own
people or raise them to the people across the
border. And the Age actually wants the Vic-
torians to throw Eederation overboard rather
than sacrifice the blessed privilege of carrying the
other fellow’s wool at about half the rate which
they pay on their own.
A vigorous three-cornered game is now
being played by the few wide-awakes who boss
the Land-of-Sleep-a-Lot. The prize is the Chief-
Justiceship, rendered vacant by Lambert Dob-
son’s demise. The choice apparently lies be-
tween Alfred Dobson, Justice Dodds, and ex-
Attorney-General Inglis Clark. The Dobson
faction is strong, but their political backers are
in Opposition, with Inglis Clark as leader. Dodds
is Acting-C.J., pro. tern., but is spoken of as being
socially “ impossible ” for the permanency, though
heavily backed by Masonic-newspaper influence.
Attorney-General Urquhart could appoint him-
self, but isn’t quite built that way. The vacancy
caused by the elevation of Alfred Dobson to
the C.J.-ship would not be a bad fit for Urqu-
hart. But, then, Braddon may desire to remove
Inglis Clark from the Opposition leadership, in
which case Urquhart’s chance of stepping into
the Solicitor-Generalship would be as remote as
the historic Buckley’s. But according to the
special Act of last session, no third Judge
can be appointed without the consent of Par-
liament, which consent cannot be obtained
until the Acting-Chief-Justice will have shown
his ability to fill the post. And as Dodds’
rumored social “impossibility” should be no
greater bar to his holding the C.J.-ship than to
his success as a puisne Judge, this paper is in-
clined to believe that John Stokell Dodds is
the future Chief-Justice of the Land of Bad
Dreams.

A wail from Cloncurry : —“ No vegetables,
no fruit; mails many days late; carriers with beer
stuck up on the road by the rains ; plenty of scan-
dal ; no beef, no mutton, but a fair allowance of
nanny-goat! ”
The marriage of Lady Coventry to Prince
Dhuleep Singh is rather a hard nut for the Eng-
lish aristocracy to crack. The prince is just as
black and odorous as the average Indian hawker.
When filthy lucre is needed to rescue the family
heirlooms from pawn and the ancient name from
the list of defaulters, ye olde nobilitie is appa-
rently color-blind and nose-plugged.
“ Long arm,” again. Two years ago, at
Georgetown, N.Q., a butcher suicided by shooting
himself with a gun. Exactly two years later his
successor, at same time and place, was shifting
jhis gun from his cart when it went off and shot
him dead also.
The young seaman who fell from the Ar-
dencraig's main-royal-yard at Stockton (N.S.W.),
the other day, must have been well put together
to escape with a dislocated knee. The terrific
jerk of a sudden pull-up by the foot, after falling
about 125ft., ought to have dragged the leg out of
a Sandow. «■

To the Members of the Australian
Mutual Provident Society.
LADIES AND GENTLEMENMy second
term of office as a Director of your Society
will terminate at the ensuing Annual Meeting,
and I now respectfully beg to intimate that I shall
be a Candidate for one of the seats then vacant.
I shall be glad of a renewal of the confidence
which I have so long had the honor to hold, and
assuring you that no effort on my part shall be
wanting to justify a continuance of the same.—
Yours faithfully,
JOSEPH ABBOTT.
March 3lst, 1898.
To the Members of the AUSTRALIAN
MUTUAL PROVIDENT SOCIETY,
LADIES AND GENTLEMEN,-I beg to in-
form you that I am a CANDIDATE for
RE-ELECTION to the Board of your Society at
the coming Annual Meeting, and, if you think
that my past services as a Director justify me in
asking for your support, I shall feel honored by
your giving me your vote. j R HILL.
