SPOUTING NOTIONS.
Stoddart s departed Eleven didn’t bat up to
its English “ form,” considering that batting-averages,
by Australians or Britishers, can be fairly expected to
run much higher in this country than in damp, bumpy-
wicketed Hingland. That’s certain. As to the extent
to which their “failure” was forced upon them by the
local bowling and fielding, nobody can speak with in-
controvertible argument. Nor would the question be
settled by the immediate sending ’Ome of an Australian
team that included Jones, Trumble, Noble, Howell, and
M'Leod, unless the five bowlers named were to repeat
their recent successes. If they didn’t carry the All-
England wickets before them, there would be reasonable
cause for complaining they were “out of form.” Nothing
is certain—except figures—in these Anglo-Australian
cricket calculations. Figures, thus far, have shown that
the best Australians at ’Ome are stronger in bowling and
weaker in batting than the best English teams, whereas
this season’s performances on their native soil show the
Australians to be stronger than their opponents in both
departments of the game. That’s good enough for the
kangaroo to swear by for the present.
* * • *
We haven’t heard the last of the famous bet-
ting cases which raised the question as to whether bet-
ting in enclosures on racecourses is illegal. The case of
Hawke v. Dunne settled that it was illegal. But a
friendly suit between parties interested in the English
Kempton Park racecourse resulted in a decision of a
higher court that it was not illegal, and that Hawke v.
Dunne was not above suspicion. It seems now that there
is to be an appeal to the Lords from that decision, but
t he parties, being both satisfied with the Court of Appeal
decision, are not hurrying the matter up. According to
latest news, however, the appeal is really coming on, so
that we shall probably know on the authority of the
highest court in the Empire whether Flemington race-
course is to be shut up before next Cup-day.
* * » *
A plebiscite of experts as to the best batsman in
the English team would possibly declare for Hayward.
M'Laren had abnormal luck, and Ranji, with a colored
gentleman's lack of balance and ballast, often lost his
wicket through striving to be “flash.” The Englishmen
should find chief cause for regret in the fact that Hay-
ward did not “strike form” earlier.
• * » «
Ranji announced in Adelaide that he and Jones
were again good friends—the latter had forgiven him.
But it didn’t look like it when Jonah clean-bowled the
Indian in Adelaide. Jonah turned three somersaults in
manifestation of his delight.
* * • *
S.A. cricket eleven ought to be altered next
season. Men like W. Giffen, Reedman, and M’Kenzie,
who are almost regular batting failures, ought to stand
down for the many rising juniors coming on. N.S.W.
taught the other provinces a lesson two years ago which
they are slow in taking to heart.
* * * *
As showing the batting failure of the English
tour, note that Wainwright, in the last match, was the
first pro. to make a century in important matches. Hay-
ward missed it by 4 runs in Sydney.
* * • *
The moral of Stoddart and co s failure is on the
surface. English Elevens for Australia should be chosen,
as far as possible, from young professional and matured
(but not too old) amateurs. A young pro.—like Brown, of
yore—has strong business inducements to keep steady,
do his best, and get his name up ; a young amateur is
likely to shirk his responsibilities and content himself
with a good time. True, Stoddart hadn’t much young
professional bowling talent to pick from, but he might
have selected at least one promising boy from the many
who hope to live by cricket. Amateurs Druce and Mason
undoubtedly failed through lack of earnestness. Briggs,
and Stoddy himself, who is much the same as a pro., are
nearly' worn out; Hearne has grown weary; Wain-
wright’s excuse is said to have been ill-health—the only
reai bit of bad luck the Englishmen can complain of.
M’Laren fell off as his wedding-day drew near, but the
happy event was on the programme anyway, and Mac.
did verv well under the circs.
* » * *
In first-class matches this season Briggs took
only 12 wickets, at the awful cost of 65 runs a-piece, yet
for all matches he had the respectable average of 26.17.
Which suggests that the best Australian batting is now
much better than it was three years ago, as regards
Briggs’s bowling. Country teams found the little man
as difficult as before. Richardson this time made a slight
improvement on his previous average, although he was
less destructive in the test-matches. Hearne proved
himself a fairly good substitute for Peel in eleven-a-side
games, and Mason, being granted a reasonable show to
distinguish himself at the end of the tour, got through
with top average on the English side —25.35 for 20 wickets.
Hayward was a disappointment merely ; Hirst and Wain-
wright calamitous failures, the latter taking the cake with
1 wicket for exactly 250. The figures altogether point to
injudicious captaincy as a factor in the English bowling
failure. Briggs was allowed to bowl 1800 balls, whilst
Mason put in less than 1100, and Hirst bowled 300 balls
more than Hayward, whose 15 wickets cost 113 runs less
than the left-hander’s 9 wickets.
As for the Englishmen’s batting in test-matches,
twelve of them played in two or more tests ; eleven Aus-
tralians (including M'Kibbin) did likewise. Of these 23
representative players three Australians headed the
crowd, and four others were among the leading ten. Nine
Englishmen finished among the last 13, and the seventh
Australian on the list (Trumble) was nine runs per inn-
ings in advance of the fourth Englishman (Hirst). Take
six men as follows from either team—Gregory, Iredale,
Kelly, Trumble, Noble, and Trott versus Hayward, Hirst,
Druce, Storer, Wainwright, and Mason. The Australian
six scored a general average of 65 rums per innings more
than their antagonists.
• • • *
Averages in first-class matches: Hill, 1196 runs,
av. 66'44 ; Ranji, 1157 runs, av. 60'89; MaoLaren, 1037
runs, av. 54'57 ; Darling, 978 runs, av. 51.47. Bowling—
Roche, 33 wickets for 684 runs, av. 20'72; Jones, 76
wickets for 1653 runs, av. 21 ‘75. Then come Noble and
Mason. As showing English bowling weakness Mason,
who heads Stoddart’s bowlers, comes fifth on the list, and
Richardson and Hearne are eleventh and twelfth re-
spectively. In batting, the first Englishman, after Ranji
and MacLaren who are second and third, is Hayward who
is eleventh. Storer is fifteenth. English critics can
explain these facts away.
* * * *
English cricket-critics should be satisfied now.
The last match of Stoddart’s team, against S.A., again
exposed the utter plainness of the bowling. Even Jack
Lyons, almost a back number, tickled Richardson and co.
to the tune of 79, and, when Hill and Darling got together
in the second innings, they seemed to just do as they
liked, Mason being the only one to keep a length. At the
commencement of the S.A. second innings, Hill wanted
86 to reach Ranji’s total for first-class matches and also
secure top average, and, by making 124 not out, did it
easily. In fact, only Ranji’s luck has put him where he
is in the averages, Hill, Darling, and MacLaren having
shown finer cricket all through.
S.A. wanted 67 runs to win at time, with eight
wickets in hand, and Clem. Hill going strong ! No wonder
Stoddart and co. were glad to get on the boat.
Lightning Jonah’s feat of taking 14 wickets for
237 runs, despite his half-crippled state, again shows that
he is the most consistent trundler in Australia.
The English cricketers left Adelaide yester-
day by R.M.S. Ormuz. Major Wardill, who travelled
with them, denied that there had been any disturbances
amongst the members of the team.” That is a yarn.
Wasn’t it a “ disturbance ” when that pro. “plugged”
that amateur—plugged him hard, very hard —for in-
sinuating that the pro. had intentionally missed a catch ?
* » * •
There were no less than three centuries scored
at S3 r dney, by Paddington against Redfern, last Saturday
—Trumper 133, Noble 138, and Meagher 108. Young
Trumper’s figures for his club are phenomenal—B2, 123,
125, 85, 120 not out, 191 not out, and 133.
* * * •
The N.S.W. wheelmen’s meet at Sydney Agri-
cultural Ground last Saturday was a tremendous success
from a racing standpoint, and deserved to be a bigger
success in the way of “ gate.”
Parsons’ preliminary to his first race evoked only
a few spasmodic clappings, as if the more decent of the
crowd felt it incumbent to welcome his re-appearance. He
is no longer a champion ; he is Parsons—not Jack.
A great run by Clinton from the bell in the
fourth heat of the March Stakes was rewarded as such
runs usually are, by the rider being beaten on the post.
This time it was Mutton who snatched the victory.
A. F. Chapman is a rider whose vibrations and
spasms are so violent when racing, that he seems to try
as much as all the other men put together. In the fifth
heat of the March Stakes Chapman’s pursuit of Gerster,
the easy winner, was almost ludicrous.
Gallant Ken Lewis, whose top-notch sprint is
somehow never seen in Sydney, went under in the third
heat of the Sydney Plate to Body, Barker, and Megson.
Nobody could extend Walne in the final of the
Sydney Plate mile. Half-a-mile from home he flew out
like a freed pigeon, and sailed round the course and down
the straight with beautiful ease. Nothing has been seen
like it in Sydney cycling since Zimmerman raced away
from the stage-coaches who tried to sprint him home in a
mile on Sydney Cricket Ground. Walne also gave them
a terrific go in the first heat of Sydney Plate. He shot
home in a dead straight line with no one able to hang
near him.
Then in the Five Miles Walne wouldn’t go ;
and Green hung behind him. The field got away; the
field lapped them. If Green were behind Walne on a
tandem he couldn’t stick there more systematically. The
racing board made the pair run an extra three miles as
penalty. Green hung to Walne just the same all through ;
but in the sprint home he was beaten all out.
Green, the general who takes all points to win,
has just about exploited the game of hanging on to
Walne’s wheel. Green’s tactics on the track are always
the same ; he picks the best man, hangs just behind him
all the time, and challenges him in the home sprint.
Walne threw his cap off and jumped for the
half-lap-home sprint in the Sydney Plate, three miles ;
Green, being on Walne’s hind wheel, jumped response,
battled at his top speed round the turn and half-way down
the straight, then, with a few final kicks, sent his wheel
in front.
They were a young and very hungry-looking
lot who came forth to battle for the amateur Two Mile
Handicap. This tin-pot brigade that races only for glory
seems to have been weaned early and to have been brought
up on condensed milk. The spectacle of 34 such youths
struggling round the track recalls the poor boys whom a
certain pug-master used to “kid” into “ bashing ” one
another on Saturday' nights. Those green unfortunates
used to get the glory, and the pug-master the cash. With
the cyclists the amateurs get the glory, and the League
the cash. There is always a doctor in attendance at the
League’s meets. Couldn’t he prescribe some roast beef
and pudding? In the good old days of the beastly fight-
ing business one strong, hearty medico, on seeing two
much-advertised “champions” come into the ring, took
the entrepreneur aside and said, “ You might have given
the poor little wretches a few good meals before getting
them to fight.”
L. M. Jackson, the big, solid youngster who
won the last A.N.A., was slow to get moving, and when
he did move he was still very slow. When it came to
sprinting he couldn’t hold the field at all.
• * • •
Vedette, the Maorilander, who essays the an-
nexation of the Doncaster mile, is another of those lively
“hacks” for which M.L. is so famous. It was only the
other day that he was permitted to join “aristocratic”
company (writes a Bulletin correspondent), and three
runaway victories in hot time determined the Sydney
trip. Vanguard’s son has a picnic parcel to carry in
Bst. 21b., and whatever beats him (assuming that he gets
a clear run) will have to go a perisher. In his last public
mile flutter (7.12) he cantered home, hard held, in 1.42.
Three trials for the Doncaster subsequently—l.4o,l.39f,
1.40. Hot 1
A few thousands will go Maoriland way should
Vedette swamp his Doncaster field. He is reckoned a
greater possibility than was Russley, who won a few
years back. M.L. books are backing the “Coaster”
themselves in Sydney, and are laying prohibitive odds to
clients.
• * m *
There is a dearth of proved stayers among the
24 acceptors for the valuable Sydney Cup, and so, with
the thumping weight of 9st. 121 b., Old Battalion is being
well backed at a short price, whilst the three-year-old
Amberite, with the steadier of Bst. 121 b., is even a better
favorite. But such great fancies may be downed by some
obscure outsider—like Phillip or The Sketch.
» * * *
The “go ” of the season in M.L. saw the down-
fall hy a nose of Yaldhurst Stead’s champion, Gold Medal-
list, in the classic Hawkes Bay Stakes. Hon. Ormond’s
Daunt (a Dreadnought) beat Gold Medallist last year —a
“fluke,” ’twas said. This time the pair ding-donged
from flag-fall, and even Derrett’s superior horsemanship
failed to avert defeat by a “ snort.” ’Twas Derrett who
piloted Carbine in the remembersome V.R.C. Derby when
he hit “ Old Jack ” and was pipped by Ensign.
•Jr * * *
Plain Bill, the Maoriland hurdler, who may
visit Sydney shortly, jumped at four pops from the best
“hack” stick-flier to the best “racehorse” in the
country. A pound under 13st. is his latest handicap in
top company. He is a grandson of Musket.
* * * *
The ancient but evergreen Hawkesbury Race
Club, N.S.W., brings off a meeting to-day (Sat.), and, as
there is a “sweep” on the big event, large fields and
good racing may be looked for. The Clarendon Handicap
looks like falling to Amourette or Nineveh, while Senior
Wrangler or Essex should succeed in landing the Nursery
Handicap. Gozoczar, The Sketch, Syerla, and The Tola
appear the best treated of the many entrants for the
Autumn Handicap, and, all being well, the first-named
should about win. The Welter may fall to Antimony, in
the probable absence of old Pharamond, who will win a
race in the near future.
• * * *
Sydney followers of the game were sorry, but
not surprised, to hear that Peter Jackson—in his day, the
greatest exponent of the modern style of fighting—had
been practically defeated in three rounds by the lusty
young Jeffries. Jackson ever required to be in the very
nick of condition to show at his best; and his Sydney
friends knew well that he could not be in anything like
condition with a few weeks’ v'ork after years of high-liv-
ing and absence from the ring.
One of the most smellful incidents connected
with Melb. cycling—and that is saying a lot—was the
publication of photos purporting to show the finish of the
10 mile Aust. Championship in certain southern journals.
One paper shows Body winning by at least 6in. and the
other corroborates the judges who gave the race to Ken
Lewis. Which is right, Heaven only knows, but one is
guilty of a glaring “ fake.” “ The apparatus cannot lie,”
hut a skilful draughtsman can alter the position of the
white line across the track in about 15secs.
♦ * * *
The A.J.C. Committee has determined not to
proceed with the erection of a new number-hoard at
Randwick, the excuse being that the only ground avail-
able for its site is too far from the grand-stand to be of
avail to its aristocratic occupants, and so the poor flatite
will have to go, as heretofore, without any adequate
knowledge of the game he has paid to witness. This is a
paltry excuse of the committee, as the space between
stand and Leger-reserve is admirably suited for such a
board, which, put at a correct angle, could be seen, not
only from the two stands, but by the “bob” public to
whom it would be a real boon.
* • * *
The Queensland champion two-year-old, Corona-
tion, together with Borgia, has been bought by a
Northerner who intends sending them Ome with a view
of picking up some of the racing plums that are
sanguinely supposed to be had for the plucking. This
sudden craze for racing at ’Ome, no doubt engendered by
Merman’s success, will be found before long to be most
costly and disappointing.
* » * *
The new “ ride-’em-out ” rule should at least
permit of a horse being eased when it is clear that the
jockey has exhausted all hope of victory and gone beyond
the point where a pull might possibly deceive the handi-
capper. In the Brunswick Stakes at Flemington, the
other Saturday, the pony Fitzroy led the field until
Parisienne came along and “ smothered ” the little fellow,
whose jockey thereupon ceased to flog his beaten animal.
But, because this happened right under the notice of the
stewards, they sent for the jockey after the race and
severely censured him for not having struggled to the
bitter end, on the chance of finishing second. Why not
have censu:ed some of the boys who, from the start, had
done their best to finish “ nowhere?”
* * * *
Cordang, the Dutch cyclist, who was cook on a
steamer ere taking to the wheel, is credited with receiving
£ll2O for his 24 hours’ record ride. Better game than
ladling out lobscouse.
* * » •
Menier, the big French chocolate-man, is “a
whale” at racing. He has at present 161 thoroughbreds
in active work—s 3 of them three-year-olds.
» * '» *
Baronet Rupert Clarke had another sweetener,
at Kvneton (Vic.), with his good-looking colt, Ranfurly.
Racing, as a rule, is made sweet for the novice owner. In
verdure clad the Turf appears delightful to Sirupert’s
eye.
* * » *
Sec. Byron Moore should be presented with a
pair of white gloves by the V.R.C. Not a single case of
welshing occurred on the Hill this year. This is stated to
be a record.
• • • *
The first day of the N.S.W. Wheelmen’s meet
was another big victory for Beeston-Humber and Swift
cycles fitted with Dunlop Tyres. These machines won
every event but one. In the Sydney Plate, the great
scratch race of the day, Walne came first on a Swift and
Green second on a Beeston-Humber.
In more than one Australian province there
is still the belief that in every case it is desirable
to have as senior or junior counsel a relative of
the presiding Judge, especially when questions
of allowing costs arise.
Rev. Father Carey, said to have been
rudely requested, the other day, by genial
N.S. Wales Justice-Minister Gould, to “ mind
his own business ” —is such a general favorite
that one of the victims of the recent Moore
Park (Sydney) shooting fray—though not R.C.
—requested Father Carey to be brought with-
out delay. The priest, upon arrival, natur-
ally inquired if the injured man wished to
come into the true fold, and was a bit broken up
at the answer—“ I don’t care a damn about the
Church—l want to follow you! ”
Frank Rogers’ “ microscopic friend,” the
grimly-conscientious C. G. Heydon, whose ap-
pointment to an acting-N.S.W. Supreme Court
Judgeship has been officially notified, has had
numerous State plums during the Reid regime.
Mr. Heydon as a criminal Judge would, in
the opinion of many, be as objectionable as
Judge Windeyer, although he is quite a different
kind of person. Tory to the last degree, cold as
an icicle, and belonging to the Trappist heaven
rather than to this base and fleshly earth, his
mental bias is that of a prosecutor, and renders
him quite incapable of making allowance for
frailties of human nature. The Bulletin doesn’t
want the virtuous Heydon to be taken from the
Bar and made into a Judge. It would prefer to
see him—in his own good time, of course—trans-
lated straight to Paradise per fiery chariot.
The boss woman of the N.S.W. woman-
suffragists sends in this protest against the land-
aninkum-tax :
I beg to inform you that I pay the Land-tax under
strong protest—l object to be taxed whilst I have no vote.
Men are not taxed without representation, why, then,
should women be so unjustly taxed, seeing that they are
in need of the protection of a vote far more than men ?
In my municipality I am taxed, but I am content, seeing
that I have the right to vote for the election of the aider-
men and auditors who control municipal affairs. I pro-
test against aliens and blackfellows being allowed a vote
if they please, whilst I and all the women of New South
Wales are absolutely without a voice in choosing those
Parliamentary representatives who have the power to
tax us, to make laws which we must obey, and to deal
with far more important interests than those which confer
a vote upon us in municipal affairs. Therefore, I repeat,
I pay this Land-tax under strong protest, it being un-
righteous and unjust to tax those who have no vote.
Death—God’s bailiff.
“ The prisoner, however, is still at large.*

‘ ‘The Prettiest Easter Show In Sydney, ”
is the title claimed for the beautiful display made
by those up-to-date jewellers, “ The Goldsmiths
and Silversmiths’ Alliance,” of 90 King-street. A
visit to this enchanting establishment of “ White
and Gold ” will amply repay country visitors at
Show-time. There will be seen a wonderful col-
lection of all the very latest London and Continen-
tal novelties of the jewellers’art. The “ Alliance”
prides itself on the great and varied assortment of
novelties which it is constantly introducing to
Australia, and the prices charged are a marvel of
cheapness, all goods being sold at London rates.*
Prospectus of the Victoria Klondike G.M.
Co. appears in this issue. Capital, 35,000 shares
(ill) paid up to 3s. on allotment. The vendors,
whose prices is 10,000 f.p. shares and =B2OOO cash
offer a property of 40 acres at Gordons (15 miles
from Ballarat) and a lot of good work. The main
shaft, 415 ft. deep, is estimated to be worth ,£l5OO
to the company. Another shaft is 60ft. down,
and from the reef at this point trial crushings of
88 tons have yielded 570 z. The estimate that
4dwt. per ton will pay when a battery arrives is,
perhaps, too sanguine, but the spec, looks suffi-
ciently tempting, anyhow. The Vic. Klondike
G.M. Co. should have no trouble in getting afloat.
Messrs Gordon and Gotch, of all over
Australasia, issue their 29th annual number of the
“Australian Handbook ’’—which covers Aus-
tralia, Tasmania, Maoriland, Fiji and Papua, so
that it is themostcomprehensiveannual publication
of its kind issued in this country. Its strongest
point as a work of reference lies in the detailed
information it gives regarding every town and
village and reasonable-sized hamlet in the nine
provinces. Any one who wants to know
anything about Yarrawonga, or Yarraweyah,
•or Breakfast Creek, or Kaitaia, or any other such
place can refer to this volume and find out all
about it population, industries, newspapers,
churches, hotels, public buildings, how you get
there, how you get away again, fares, scenery,
local Rechabites, parks, rivers, banks, and other
things. The detailed information supplied about
a great number of perfectly new towns in Wes-
tralia shows how carefully the work is kept up to
date.

Of the
VICTORIA KLONDIKE GOLD-MINING
COMPANY,
Gordons, Victoria.
To be Registered as a No-liability Company.

EACH.
10,000 shares fully paid up to be allotted the ven-
dors, also the sum of =£2ooo cash.
25,000 shares are offered to the public at Is. 6d.
per share on application and Is. 6d. on allot-
i ment.
I Proceeds, less brokerage and flotation expenses
(not to exceed =£soo), to be placed to the credit
i of the company.
I The company may be considered floated when
15,000 shares have been applied for.
I As 10,000 contributing shares have already been
applied for, early application will be necessary,
I and shares will be allotted in priority of appli-
| cation.
PROVISIONAL DIRECTORS :
! The Hon. Pharez Phillips, M.L.C.
Wm. Holyoak, Esq., merchant, Melbourne
J. H. Thompson, Esq., merchant, Melbourne
i Arthur Robinson, Esq., Commercial Agency,
34 Queen-street, Melbourne
| Henry Thompson, Esq., mine manager, Mel-
bourne
E. M‘Robert, Esq. (director South Germans),
Melbourne
BANKERS:
London Bank of Australia Limited, Melbourne
BROKERS :
Stanhope O’Connor, Esq., Exchange, Mel-
bourne
J. D. Woolcott, Esq. Exchange, Ballarat
S. H. M‘Go wan, Esq., Exchange, Bendigo
H. Robinson, Esq., Exchange, Maryborough
SOLICITOR :
Herbert H. Oakley, 95 Queen-street, Melbourne.
LEGAL MANAGER (pro. tem.):
J. W. H. Evans (Evans, Smith, and Co.),
11 and 13 Baring-chambers, 54 Market-street,
Melbourne.
Application for shares will be received by any of
the above-named brokers, and at the office of
Messrs. Evans, Smith, and Co., 54 Market-street,
where samples of the gold may be seen and further
information of the mines obtained.
The company is being formed for the purpose of
acquiring Leases Nos. 2919 and 3003 (P.P. Bal-
larat), situated at Gordons, 15 miles east from
Ballarat, comprising an area of 40 acres, more or
less, through which four parallel highly auriferous
quartz reefs traverse the whole length of the pro-
perty.
One of these is a continuation of the famous
Black Horse line, at Egerton. Gold has been won
from the Black Horse mine valued .£470,022, out
out of which £178,000 has been paid in dividends.
The Egerton has won gold valued £780,482, and
paid £310,729 in dividends. An additional 30
acres adjoining has been applied for on behalf of
the company.
Three of these reefs are known as Kangaroo
Bob, Fisher’s, and Peterson’s.
The Kangaroo Bob averaged 2 feet wide, and
yielded as high as 30 ounces per ton.
The Fisher’s reef was found to average in width
7 feet, and returned yields equal to two ounces per
ton.
Peterson’s reef is about 4 feet wide, and gave an
average return of Bdwt. of gold per ton from the
surface to the 400-feet level. This reef was fol-
lowed 20 feet down from the 400-feet level, and a
trial crushing returned 15dwt. to the ton.
The main shaft is 415 feet deep, in good con-
dition, timbered with the best of material, and
ladders fixed from top to bottom, is 10 feet by 4
feet in the clear, divided into three compartments,
and is worth over £l5OO to the company.
There is a shaft sunk 60ft., from which a cross-
cut has been driven 40ft., where a reef was met
with, varying in width from 6 to 10ft., carrying
gold all through. Two trial crushings have been
taken from this reef—s 6 tons yielded re-
torted gold, 32 tons yielded 270 z. 15dwt. retorted
gold ; 4dwt. per ton will pay when a battery is
upon the ground.
There are fully 100 tons of mullock upon the
surface, carrying payable gold.
Experts recommend that a crosscut be put in
200 ft. deep in the main shaft, which should strike
the main reef after driving 75ft., thereby giving
the company 200 ft. backs from this level.
The contract price for driving is less than Bs.
per foot.
The value of the gold is £4 2s. 3d. per ounce.
The provisional directors have every confidence
in recommending the venture, and it is expected
that almost immediately the machinery has been
erected that the mine will become dividend-
paying.
Intending investors are invited to personally
inspect the property, which can easily be accom-
plished in one day, it being within 1| miles of the
Gordons railway station.
Samples of the eastern lode may be seen at the
office of the legal manager (pro. tem.), 34 Market-
street.
ME. ARTHUK’S REPORT.
To Messrs. Evans, Smith and Co., 13 Baring-chambers,
Market-street, Melbourne.
Dear Sirs, —Having examined the property described in
Lease No. 2919, at Gordon’s, by your request, I have
much pleasure in submitting my opinion of the property.
There are four distinct reefs running through the lease,
A shaft has been sunk 400 ft. ; it is well timbered and in
good condition, has three compartments, and I consider
it a valuable asset for the future working of the mine.
At the bottom of the lower level, I am informed on good
authority, there is a reef carrying gold equal to 15dwt. to
the ton. I was unable to go down as the shaft contains a
considerable quantity of water.
The most important feature at the mine is the last
shaft sunk to a depth of 60ft. from the surface. A drive
was put in at 40ft., and struck a new reef, which has been
driven on 50ft. to the north, and 70ft. to the south. The
size of the reef, I should say, is from 6ft. to 10ft. wide,
carrying gold all through. A trial crushing of 56 tons
was taken from about 3ft. of the reef, and yielded 30oz.
of smelted gold. I took several prospects from the
bottom of the drive, with the object of testing whether
it went down. I found gold in all of it, the best being at
the back of the tunnel. With a crushing plant upon the
ground I consider the mullock would be payable.
The reef is very much broken up, and I would recom-
mend that a crosscut be put in from the 400 ft. shaft, at
about 200 ft. deep, to strike the lode now being worked in
the new shaft. I then believe it would be more defined
and most probably richer. By the broken appearance of
the reef, I am of opinion that the alluvial in the neighbor-
hood was shed from the reef. One of the best leads in
the district takes its start about 20 yards from the present
shaft. The reef has never heen worked upon the pro-
perty, and I consider it a new find, and a continuation of
the famous Egerton line of reef; and, with good manage-
ment and properly opened up, I would recommend it as a
good venture with great possibilities.
There are 30 to 40 tons of stone at present at grass,
which 1 would recommend crushing at once, and should
think it would turn out from 12dwt. to lddwt. per ton.
A quantity of mullook companies at the 1800 ft. and 1300 ft.
levels would to the ton.
Yours faithfully,
R. ARTHUR.
Moonie Ponds, 9th February, 1898.
Since writing this report, 32 tons were crushed for 270 z.
15dwt. gold.
EXTRACTS FROM REPORTS.
Mr. Thomas M‘Cluskey, who has been mining for 31
years, writes :
I am thoroughly acquainted with the features of the
lodes on the property. Crushings from the Kangaroo Bob
yield from 20oz. to 30oz. to the ton.
Peterson’s lode is about the centre of the lode forma-
tion. From the 300 ft. to the 400 ft. levels 6000 tons of
stone were crushed for a return of 3000 oz. of gold. The
lode was followed underfoot at the 400 ft. level, and the
trial crushing returned 15dwt. to the ton.
The increase in the yields was most marked the deeper
the lode was worked, and 1 feel quite positive the lode
will at deeper levels prove highly auriferous and return
splendid dividends. There is little doubt but that the
famous reefs worked by the Black Horse United and
Egerton companies at the 1800 ft. and 1300 ft. levels would
be found in your area.
Mr. Wm. Sterritt. M.A.1.M.E., writes:—
There are four parallel well-known and highly auri-
ferous reefs the whole length of the property.
The Kangaroo Bob reef averaged 2ft. in width, and re-
turned yields over 20oz. to the ton.
Fisher’s reef is 7ft. wide and 350 ft. west of Kangaroo
Bob. It averaged 3oz. to the ton to the 200 ft. level. The
early prospectors made big wages by dollying the stone.
Peterson's reef is 250 ft. east of Kangaroo Bob. Its
width, about 4ft., gave an average return of lOdwt. from
the surface to the 400 ft. level. This lode was followed
underfoot a distance of 20ft. from the 400 ft. level. Trial
crushing returned 15dwt. The lode being the whole
width of the face, and gold-bearing throughout, will re-
turn dividends from the start.
Your eastern line of reef has been driven for 150 ft. at
40ft. from the surface. The reef is nearly 12ft. wide,
and gold-bearing throughout. The gold is free and good
quality, easily saved by ordinary milling. 3dwt. per ton
would pay on this line of reef. The reef is well defined
between slate and sandstone walls, of a permanent ap-
pearance, and has an underlay to the east of Ift. in 4ft.
My opinion is that further south .you will find a very rich
shoot of gold, as an immensely rich alluvial gutter was
worked 50ft. further south, and I consider it one of the
best shows in this district.
I may say that the pleasing and satisfactory feature of
the lodes is that the gold is not found in them in pockets,
but is distributed throughout, so that in working them
there will be no waste, the whole of the stone being fit
for the battery.
I am of the opinion that as deeper levels are opened up
the reefs will get richer, this being the characteristic of
the district lode formation of which yours is a part. The
greater portion of the gold obtained in the district has
been got below 400 ft., as in the Black Horse and Egerton,
the best gold in these two mines being obtained at 800 ft.
and 1400 ft. Your reefs being in this same auriferous belt
of country as the foregoing mines, I think your property
is the best show in the district, and have no hesitation in
stating that, properly developed, it will return good
dividends for many years.

The story goes that when Prince Henry of
Prussia visited England recently he hunted the
Prince of Wales about with a special message
from the Kaiser, intended to tone-down the bun-
kum of the Kiel banquet braggadocio. But
Tummy, it is said, dexterously avoided being
ear-wigged on the subject, and now he and
William the Wonderful are “ not playing ” more
than ever.
“ The disclosures in court about the meat-
supply of a swell London caravanserai,” writes
The Bulletin’s London correspondent, “may
lead to a war declaration by some foreign
powers whose representatives were housed therein
as the Queen’s jubilee guests. It now turns out
that two warriors, who for a couple of months
guarded the main entrance, should have been
stationed at the kitchen entrance through which
went all manner of cat’s meat and meat that no
cat would eat, to be fixed up for the menu as filet
de bceuf <1 la viennoise. It appears that a certain
butcher has, through dummies, gradually acquired
most of the hotel shares, and he amply used his
chances. Stale sheep’s plucks, sweetbreads that
were far from sweet, and game that was game.
What a stomach-squirm will travel round at the
idea of a cute tradesman thus getting quadruple
prices for the refuse of the slaughter-house ! ”
Within a year have been killed three sons
of the late Sherbourne Sheppard, one of the
pioneer squatters of Vic. after whom Shepparton
is named. One son was killed in a bicycle road-
race at Werribee (Vic.) ; a second burst a blood-
vessel on the bike and died from the effects ; and
news lately came that the third, a naval lieuten-
ant, has been killed by a panther in Central
Africa. Old Sherbourne Sheppard—he wasn’t
old then —was the central figure of a memorable
row in the early days of Vic. Leaving his station
at Tallygaroopna to the care of “Brab” Purcell,
afterwards of a Melb. horse-bazaar, he took a trip
to his home in Ireland, only to find on his return
in 1850 that things had gone wrong and that one
Khull was in possession of the homestead with
10,000 of his own sheep fattening in the pad-
docks. Sheppard took legal advice, and the advice
was to recapture the station by force. Accord-
ingly, he got together Archie Knight (afterwards
a “cadet”), Albert Le Souef (now director of
Melb. Zoo.), Jack Doherty (afterwards M.L.C.),
Purcell, and a score of good, reliable fighting men.
These, armed with swords and pistols, marched
on Tallygaroopna and made formal demand for
the premises. Khull declined with thanks, and,
a trifle scared at the display, barricaded the home-
stead. Sheppard’s party then set to work with
axes, battered a hole in the wall, and bounced in,
ejecting the Khull party, which was too scared to
retaliate. The next move was to chuck all the
Khull furniture and stores into the bush, and, as
the place had been provisioned for about six
months, the result was that all the blackfellows in
Vic. rolled up sooner or later to participate in the
“ plenty flour and thugar ’’ that was wasting
under the eucalypts. Khull’s 10,000 head of sheep
were driven off to Benalla pound, to be sold by
auction for half nothing, and Sheppard gained his
point. Subsequently Khull took action, and a
lengthy suit resulted in his getting a nominal
verdict, but he couldn’t shift Sheppard.

AUSTRALASIA TO ENGLAND. THE GREAT INSURANCE RACE.

National Mutual Life Association of Australasia.
AS our readers are aware, this old-established and sound colonial office has lately opened a chief
office for Great Britain and Ireland at 75 and 76, Cornhill, London, and districts and agencies
are being established in various parts of the United Kingdom. Already an excellent board of direc-
tors have been formed in London with Sir Saul Samuel, Bart, K.C.M.G., C. 8., as chairman, and the
General Manager for the United Kingdom, Mr. John Munro, an able, scholarly and conscientious in-
surance man, in whose hands the interests and reputation of the Association are absolutely safe.
The association was established in 1869, and at once commenced a new era in Life Assurance by
introducing a non forfeiture clause under which policies could not be lapsed for non-payment of pre-
miums so long as the surrender values were sufficient to pay the premiums. Numerous instances are
cited in the prospectus, which show how beneficiently this considerate and equitable arrangement
has operated. All the best features of the leading insurance offices of the world were kept in view by
the founders of the Association. In 1896 the Mutual Assurance Society of Victoria was absorbed,
with the result that the funds now exceed .£2,500,000. The individuality of Colonel Templeton was
well asserted in the formation of this excellent society, and that both the Colonel and those connected
with him looked upon the office with pride is shown from the following extract as to the reasons for the
formation of the office, published in the prospectus—
The introduction of such a feature alone (the non-forfeiture principle) was sufficient warrant for the establishment of
the Association ; but there were other reason, viz.—To liberalise the general conditions of policies, removing stringent
conditions unfair to members of a Mutual Life Association ; to introduce an improvement in the mode of dividing pro-
fits, the rule adopted by the association being that profits should be divided among members in proportion to their
contributions to the profit fund. The principle of Mutual Assurance were strictly observed from the inception of the
Association, no advantages being given to the first members, who were admitted on exactly the same terms as those
upon which members may now enter.
Thus was established the first office in the world to insert into Life Policies a truly Non-Forfeiture Clause for the
protection of those who might omit to pay their renewal premiums.
Whilst the society is fully abreast of the times in regard to the various departments and the absence
of restrictions antagonistic to the interests of members, the bonus-paying powers are of a high order.
Thus policy No. 12, assuring nominally <£soo, has been in force 23 years, and the reversionary bonuses
added amount to £306 ; policy No. 204 for £5OO has been in force 22 years, and bonuses amounting to
£264 have been added ; policy No. 284, assuring £lOOO, has had, after 21 years, £703 in bonuses added,
increasing the assurance to £1703. We learn that up to the present the reversionary bonuses allotted
to members aggregate the large sum of over £900,000. The results of the year 1897 have been cabled
to London : The new assurances amounted to £1,100,000 with new annual premiums of £33,000. The
income for the year amounted to £450,000, and the funds came to a total of £2,600,000 in addition to
which there is an investment fluctuation fund of £70,000. The society is well established in the
various colonies, and, in addition to the Chief Office Board at Melbourne, there are Boards of Direc-
tors at Sydney, Adelaide, New Zealand, Brisbane, Tasmania, Western Australia, and Cape Town, in
addition to the London Board, to which we have already referred. The society is viewed, from any
and every standpoint, a first-class Life Office, which any agent or other person can, with the fullest
confidence, recommend to his friends. With the spread of imperialism, and the great and increasing
desire to draw the colonies closer and closer to the Mother Country for the mutual good of all, there is
an additional reason why Britishers should give practical proof of their love of the colonies, of that
broad patriotism so frequently accentuated by our modern statesmen, by extending a generous welcome
and patronage to The National Mutual Life Association of Australasia.

“No wonder the beggars try to bolt;
they’re let down too easily when caught; the
maudlin sympathy of civilians encourages them.”
This is printed as a Sydney policeman’s comment
on recent attempts at escape from gaol made by
certain prisoners. “ They’re let down too easily.’’,
A N.S.W. escapee when caught is often triced up
and given 25 lashes ; he is also punished through
his dietary, and remissions earned by previous
good conduct are wiped out. In the early days
even the gallows were called on to punish attempts
at escape. A record of the year 1800 describes
the fate of five convicts who tried to escape from
Sydney stockade. One got 1000 lashes, another
500 lashes, and the rest were sent to the chain*'
gang in iron collars. A policeman’s • life had joy-
ous hours in those days. Escapees were not then
“ let down too easily.”
“An old-time Pressman,” on a narrowly
averted tragedy:—
We hanged a reporter once—at Rockhampton. It was
this way i Joe Lee, shipping reporter on the Northern
Argus, was a Jew—and a very good sort at that. Not
very clever, hut his colossal cheek got him along all right,
and editor Bouoicault loved him like a brother. Well,
Joe and half-a dozen other habitues were in Macallister’s
bar. It was prime drinking weather —always is in the
city of Sin, Sweat, and Sorrow. Also, it was Saturday—
and pay-day. So it went round pretty often. And the
talk got upon hanging, because on the Monday there was
to be an official exhibition thereof. Lee said he believed
a man could train himself so that when he was turned off
he could cheat the hangman. Whereupon a chap named
M‘Kenzie insisted that Joe should show us how it was
done. We got a rope, threw it over a beam in the bar,
fixed up the loop, and trussed up Lee before he had time
to say a prayer. Of course, we didn’t mean to hurt him,
but, as the devil would have it, just as we ran him up the
circus band drove past, and we walked outside to have a
look, all still holding on to the rope, and clean forgetting
Joe, so that, only for Jack Macallister, who jumped over
the bar and cut him down, bringing us all down in turn,
tbere’d have been an inquest right enough, and a verdict
of manslaughter, and cross-headed articles in the papers
about “ brutal practical jokes.” As it was, Joe was black
in the face, and somebody had to open a vein in his
neck. But he was good-natured, and forgave us. In-
deed, I think he was rather proud of the experience,
because years afterwards, when he and I were on Sydney
Evening News, and Lester were hanged, Joe
asked to be sent to the function, because, as he told the
chief of the staff, he’d been through it all himself and was
therefore an expert.
Dear Bulletin, Harry Monck ”
whom I take to be an ex-’birder desirous of show-
ing that his craft isn’t quite as black as painted—
is unfortunate in having his denial of child-slaving
printed in the same issue which records that a
recruiting-vessel the other day reached Q. with 14
boys aboard —and not one over 13 years of age.
In any case, his objection to A. B. Kellie’s yarn
is beside the question. Mr. Kellie fixed no date—
his incidents might have happened 20 years
ago or at any intervening time; and no one
who knows much about the Q. slave-trade before
the new regulations would put child-outrage past
the old-time brutal blackbirder. Further, it is
merely said the child was outraged on the vessel—*
nothing is said about sailors. The responsible
person might have been ttie Govt, agent, or a
missionary passenger. Further, “child” is as
vague a term as “ boy ”; and a kanaka 12-year-
old, for example, is ripe enough for “outrage”
without it being necessary to defend the sailor-
man against any particular aspersion. In a word,
“ Harry Monck ” has jumped at a conclusion —
and missed the right one.— An Apmirer of the
Yarn.

E. W. O’Sullivan, M.P., Keystone of the !
N.S.W. Democratic Arch, is noweditorof Sydney
Freeman’s Journal (faix), and the result is a
serial story described as follows in the heading ;
UNDER THE SOUTHERN CROSS.
A, Romance, Introducing Stirring Scenes and Episodes
in Australasian History. ' „
By E. W. O’Sullivan, M.P. i
[This story is written with five objects in view—to
assist in the creation of an Australian sentiment, to
preach the confederation of the Anglo-Ctltic race, to re-
move the strange misconceptions about Australasia which
exist in various circles in Great Britain, to familiarise the
elder branches of our race with the sidelights of our his-
tory, and show that Australasia is a country worthy of
more attention than she has hitherto received from the
people of Great Britain and Ireland, Europe, and the
United States.]
It appears to consistof long conversations between
an-English O’Sullivan, an Irish O'Sullivan, and
an Australian O’Sullivan, who tell long stories
and put leading questions to each other to evoke
further long stories. There are also several female
O’Sullivans in it.

“I Was Weak, Pale, & Sickly-
Looking.”
ANAEMIA MARVELLOUSLY VAN-
QUISHED BY DR. WILLIAMS’
PINK PILLS.
Mrs. Lardelli, a fine, handsome woman of about
35 years of age, who resides at 162 Do wl in "-street,
Sydney, recently said to a reporter of the Balmain
Observer, who called upon her to get particulars
of an illness through which she had just passed :
“I am quite well now, and don’t think I ever
enjoyed better health—thanks to Dr. Williams’
Pink Pills for Pale People ! but I was very bad
indeed. The first start of my illness was loss of
appetite, and as I also began to feel a bit low in
spirits occasionally, and also was growing rather
pale, I went to a chemist. ‘ Why,’ he said, ‘you’re
anaemic,’ and he showed me in the looking-glass
NS
Offered Jfciiyiyij hcfj.
how white my
gums were get-
ting ; also, the
bad color of the
inside of my eye-
lide. He said,
‘You want a
strong tonic,’
and he gave me
one. However,
I continued to
get paler and
more sickly-look-
ing, my appetite
grew gradually
less and less, till
I felt that I
hardly cared
whether I ate
anything or not,
and when I did
manage to get a
little food down s
it seemed to stick like a hard lump on my chest.
I was losing flesh rapidly, and was growing so
weak that I could hardly get out of bed in the
mornings, and when I did do a little work it
knocked me up completely, and I had to go and
lie down on the bed to recover. At last every-
thing became a trouble to me, and I got into a
low, hopeless sort of state, and hardly cared what
became of me. Nearly all the time I had
suffered from a peculiar dull pain in my
back, so I made an effort and went to an-
other chemist and asked him what this pain
meant. He told me my liver was out of order,
and he gave me some pills and medicine; but
these did me no good at all. Altogether, I sup-
pose I must have tried a dozen different kinds of
medicine without any good effect. You have no
idea what a wretched state I was in by this time.
I used to lie down all day on the sofa, hardly able
to move anything but my eyes. However, I could
read, and it was because of an account of a re-
markable cure that I saw in the Daily Tele-
graph that I decided to try Dr. Williams’ Pink
Pills for Pale People. I sent my daughter for a
box. In less than a week’s time there was a
great difference, I can assure you. My friends
congratulated me on a change for the better in
my appearance. I continued the pills, and very
soon I was able to do a little work again. My
appetite came back, and a slight tinge of color
appeared in my cheeks. I got slowly, but surely,
better every day, and in about two month’s time
was completely cured, and felt as brisk and lively
as a girl of 18. As I have said before, Dr. Wil-
liams’ Pink Pills for Pale People did this for me
when lots of other medicines failed to do me any
good at all, and I shall always recommend them
to my friends. I give my full permission to the
Dr. Williams’ Medicine Company to publish the
facts of my cure wherever and whenever they may
think fit.
“E. LARDELLI.”
Hopelessly Depressed, Thin and
Tired.
DR.
WILLIAMS' PINK PILLS BRING
BRIGHTNESS AND VIGOR.
Mrs. Johns is a pretty and vivacious young lady
of about 25 years of age, and in answer to the
questions put to her she said : —“ I have been very
ill, but at present I am enjoying excellent health
and spirits. Indeed I don’t know that I ever felt
better in my life. I can safely say that I owe my
satisfactory recovery entirely to Dr. Williams’
Pink Pills. The first cause of my illness was, I
think, the worry and extra work caused by moving
from place to place, and I have been a great
traveller during the last year or two. Anyhow,
after my last journey I began to feel very low and
depressed in spirits ; I could eat scarcely any-
thing, and began to get very thin. And as far
as my color—well, I was as yellow as if I had a
severe fit of the jaundice. An old friend of mine
who met me in the street, looked me straight in
the face and passed me by without recognition,
and she was positively shocked when I ran after
her and told her who I really was. I got from
bad to worse ; the least exertion wearied me, and
my ordinary housework became too much for me.
My gums grew quite white, and my lips looked a
blueish-grey color. My sleep was restless and dis-
turbed, and I was so tired in the mornings that I
could hardly get out of bed, and, when I did, I
used to lay on the sofa, feeling I hardly cared what
happened to me. I saw a doctor, who said I
needed a strong tonic, and I had several bottles of
physio from him, besides other bottles of tonics
which I obtained from chemists ; but I grew no better. I was in this miserable and hopeless con-
dition when I heard from someone who had been cured by them about Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills,
and I at once got some. After taking a few I was agreeably surprised to find a real and distinct
change for the better. I got rid of that miserable depression of spirits, which in itself was enough to
keep me ill, and, in a few days, I was able to sleep the night through without interruption. The Pills,
too, gave me a good appetite, and T began slowly to get cheerful and fat again. My color came
back, and, after taking six boxes, I was completely cured, and found myself as strong as ever. I shall
always remember my cure with gratitude, and shall, you may be sure, recommend this wonderful
medicine to any of my friends who may have the bad fortune to suffer as I did.”
Mrs. Johns resides at 60 Yurong-street, Sydney,
“MY DAUGHTER WAS FADING AWAY.”
Mrs. Hutchison, of St. George’s-road, North Fitzroy, Melbourne, said of her daughter May, aged
18 : ‘ After her 15th birthday my daughter became very delicate, and suffered from poorness of blood
(anaemia). It turned to water at periodical times, so said a prominent ladies’ doctor under whose
treatment she was for seven months, but I’m sorry to add without any benefit accruing ; indeed, she
became worse day by day until I began to fear for her life. A young chemist suggested her trying
Dr. Williams’Pink Pills for Pale People. She took his advice, with the result that she rapidly re-
covered her previous good health. She only took a few boxes, and is perfectly cured, and says she can
recommend them strongly to her girl acquaintances ; in fact, she has done so, and a young lady at
Clifton Hill, who was a great sufferer, is now taking them with much success. You have our full
permission to make known the particulars given you, and I only hope that no mother will experience
the pain I felt when I thought I saw my daughter fading away.”

listless every day.
The doctor said she
was suffering from
anaemia, or poorness
of blood. We then
became seriously alarmed, and consulted different
medical men, who prescribed, and told Miss May
to take as much exercise as possible. She, being
fond of flowers, naturally turned her attention to
the garden, but had to abandon the work, as the
least exertion exhausted her. Often, whenlthought
she was out taking exercise in the fresh air, I have
found her in the drawing-room with her head
resting in her hands, and in a somnolent state.
Then, when disturbed, she would steal quietly to
her room, and remain there for hours at a time.
At this stage we became extremely anxious con-
cerning her health, and again sought medical
advice, as we thought she was going into a de-
cline. It was of no use though ; nothing did her
any good. She was then so thin and weak that
the slightest exertion tired her. She also suffered
from severe cramps in the. legs.
“Friends then recommended patent medicines,
and two or three kinds were tried, unsuccessfully.
“At last I decided to take her away to the
country for a change, but just as I was about to
do so I saw in the Tarrangower Times an ad-
vertisement about Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills for
Pale People, and noticing that people with
symptoms similar to those of my daughter had
been cured, I determined she should give them a
trial.
“After using half a box a great improvement
was noticeable, and after using three boxes she
regained her appetite, was able to sleep with
comfort, and was so strong and well that we
considered there was no necessity for her con-
tinuing to take Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills. So
she discontinued them. I honestly believe my
daughter’s recovery was due to this great remedy.
She is now as well as ever she was in her life.
She has a fine color; is able to eat and sleep well,
and can take any amount of out-door exercise
without feeling fatigued. I feel very grateful to
Dr. Williams for my daughter’s recovery, and
would like to have the case made known, as it
may induce others who suffer as my daughter did
to try this remedy to their benefit, and thus
Eerhaps bring sunshine and happiness to their
omes.”
Great satisfaction has been expressed by Miss
Bowe’s large circle of friends that she is again
enjoying health, and able to take her accustomed
place at local social gatherings.

The Bay at Barranjoey.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
It was in a Sydney waterside hotel that
the dilapidated, sun-cracked, battered wreck
lifted up his voice and babbled of the good old
times.
By dint of hard work —honest and otherwise —
he had become sole owner of a little coasting craft,
big enough to smuggle with and small enough to
be handled by two men. Then he arranged with
a Hawkesbury syndicate to transport certain
liquors to Sydney without paying duty. Accord-
ing to his manifest his cargo consisted of oysters,
shingles, or similar trade.
* * * *
“Yes, the business paid all right. The
materials cost at the rate of 3s. 6d. per gallon,
and when made up sold for 145., and as fast as it
was got ready it was got rid of. But the business,
like most which pay well, had its risks ; if caught
it meant that I would lose my boat and my friends
would lose my society for months. Landing the
rum in Sydney was easy enough; at night I’d
pull over from Neutral Bay to a wharf near
Erskine-street; putting a cask in a bag I’d carry
it up to a room at the back of one of the hotels,
where a light was left burning for me. I’d leave
the cask on the table, and when I returned with
another cask the first one had disappeared, but
the money for it was on the table. The trouble
lay in getting the yeast up to the Hawkesbury.
This I at last managed by having a false-bottom
built into a cask which was nicely painted and
placed on the cabin table. When the revenue-
stationmaster came aboard, if he said he’d like a
glass of grog, it was drawn off for him, under his
very nose, from this innocent-looking cask.
“But I came near being caught once. I had
my usual cargo of 80 gallons of rum aboard, and
was making for Sydney from the Hawkesbury,
but, as a strong easterly was blowing, I dropped
anchor inside the bay at Barranjoey. I had
turned in for the night when I heard steps on
deck : it was one of the men from the station.
“Hullo, George, what brings you here to-
night?”
“ Any rum aboard ? ”
“Yes, 80 gallons.”
“ Well, you’d better get rid of it before morn-
ing ; we’re coming aboard first thing—you’ve been
informed on.”
This was important news to a man following my
profession. I felt George was incapable of joking,
so I made up my mind to go back to the Hawkes-
bury that night and unload. I called up the
hand : he wanted to know whether the easterly
had dropped. I told him that it hadn’t, but that
we were going to make a move for all that. We
returned to the river; unloaded the rum ; dug a
trench, in which we buried the casks, and then
got back to the Bay again to our previous anchor -
a g e , just about an hour before daylight.
About ten o’clock in the morning I saw the
station boat leave the station wharf; it passed all
the other boats, and made straight for us. So
George had not been joking.
“Good morning, Jones.”
“ Good morning, Capt. Morrison.”
“ What have you got aboard ?”
“Oysters, for Sydney.”
“ Anything else ? ”
“No. What else do you think I carry ? ”
“You’d better get the hatch open.”
When I had got the tarpaulin off the hatch,
Capt. Morrison said he would have to get the
oysters on deck. I told him he could do as he
liked, but that he would not find anything. He
then turned to George—my friend of the night
before—and told him to get the shovels out of the
boat.
“ One moment, Captain Morrison,” I said ; “ I
can’t allow a spade to be used on those oysters —
they are live fish, and if you dig a spade into them
you will kill them, and they will be no good to
me. If you want the oysters taken out of the
hatch your men will have to pick them out with
their hands, or you must take the consequences.”
So the men started putting the oysters on deck
with their hands ; but it was fearfully slow work,
and in handling the shells the men cut their hands
and began growling. Capt. Morrison stood it for
about a quarter of an hour, and then told them to
use their shovels. They got the oysters on deck,
but, of course, found nothing.
“ Are you satisfied, Captain ?”
“ Yes, it must have been a hoax.”
“ Where shall I put the oysters for you ?
You’ve got enough to keep you going for a day or
two.”
As I got no answer I ran alongside the Govt,
wharf and put them on the beach. I went
back again to the Hawkesbury, and a great dis-
cussion took place; they had another 80gals.
ready for me; so that, with the 80gals. buried,
there were 160 gals. consigned to Sydney. After
much consultation I resolved to chance it and
then retire from the business. The whole lfiOgals.
were put aboard. When I got down to the coast
there was, curiously enough, a strong easterly
blowing, which made it impossible to make for
Sydney. I couldn’t go back to the Hawkesbury
again, so I ran into Barranjoey, and this time
dropped anchor almost alongside the Government
wharf. Capt. Morrison passed in a boat. I
hailed him and asked him whether he was coming
aboard. He replied, “No.” I said, “You had
better. I’ve got plenty of rum aboard now.” He
told me to go to a place which will, I suppose, in
due course receive me.
I got up to Sydney, landed my cargo, and
called at the Customs on Mr. Brown, who was in
some way very friendly with Capt. Morrison. I
stated my case to him, and said that the Govt,
had no right to destroy my property. He admitted
this in the abstract, and, after thinking over the
matter, said :
“ How much were the oysters worth a bushel ? ”
“ Ten shillings.”
“ How many bushels had you ? ”
“120.”
“Well, if you .call to-morrow, I will let you
have an answer.”
When I did call Mr. Black handed me a cheque
for <£6o, and told me to hold my tongue.
I did.
HENRY BENN.
Slowly but surely the communal idea is
being thrown over at the S.A. village-settlements.
At first settlers used to assemble every morning
and heatedly discuss whether Jones should dig
and Brown plant, or whether it should be the
other way round. When the matter was decided
it was time for dinner; and occasionally there
wasn’t anything to eat. Now they’re, in some
cases—the most successful of them —employing a
salaried boss. The community decides what shall
be done (helped thereto by the Govt, inspector),
and the boss allots the work. But it’s still hard
battling : the drought and the low river (most of
the settlements are on the Murray) have been a
terrible handicap.
Business, Bobbery, etc .
In connection with the combination of Vic.
farmers who are shipping their wheat to England in order
to maintain a reasonable price being paid by the local
millers, it is instructive to note that two of the most
active agitators in favor of the movement are intimately
connected with one of the biggest speculators and holders
of wheat in the province. He could almost flood the
market at 3d. less than the Vic. farmers’ combination is
likely to realise at Home. Heads the speculator wins,
tails the farmers’ combination loses, is just what it means
—in the long run.
* • * *
The big fortune made by a leading Southern
wheat-operator dates from the discovery of rust in wheat
in his particular region. He had been a farmer in Eng-
land, so was well acquainted with the cereal disease.
Farming not paying there, he came to Australia and went
into the same line. After a few successful seasons a fair
harvest arrived, but threshing-machine returns staggered
him by their extensive smallness. His overseer could
give no explanation of the cause, but remarked
that the men and their clothes were as red as bricks. The
reason flashed through his brain at once, and he lost no
time in sending out buyers to purchase at any price all
the old wheat they came across. Everyone thought him
mad, but that charge was removed when the full state of
affairs was discovered. In subsequent years he sold his
business to a limited co., whose last dreadful balance-sheet
recalls the above incident.
» * * *
A big alleged embezzler, whose delinquencies
were so carefully and smellfully hushed up a little while
back, was everywhere regarded as a partner in the con-
cern he took down. Even his own wife swallowed the
fiction that he was a member of the firm, and entrusted
him, some years back, with many thousands of her own
to “ put into the business.” Where it was put the lady
wonders to the present day, but the astute hubby invari-
ably pitched a tale of great profits and turned up with fat
dividends every half year.
• • * *
The minnows in the Melb. tea-trade are nearly
all parsons’ sons, the balance being preaching laymen.
Why this is thus no one seems to know, but the fact
remains that they all make a good living in this trickiest
of trades, whereas the godless dealer in the leaf somehow
seldom manages to make enough to pay his way.
* * * •
The spruceness and beaming faces of one or two
of the little men who were in the late big Melb. blaze is
remarkable. Prior to the fire most of them were shabby,
gloomy, and had “assignment” written large in the
wrinkles on their faces and on the fringes of their pants.
The purifieation-by-fire theory looms large in the minds
of those who know anything.
* * » *
Rumored that an amalgamation of certain big
Melb. retail groceries is a certainty. They are at present
cutting one another to pieces, but by amalgamation they
propose to open up scores of small groceries, and, instead
of fighting each other as at present, to “go for’’their
smaller rivals, wipe them out and monopolise the trade.
• * * * .
Although big sums pass through Salvation
Army hands no cases of defalcation are ever heard of. Is
this because no money is ever embezzled, or is it that the
S. A. is too wise to wash its dirty linen in public ?
* * * *
So keen now is life-assurance competition that
as many as three rival offices, represented by buggy,
doctor, and agent, are often found simultaneously in the
same small township. A pointer for would-be insurers
failing to pass Sydney medical inspection : Go West, the
further the better, and, with average luck, in less than a
month some travelling medico will put you through as a
first-class life. The reason sticks out: The doctor is
under obligations to the agent; two or three weeks may
have passed without a proposal, and when one dobs
comes along the agent remembers the doctor “has a
mouth on him ” —circumstances that tend to an optimistic
view of a client’s innards.
* * * *
S. : “ The experience of two generations advises
—beware of Lazarus mines. Mines are continually being
resurrected, and the resurrectionists always tell the same
story : The mine was worked down to a certain level 30
years ago, the lode was 4ft. wide, and the walls soft, and
25dwt. was the poorest crushing. But the water became
too strong, or the party went away to bury its aunt and
forgot to return, or it started on a purple tear and ran
down a steep place into the sea; and there is that magni-
ficent property waiting for a co. to put up machinery on
it and grow disgustingly rich. I have been in some ven-
tures of this kind and have observed many, and the fool,
having learnt wisdom, tips the other jackass. Fight shy
of the Lazarus! Miners 20 or 30 or 40 years ago knew
what they were about, and nothing less convincing than
death could keep them off decent stone once they got on
to it. The popular belief that the men of the Sixties were
slouches at reefing is also a delusion and a snare, as good
miners know after a peep into old workings. They knew
enough to get the gold out, and when the water met them
in a good thing they sat down in that locality till the lifts
were in and never lost grip for 10 seconds, and as a general
thing they had not long to wait. Better play-up your
money at fan-tan than back the Lazarus, which is the
most hopeless gamble available to a Christian man.”
• * *-■ *
“But,” gasped the city-speculator to the tired,
clay-stained man leaning on the windlass, “ we were told
it would go 2oz. to the load ! ” “Well,” said the other,
thoughtfully, “ maybe it will—you see, we ain’t quite
crushed the whole o’ the lode yet.”
• • * •
Melb. City Council lately let a contract for
5000 tons of coal to the Adelaide s.s. co., although the
tender of a local colliery was only l£d. a ton higher. The
Council replies to the consequent outcry: “We are here
to protect the interests of the rate-payers.” Save us
from our protectors! Gippsland coal-supplies have
served to break up a monopoly and reduce the cost of im-
ported stuff nearly 50 per cent in a few years. The Fatted
Council still cries out about the inferiority of Gippsland
coal, despite expert evidence to the contrary, and the
proved superiority of the Jumbunna product at properly-
supervised trials. Vested interest returns many Staunch
champions to the Council; hence the discouragement of
local industries.

Dear Bulletin. — Re sarsaparilla - man Ayer.
Such wholesale medical fakirs hold the druggist at their
mercy, and regulate prices throughout the trade.
The retail - man’s margin of profit on a patent
medicine is insignificant, and his returns must
come from draughts and boluses of his own mix-
ing threepenn’orth of stuff for eighteenpence. I
have never met a chemist who was not completely out of
a patent compound if any chance offered of trading off one
of his own specifics—probably the very thing asked for,
without the label. Astonishing how much people will pay
for a label.—T.D.
• * * *
“ Made in Germany ” has proved a profound
humiliation for the bumptious Briton. Parliamentary re-
ports show that the “ Made in Germany ” brand didn’t
deter the average purchaser at all, and that it proved a
splendid advertisement for the German industries. To
crown all, the British manufacturers, in several cases,
actually descended to the ignoble device of putting afore-
said foreign brand on British goods to ensure their sale.
» * * *

As the sewerage is being connected in Melb. there are
frantic efforts to sell houses for removal. I saw a decent
one marked up to-day for £9! Here is a place in which
the industrious thriftite has stewed, for weary years,
pinching self and family, for the busted Permanent
Evanescent Building Society.. Oh, miserable ant, how the
grasshopper laughs at you ! ”

The Worker’s Worst Foe.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Jhe batman in his mansion fine
Dwells royally at ease ;
He drinks the most expensive winej
He eats the Gouda cheese.
Ihe weeds he smokes are Havanese,
The best that coin can buy,
And through their curling wreaths he sees
Chateaux- not in the sky.
His womankind in diamonds he
Decks splendidly, of course—
-1 hose gems should blood-red rubies be,
To signify their source..
His horses are of noble blood,
All full of pride and fire ;
He owns indeed as fine a stud
As Patman could desire.
Such horses ! Biped trudging by—
Scant dinner in your can —-
Just pause and watch them prancing high,
And ponder, Working-Man !
111-clothed your children are ; your wife,
What clothes should she require ?
The Patman’s womenkind through life
Go clad in silk attire.
The Patman’s daughters and his sons
Right daintily are fed ;
Your wan-faced wife and little ones
Thank God when they have bread.
He lives for many years, and dies
In smell of sanctity ;
Por he is strong, and he is wise,
And hard of grasp is he.
You live as long as you can slave,
And like a dog you die.
“ You’ll go to Heav’n if you behave ! ”
Bah ! Fatman owns the sky.
He runs the creeds of all the world,
And, smiling in his pew,
Hears lurid maledictions hurled
At wickedness—that’s you.
And is he, then, your fellest foe,
This Fatman full of glee,
Who sweats your blood and brain ? Ah, no !
There is a worse than he.
In your own ranks the traitor lurks,
All smug, and slick, and prim ;
Blame not the Patman, or his works,
Por your distress. Blame him.
He bows beneath the Patman’s yoke,
And never feels it gall.
He doesn’t drink, he doesn’t smoke,
He slaves and saves—that’s all.
He sneers at your aspirings grand,
And quietly says he :
“ I have a steady billet, and
That’s good enough for me.”
And if he’s “ sacked ” with notice curt
He doesn’t storm or rage,
But worm-like crawls through kindred dirt
To take the lowest wage.
The Patman is a man of might ;
And open foe is he :
But this fish-blooded parasite
Is your worst enemy.
CREEVE ROE.
A Perverted Punishment.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“I contend that any woman can deceive any
man, if she has an ounce of brains, and get off
scot free, too —no matter how clever the man may
be. Why, Samson was a baby in Delilah’s hands!”
The first woman delivered this speech insinuat-
ingly) with a world of suggestion in her look and
tones.
“ But, Mrs. Norton, there’s nothing to show
that Samson was a giant intellectually. Now,
my husband is a genius. ”
The second woman, who was thirty, and looked
twenty-five, leaned back among her cushions with
the air of a person who has said something pecu-
liarly convincing. Mrs. Norton shrugged her
shoulders, and prepared to depart. “ A man may
have musical talent and yet be an arrant fool, for
all that,” was her Parthian shaft.
“I loathe that woman,” murmured the other,
cordially, to herself, as she heard the outside
door shut. Her husband, entering just then,
caught her words. He was a small, thin man
with brilliant eyes, and long hair pushed back from
his forehead and falling to his collar.
“ Eh ! you loathe her, my dear ? ” he said, en-
quiringly. “Then why the deuce do you see
her? Just the way with you women, though.
Loathe her, eh ? and dose her with tea ! Well,
that s logical, I can’t deny—though once your sex
used somewhat swifter poisons for the friends they
‘loathed.’” *
The woman glanced up, annoyed. “ I wish you
could contrive to exempt me from the fire of your
sarcasm ! ” she said, icily.
The man smiled, and, sitting down beside her,
slipped an arm round her waist. “What is the
matter, dear? Surely you know I was jesting ! ”
His voice was anxious, but his eyes were cold cud
abstracted.
The woman looked into his eyes a second and
seemed to have made up her mind to something.
“Listen!” she said, imperiously. “Do you
know what Mrs. Norton wanted me to do ? ”
“No,” replied the man.
“ She wanted me to deceive you.” She looked
at him as if she expected an outburst.
“ Ah ! ” said the man, with a bewildered air ;
“ deceive me ? But what for—what about ? ”
The woman seemed disappointed. “How and
when do wives usually deceive their husbands ? ”
she asked, meaningly.
The man laughed easily. “ Why, in a hundred
different ways, and every day of their lives, dear! ”
The woman’s face flushed, and her eyes grew
bright and hard. “ You are very sure of me,
George! ”
“ Yes, you are my wife ! ”
“ Yes, I am your wife—but I am a woman, too,
George.” She was silent a moment, then sud-
denly burst into a passion of sobbing, hiding her
face in her hands and throwing herself sideways
among the cushions on the lounge.
The man looked annoyed, perplexed: he glanced
first at his wife, then round the room till his eyes
rested on a grand piano at the further corner, and
the thought to calm his wife’s nerves came to his
musician’s.soul. He crossed the room on tip-toe,
and, opening the instrument, touched the keys
silently and lovingly for a moment with caressing
finger-tips ; then siowly and dreamily the music
woke and whispered through the room.
But the woman started up and gazed at her
husband as if she could not believe her eyes.
She interpreted his action into the cruellest
indifference to her tears. Suddenly she sprang
up and crossed the room. “Stop !” she almost
screamed.
. The musician started and looked up at her, dazed;
his soul was lost in the music ; he had forgotten
her presence completely. “ What is it ?” he
asked, absently.
“You ask me what is it!” she cried in a tempest.
“You don’t want to know. You’d be better
pleased if I left you to your cursed music.” The
man started and cowered —she had always said
she loved music.
“ But I’ll not! —do you hear—l won’t! Do you
think I’m going to put up with your indifference ?
I am not a stick or a stone. lam a woman. I
want—oh, if you are a man, you know what I
want! ”
She stood before him splendid in her florid
beauty, her eyes filled with passion and pride, her
breast heaving, her red lips panting with the sobs
she vvas struggling to restrain. The man stared
stupidly at her. He was utterly unable to cope
with the situation. Women were passionless
beings to him ; angels with soft voices that sug-
gested perfect melodies. His wife’s voice now
was harsh and strident with anger, and it irritated
him. He could not understand that she or any
woman could be. swayed by a passion that de-
manded response in kind. He felt disturbed and
vexed by the whole occurrence, but put it down to
his wife’s “ nerves ” !
“ My dear,” he said, kindly, and quite sincerely,
“ I am afraid you are not well.”
“ I am well enough, thank you,’’said the woman.
“Mrs. Norton was quite right—you are a fool.”
The man was just a little nettled. “Did she
say that ? ”
The woman leaned forward —“Yes, she gave
that as a reason why I should be unfaithful to
you.”
“ Well ? ” he asked, his face paling a little.
“ And I told, her that your indifference was
freezing me, killing my love for you ; that I was
commencing to hate you. I told her you were
wrapped up in your music and utterly neglected
your wife.”
“ Go on ! ” said the man, with a queer, strained
voice.
The woman saw she had made him feel at last,
and her mood urged her further. She told me she
knew a man who worshipped me, and asked me
why I didn’t seek consolation from others.”
“ What reply did you give her ! ”
“ I said you were too clever to be deceived. She
said you were a fool. I was the one in the wrong.”
She tapped the floor with her foot and watched
the man covertly from the corners of her eyes.
The man was roused. He paced about nervously,
with clenched hands. Suddenly he turned and
faced her. “ Having discovered lam a fool, what
do you intend to do ? ”
“ I am not going to put up with your treatment
of me any more ! ”
“ Which means ? ”
“ Anything you like —I shall take my revenge.”
“ Good ! ” said the man quietly ; “ that leaves
me free to return to the mistress I should never
have deserted.”
“ Your wretched music!” sneered the woman
as she swept from the room.
* * • *
The man locked the door after his wife had left,
and sat down to think. All his life was disturbed,
disarranged. Hours passed, and still he remained
gazing into vacancy. By-and-bye the dark came,
and servants tapped at the door, but he did not
answer them. For hours he sat brooding over he
knew not what. When midnight chimed he roused
himself and went to the piano. Then, while his
cheeks were still wet with tears of self-pity, the
musical inspiration of his life came to him.
Days passed, and the room became littered with
scores. The man seldom left the piano for an
hour, and his meals were taken irregularly and at
long intervals. He grew thinner and even paler;
but his eyes shone brilliantly, and he was filled
with the ecstasy of creation.
Meanwhile, his wife attributed his pre-occupa-
tion and seclusion to grief at the loss of her. As
the days passed she grew sad and lonely, and
commenced to relent. One evening at twilight
she slipped in with the servant as her husband un-
locked the door. “George!” she whispered, her
eyes full of love and entreaty. But the musician
looked at her as if he could not understand—he
thought she had returned to him from her lover—
from the dead.
“ George—dear George ! ” said the woman
tremulously. “I have come to tell you that I
love you still, dear, and I forgive you. I want
you to forgive me, too, dear, for making you
suffer.” She moved towards him, but he waved
her off.
“ You have come back ! ” was all he could say.
The woman commenced to protest. “I have
never been away since I left you. I have never
been out of the house or seen anyone. I’ll cut
Mrs. Norton if you like, dear. All I said the
other day was just to punish you, George. You
know you neglected me, George, and I wanted to
punish you, but I didn’t think you’d suffer so
much, dear. I thought you cared more for your
stupid old music than you did for me ! ”
The man shivered. “ Stupid old music!” he
repeated, but the woman thought he agreed with
her.
“ Yes, dear,'’ she said. “ I can see now how
foolish I have been, and I want you to forgive me
for being so cruel to you.” She went close to
he shivered again as she touched him, but per-
mitted her to kiss him, and even returned her
caresses.
Presently she glanced round at the untidy room,
with its litter of papers. “What are all these
papers—music scores ? How tremendously you
must have been writing, George ! ” She picked
up a loose sheet nearest her ; by chance it was
numbered “ One,” and across the top was scrawled
an Italian phrase. The woman deciphered it with
difficulty, then looked up and met her husband’s
eyes. “Is this the name of your opera?” she
asked, pointing to the writing on the score.
“Yes,”he answered, miserably.
“ I am not a linguist—will you tell me what it
means ? ” she asked, with a terrible calmness.
The man stammered evasively, “It is just a
name.”
“ Yes ; but what does it mean ? ”
“ The Unfaithful Wife,” he answered desper-
ately.
